# Tufte / charting — FULL research corpus (recovered from transcripts)

> **What this is.** The complete per-agent deep-research behind the `charting` skill, extracted
> **verbatim** from the `StructuredOutput` of every research agent across the two workflows
> (`wf_bc6191f6` = 107, `wf_10a0e5d0` = 105). This is the RAW corpus that the 9-finding synthesis
> in [charting-research.md](charting-research.md) was distilled from, and the distilled principles
> in [evidence-tufte-principles.md](evidence-tufte-principles.md) after that. **De-noised by
> construction** — only StructuredOutput research content is here; no prompts, tool logs, rate-limit,
> retry, or token-accounting text. Extracted deterministically (jq/Python), not re-summarized, so
> nothing is paraphrased away.

> **Scale:** 47 source extractions · 150 adversarial verifications · 15 synthesis records, from 212 agents.

---

## Part A — Source extractions (47)

Each research agent read one source and extracted claims with a verbatim supporting quote and an
importance rating. Grouped by run.

### Run A — canonical (107 agents)

#### Source `a0136bc5` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020 (CHI 2020; arXiv preprint 1907.02035, submitted 2019-07-03, revised 2020-01-08)
- **[central]** Y-axis truncation reliably increases readers' perceived effect size/severity of differences, and this bias is statistically robust (Experiment 1, n=40).
  > "Increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)."
- **[central]** Adding explicit visual cues that signal truncation (broken-axis marks, gradient fade at the bottom) does NOT meaningfully reduce the exaggeration effect — truncated charts with break indicators were perceived about the same as plain truncated charts. This contradicts the popular belief that a break mark makes a truncated axis 'honest.'
  > "There was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05)."
- **[supporting]** The truncation-induced exaggeration persists even when readers are forced to read and estimate the actual numeric values, showing it is a perceptual magnification effect rather than mere inattention to the axis (Experiment 3, n=25).
  > "perceived severity was significantly different across levels of truncation (F(1,20)=11, p=0.003)"
- **[central]** The authors explicitly reject a universal 'all charts (or bars) must start at zero' rule; whether truncation is honest or deceptive depends on the effect sizes and task, not a fixed convention.
  > "We resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that...all charts with quantitative axes should include 0."
- **[supporting]** Truncation affected perceived effect size similarly for bar charts and line charts, with no significant difference between the two chart types — undercutting the common convention that line charts may be truncated freely while bars may not.
  > "There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50)."

#### Source `a08f5b84` — quality: **blog** · published: 2020-08-28
- **[central]** Tufte's data-ink ratio principle holds that good graphics maximize data-ink and erase as much non-data-ink as possible; the blog states this as a normative rule without any supporting empirical study.
  > "Good graphical representations maximize data-ink and erase as much non-data-ink as possible."
- **[supporting]** Tufte's 'Lie Factor' measures graphical distortion as the ratio of graphical change to numerical change; the article's worked example reports a Lie Factor of 14.8 where a 53% numerical change is drawn as a 783% graphical change.
  > "Lie Factor of this graph is 14.8. A numerical change of 53% is represented by a graphical change of 783%."
- **[supporting]** Graphical integrity requires that the physical size of marks on the graph be directly proportional to the numerical quantities they represent.
  > "The representation of numbers, as physically measured on the surface of the graph itself, should be directly proportional to the numerical quantities represented"
- **[supporting]** Tufte defines sparklines as data-intense, design-simple, word-sized graphics, and the article repeats this definition without citing evidence on their effectiveness.
  > "Data-intense, design-simple, word-sized graphics"
- **[supporting]** Tufte defines chartjunk as the excessive and unnecessary use of graphical effects, specifically naming moire vibration, heavy grids, and self-promoting graphs; the article presents this as Tufte's taste without testing whether removing such elements aids comprehension.
  > "The excessive and unnecessary use of graphical effects in graphs"

#### Source `a14e0e29` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014-12
- **[central]** Tufte's canonical definition of sparklines places small, word-sized data graphics directly before or after words inline in running text; this IEEE TVCG paper treats those as a special case of the broader 'word-scale visualizations' it studies.
  > "Word-scale visualizations are a more general version of sparklines--small, word-sized data graphics that allow meta-information to be visually presented in-line with document text. In accordance with Edward Tufte's definition, sparklines are traditionally placed directly before or after words in the text."
- **[supporting]** The paper's empirical contribution on sparklines is a quantitative analysis of how graphic placement affects readability and text disruption — i.e., it studies placement/layout effects, not whether sparklines improve comprehension or recall versus alternative chart forms.
  > "We also contribute a quantitative analysis that highlights the effect of different placements on readability and text disruption."
- **[supporting]** Placements beyond Tufte's traditional before/after position (between text lines, in added vertical/horizontal space, or as interactive overlays) are proposed as enabling a wider range of word-scale graphics and more flexible integration with text layout.
  > "We describe alternative placements that permit a wider range of word-scale graphics and more flexible integration with text layouts. These alternative placements include positioning visualizations between lines, within additional vertical and horizontal space in the document, and as interactive overlays on top of the text."
- **[tangential]** The authors identify six variables that govern where a word-scale graphic can be placed and how much it disrupts the source text, and derive design guidelines from these.
  > "we... identify six important variables that control the placement of the graphics and the level of disruption of the source text. Finally, we use this analysis to propose guidelines to support the design and placement of word-scale visualizations."

#### Source `a1bb0c14` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-04 (CHI '15; NELLCO working paper dated 2015-02)
- **[central]** In a controlled crowdsourced experiment (N=250 for the exaggeration study), a truncated/non-zero y-axis on a bar chart significantly inflated viewers' magnitude judgments: deceptive bar charts drew a mean 'how much bigger' rating of 2.77 (95% CI [2.26,3.28]) vs 1.45 ([1.27,1.62]) for the zero-baseline control, p=0.0003. This is direct empirical support for the convention that bar charts should start at zero.
  > "The treatments that involved bar-charts (affected by the "truncated axis" distortion), the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37."
- **[central]** Encoding a quantity by a circle's radius instead of its area (so the value scales with area roughly quadratically) significantly exaggerated perceived magnitude: deceptive bubble charts drew a mean rating of 2.71 ([2.27,3.15]) vs 1.71 ([1.45,1.98]) for the area-faithful control, p=0.0007, r=0.34. This empirically confirms Tufte's area/graphical-integrity principle.
  > "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."
- **[central]** Across all three magnitude-distortion techniques (truncated axis, area-as-quantity, aspect ratio), deceptive charts inflated viewers' comparative magnitude responses by 58.5% to 129.5% relative to the undistorted control, with all three differences significant at p<0.001 — quantifying that these classic 'graphical integrity' violations produce large, not marginal, message-level deception.
  > "When asked to compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5, the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition."
- **[supporting]** Of the three exaggeration techniques, aspect-ratio manipulation on a line chart produced the largest deceptive effect (line > bar > bubble): deceptive line charts drew a mean rating of 3.19 vs 1.39 control, with the strongest effect size (r=0.66, p<0.0001), indicating that altering a time-series line chart's aspect ratio distorts perceived rate of change more than axis truncation or area mis-encoding.
  > "For the "aspect ratio" distortion type, the mean ranks of Line (Control) and Line (Deceptive) were 24.42 and 55.04, respectively; U = 1409, Z = 5.88, p <0.0001, r = 0.66."
- **[supporting]** An inverted y-axis reversed the message entirely rather than merely exaggerating it: 30 of 38 viewers (78.9%) of the deceptive line-area chart read a rising trend as 'declined', versus only 1 of 40 (2.5%) in the control, p<0.0001 — showing axis-direction reversal causes qualitative misreading, a more severe failure than magnitude distortion.
  > "Out of the 38 selected participants who saw the deceptive visualization, 30 responded incorrectly, 7 correctly and one chose the uncertain response. For the 40 participants who saw the control condition, 39 responded correctly, 1 responded incorrectly and no participant reported uncertainty."

#### Source `a28bdbc2` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2011 (April–June; Visual Business Intelligence Newsletter, Perceptual Edge)
- **[central]** Few's reading of Bateman et al. (2010) 'Useful Junk?' is that embellished (chartjunk) charts produced comprehension EQUAL to plain charts (not better), and the only measured benefit was better LONG-TERM recall two-to-three weeks later, with no advantage soon after viewing. This contradicts the popular reading that the study proved chartjunk improves comprehension.
  > "When a simple message...is presented in a chart that includes embellishments relevant to that message...then a reader will comprehend the message as well as he would had the graph lacked embellishment and will also better recall the basic subject of the chart and pattern in the data two or three weeks later, but no better soon afterwards."
- **[central]** Even the original Bateman et al. authors, as quoted by Few, reported only that accuracy on embellished charts was 'no worse than' plain charts (i.e., no comprehension gain) and merely 'question[ed]' minimalism's premises rather than refuting data-ink/minimalist principles.
  > "We found that people's accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain... our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach to chart design."
- **[supporting]** Few argues the Bateman study is undermined by methodological shortcomings, specifically small and unrepresentative test groups and uncontrolled experimental factors, so it cannot support broad claims that chartjunk aids comprehension.
  > "Methodological Shortcomings ... Small, Unrepresentative Test Groups ... Unconsidered and Uncontrolled Factors"
- **[supporting]** The recall benefit found in the study was conditional: the embellishment had to be RELEVANT to the chart's single-sentence message, not arbitrary decoration. The result therefore does not license generic chartjunk or ornamentation unrelated to the data's point.
  > "a chart that includes embellishments relevant to that message, along with quantitative values that are related to but incidental to the message"
- **[tangential]** Few endorses Tufte's original position that decoration cannot substitute for substantive content (decoration does not rescue weak data) — an aesthetic/taste claim, not a controlled-study finding, illustrating the evidence-vs-convention split in the research question.
  > "Chartjunk does not achieve the goals of its propagators... Chartjunk can turn bores into disasters, but it can never rescue a thin data set."

#### Source `a4673c00` — quality: **primary** · published: 1991-03 (Information Systems Research, vol. 2, no. 1, pp. 63-84)
- **[central]** Whether tables or graphs produce better performance is task-dependent, not a blanket property of either format: graphs emphasize spatial information and better support spatial tasks (perceiving relationships/trends), while tables emphasize symbolic information and better support symbolic tasks (extracting discrete values). Performance is best when the representation matches the task ('cognitive fit'). This directly qualifies Tufte's 'tables often win for small datasets' claim — the evidence says the winner depends on the task type, not on dataset size.
  > "graphs emphasizing spatial information and tables emphasizing symbolic information... Performance on a task will be better when there is a 'cognitive fit' between the information emphasized in the representation and the information required by the task—graphs support spatial tasks and tables support symbolic tasks."
- **[central]** A controlled laboratory experiment (128 MBA students) found strong empirical support for cognitive fit: matching the problem representation to the task produced the largest performance gains, providing experimental evidence that representation choice should follow task characteristics.
  > "The empirical study revealed strong evidence supporting the relationship between performance and cognitive fit... matching problem representation to task produced the largest performance gains."
- **[supporting]** Users in the study preferred tables over graphs for symbolic (value-extraction) tasks, so stated preference aligned with the fit-based performance advantage — supporting the position that tables can be the better choice for look-up/exact-value tasks rather than graphics being universally preferable.
  > "users preferred tables over graphs for symbolic tasks"
- **[tangential]** The authors prescribe that display designers prioritize task characteristics and the appropriate problem representation over accommodating individual user preferences, and suggest cognitive fit may underpin a broader problem-solving theory.
  > "designers should prioritize task characteristics and appropriate problem representations over accommodating individual preferences, suggesting cognitive fit may underpin a broader problem-solving theory."

#### Source `a4c6c7b2` — quality: **secondary** · published: Unknown — PDF creationDate is an epoch placeholder (D:19700101021407Z); producer "Mac OS X 10.2.8 Quartz PDFContext" implies creation c. 2003-2004; surfaced via WikiLeaks Global Intelligence Files (Stratfor), published 2012
- **[central]** The source presents data-ink maximization and chartjunk avoidance as prescriptive rules, defining the data-ink ratio formulaically (data-ink / total ink) and listing pruning steps (erase non-data ink, replace bars with single lines, drop axis lines, drop boxes/legends). It cites NO controlled study — it is normative doctrine, not evidence. This anchors the exact Tufte claim that studies like Bateman et al. 2010 later tested.
  > "Tufte defines the data ink ratio as: Data Ink Ratio = (data-ink)/(total ink in the plot) ... Prune graphics by: replacing bars with single lines, erasing non-data ink; eliminating lines from axes"
- **[central]** The source treats not beginning the vertical axis at zero, and encoding magnitude by area or volume instead of linear scale, as deceptions to 'watch out for' — i.e., Tufte's normative position is that magnitude should be linearly encoded and (implicitly) bars should start at zero. Presented as integrity rules with no empirical or standards citation.
  > "Use area or volume representations instead of linear scales to exaggerate differences. ... Exaggerate the vertical scale, or don’t begin at a ZERO point."
- **[central]** The source prescribes direct labeling over legends as a rule for integrating text and graphics, asserting legends should be avoided and data annotated directly on the graph. Stated as taste/convention with no controlled-study support cited in the document.
  > "Closely interweave text and graphics: attach names directly to parts, place small messages next to the data, avoid legends if possible and annotate the data directly on the graph"
- **[supporting]** The source asserts denser displays are inherently better, defining data density as entries-per-area and stating 'High density is good' because the eye/brain can filter and select. No legibility ceiling or threshold is acknowledged; presented as aesthetic principle, not empirically bounded finding.
  > "Data density = (no. of entries in data matrix)/(area of graphic) ... High density is good: the human eye/brain can select, filter, edit, group, structure, highlight, focus, blend"
- **[supporting]** The source states tables can replace graphs for simple data and that non-comparative datasets belong in tables rather than charts — documenting Tufte's tables-often-win claim. Offered as a guideline with no supporting study cited.
  > "Often text tables can replace graphs for simple data ... Non-comparative data sets usually belong in tables, not charts"

#### Source `a4d9c842` — quality: **secondary** · published: 1994-11-28
- **[central]** The source is an explicit second-hand restatement of Tufte's principles, not original research: it presents the data-ink-ratio rule (maximize data-ink, erase non-data-ink, erase redundant data-ink) as normative design doctrine with no empirical study or controlled test cited anywhere in the document. This makes it useful only as a canonical statement of Tufte's convention, not as evidence for it.
  > "We see that "for non-data-ink, less is more. For data-ink, less is a bore." This suggests five principles of data graphics: "Above all else show the data. Maximize the data-ink ratio. Erase non-data-ink. Erase redundant data-ink."
- **[central]** The document states Tufte's graphical-integrity rule that on-surface magnitude must be proportional to the data, and formalizes distortion via the Lie Factor = displayed-effect-size / actual-effect-size. This is presented as a definitional/normative rule, not an empirically validated threshold.
  > "Lie-factor = size-of-effect-shown-in-visualization / size-of-effect-in-data."
- **[central]** The source asserts, as Tufte's claim, that small multiples (a single design repeated across values of the independent variable within the eyespan) are the best design solution for a wide range of comparison problems. This is stated as design conviction, with the only accompanying 'evidence' being illustrative example figures, not a controlled comparison task.
  > "for a wide range of problems in data presentation, small multiples are the best design solution."
- **[supporting]** The source advances Tufte's high-density doctrine that the remedy for an overcrowded display is redesign, never data removal, and that data-thin displays are actively harmful — implying no acknowledged legibility ceiling. This is asserted normatively with no empirical density/comprehension study.
  > "But "clutter and confusion are failures of design, not attributes of information." If a visualization is too cluttered, don't remove data, change the design."
- **[supporting]** The source endorses direct labeling over legends/codes and recommends tables for small datasets, both stated as Tufte admonitions rather than findings from controlled studies of labeling or table-vs-graphic performance.
  > "Avoid codes, particularly one-time codes and legends. Place legends directly on the visualization."

#### Source `a5278850` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020-09-06 (arXiv preprint; IEEE VIS 2020 short paper)
- **[central]** The paper summarizes the empirical state of the chartjunk debate by asserting that controlled studies have shown extreme minimalism (maximizing Tufte's data-ink ratio) is not always optimal and that visual embellishments can be useful depending on context — directly contradicting a strict reading of Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk prescription.
  > "Previous studies have shown that extreme minimalism is not always best, and that visual embellishments can be useful depending on the context."
- **[supporting]** This is a qualitative study based on semi-structured interviews with 20 practitioners about attitudes and design practice — it is evidence about how practitioners interpret chartjunk, not a controlled comprehension or recall experiment, so it documents opinion/practice rather than measuring whether removing non-data ink improves performance.
  > "We conducted semi-structured interviews with 20 data visualization practitioners, investigating how they understand chartjunk and the factors that influence how and when they make use of embellishments."
- **[supporting]** Among practitioners there is no single agreed definition of 'chartjunk'; understanding is broad and pluralistic and driven by personal and situational factors, which undermines treating 'chartjunk' as an objective, well-bounded category rather than a contested convention.
  > "Our investigation uncovers a broad and pluralistic understanding of chartjunk among practitioners, and foregrounds a variety of personal and situated factors that influence the use of chartjunk beyond context."
- **[tangential]** Practitioners are largely unfamiliar with the academic empirical literature on embellishment/chartjunk and instead follow practitioner and social-media discourse, indicating a gap between Tufte-derived academic prescriptions and what designers actually apply in on-screen/web work.
  > "more likely to be familiar with discussions happening on social media or in practitioner publications than in academic papers."

#### Source `a56eddd2` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010
- **[central]** This is a controlled experiment (IEEE TVCG 2010) that directly tested small multiples against a single shared-space line graph for time-series comparison, finding that split-charts-per-series techniques (small multiples, horizon graphs) are more efficient than shared-space line graphs when comparing series across a LARGE visual span, while shared-space line graphs win for comparisons over a SMALL visual span. This is conditional, task-dependent evidence — not a blanket 'small multiples always win.'
  > "Techniques that create separate charts for each time series—such as small multiples and horizon graphs—are generally more efficient for comparisons across time series with a large visual span. On the other hand, shared-space techniques—like standard line graphs—are typically more efficient for comparisons over smaller visual spans where the impact of overlap and clutter is reduced."
- **[central]** The advantage of small multiples (split-space) over a single overlaid chart depends on task type: split-space techniques were faster for 'dispersed' comparison tasks (discrimination), whereas shared-space line graphs were faster for 'local' tasks (finding the maximum at one point in time).
  > "Split-space techniques (SM and HG) were faster than shared-space techniques for Discrimination ... Shared-space techniques (SG and BG) were faster than splits-space techniques for Maximum"
- **[supporting]** The study is a properly controlled graphical-perception experiment comparing four encodings — simple line graphs, braided graphs, small multiples, and horizon graphs — across three tasks (maximum, slope, discrimination), three series counts (2, 4, 8), and three chart heights (48, 96, 192 px), making it real empirical evidence rather than design opinion.
  > "the study compared four visualization techniques: line charts, braided graphs, small multiples, and horizon graphs ... three tasks ... three numbers of time series (2, 4, 8) and three diagram heights (48, 96, and 192 px)"
- **[supporting]** Increasing the number of concurrent time series degrades performance, which is empirical support for a legibility/data-density ceiling: denser displays of many overlaid series are not monotonically better for comprehension.
  > "Many concurrent time series will cause decreased performance"

#### Source `a790e22d` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018 (IEEE Transactions on Visualization and Computer Graphics / InfoVis)
- **[central]** Gleicher classifies all visual comparison into three design categories — juxtaposition (items placed in separate adjacent spaces, i.e. small multiples), superposition (items overlaid in the same space), and explicit encoding (the relationship itself is drawn) — and states that NEITHER juxtaposition nor superposition naturally scales to many items: overlay becomes cluttered, while small multiples separate items and thereby hinder some kinds of comparison. This contradicts any blanket claim that small multiples are universally superior for comparison.
  > "neither superposition or juxtaposition designs naturally scale to many items. Superposition becomes cluttered with many objects in the same space. Juxtaposition with many items separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison."
- **[central]** The framework is NOT validated by controlled experiments — it is built from informal literature review, thought experiments, and the author's own design experience — and the paper states that experimental comparison among the three design types has been 'elusive.' So the choice between small multiples and overlay is not settled by strong empirical evidence in this source; it is reasoned design guidance.
  > "this paper's framework is based on informal explorations of the literature, thought experiments, and experiences of applying these ideas to design visualization solutions. ... among the three design types, and developing effective combinations, have been elusive."
- **[supporting]** Juxtaposition (small multiples) shifts the work of relating items onto the viewer's memory and visual search, which is why it strains at scale; the paper says giving the separated panels similar/shared layouts reduces that burden — supporting Tufte's small-multiples rule that panels share a common scale and structure.
  > "Juxtaposition places much of the burden of working with relationships on the viewer, which creates issues with scaling. ... For juxtaposition designs, having similar layouts can reduce the burden on the viewer."
- **[supporting]** For time-series comparison specifically, the overlaid multi-line 'spaghetti plot' (superposition) does not scale to many series; the documented remedy is a primarily-juxtaposition design (small multiples) augmented with a shared reference. This gives a concrete condition under which separated panels beat one dense overlaid chart.
  > "the common “spaghetti plot” for comparing time series does not scale to larger numbers of series, so they provided a new design that is primarily juxtaposition, but with superposition elements (including a reference) to aid in comparison."
- **[supporting]** The number of items is a primary axis on which comparison difficulty scales, and many designs that work for two-item comparison fail to extend even to three items — a caution against assuming a layout that compares two things will work for a grid of many.
  > "many of the designs used for two-way comparison do not scale to even three."

#### Source `a8e076ff` — quality: **blog** · published: No explicit publish date; footer copyright reads "© simplexCT 2014-2015", suggesting publication circa 2014-2015.
- **[supporting]** The data-ink ratio originates with Edward Tufte in The Visual Display of Quantitative Information and is defined as data-ink divided by total ink used to print the graphic, operationalizing his maxim 'Above all else show the data.'
  > "The Data-Ink ratio is a concept first introduced by Edward Tufte—in his book the Visual Display of Quantitative Information... Or mathematically by the formula: Data-ink ratio = data-ink / total ink used to print the graphic"
- **[central]** Removing non-data-ink (chartjunk) dramatically improves the legibility of a chart — asserted as fact with no controlled study, experiment, or counter-evidence (e.g., Bateman, Few, Borgo) cited anywhere on the page.
  > "In fact, removing this non-data-ink dramatically improves the legibility of the chart."
- **[supporting]** Non-data-ink can be removed entirely without reducing the amount of data/information the graph communicates.
  > "We can completely remove this ink without reducing the amount of data communicated by the graph."
- **[supporting]** Ink is partitioned into two categories: data-ink (representing the measured quantities, the core of the graph) and non-data-ink (unnecessary elements that do not clarify the intended message) — the latter framed by definition as worthless, presupposing rather than testing the minimalist conclusion.
  > "Tufte splits ink used to display information into two categories: Data-ink and Non-data-ink. Data-ink is that portion of ink dedicated to represent the core of a graph—the measured quantities. Non-data-ink is ink used to draw unnecessary elements of a chart that do not contribute to clarifying the intended message."

#### Source `aa0afa55` — quality: **primary** · published: 2021 (Journal of Applied Research in Memory and Cognition, vol. 10(2), pp. 298-311; online 2020, DOI 10.1016/j.jarmac.2020.10.002)
- **[central]** Across five controlled experiments, y-axis truncation on bar graphs causes viewers to perceive depicted differences as larger (a 'truncation effect'), robust across participants with 83.5% showing the effect — providing empirical support for the long-standing Tufte/expert injunction against truncated bar axes.
  > "In five studies, we provide empirical evidence that y-axis truncation leads viewers to perceive illustrated differences as larger (i.e., a truncation effect). This effect ... was robust across participants, with 83.5% of participants across these 5 studies showing a truncation effect."
- **[central]** The misleading effect persists even after viewers are explicitly taught/warned about y-axis truncation immediately before judging the graphs; the warning reduced but did not eliminate the effect.
  > "The results of Study 2 were surprising in that an explicit warning did not eliminate the truncation effect."
- **[central]** The zero-baseline rule the study empirically validates applies specifically to BAR graphs; for line graphs and dot plots, which do not encode value by length/area, truncation may be appropriate and a zero baseline may not be necessary.
  > "our recommendation to not truncate vertical axes is specific to bar graphs. Line graphs and dot plots, for example, do not represent numerical values as continuous visual areas and truncation may be appropriate in these cases (Cleveland, 1994; Zoss, 2016, p. 42)."
- **[supporting]** Individual differences in graph literacy did NOT predict susceptibility to the truncation effect — higher graph literacy did not protect viewers from being misled by truncated bars.
  > "We also found that individual differences in graph literacy failed to predict the size of individuals' truncation effects."
- **[supporting]** The expert recommendation to start bar axes at zero (that bar length must be proportional to the quantity) is empirically supported by this data, addressing prior demonstrations that used only a single trial per condition.
  > "Data visualization experts have recommended that the y-axes of bar graphs specifically should start at zero ... Our data support this recommendation empirically."

#### Source `aa298ca4` — quality: **primary** · published: 2004-05-27
- **[central]** Tufte defines a sparkline as a small, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution meant to be embedded inline in text, tables, or other graphics rather than stand alone — the canonical definition used by item (2) of the research question.
  > "A sparkline is a small intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution."
- **[supporting]** Tufte claims sparklines achieve extreme data density: a financial table augmented with sparklines shows roughly 14,000 readable values (vs 24 in the numeric table) and adds about 5,000 more numbers using only 21% more space — an effectiveness/density argument, but asserted by Tufte without any cited controlled study.
  > "This financial table reports 24 numbers accurate to 5 significant digits; the accompanying sparklines show about 14,000 numbers readable from 1 to 2 significant digits."
- **[supporting]** Tufte's design rule for time-series sparklines is an aspect ratio producing average hill-slopes of ~45 degrees — presented as a design preference (taste/convention), not backed by empirical evidence on this page.
  > "For judging slopes and velocities up and down the hills in time-series, best is an aspect ratio that yields hill-slopes averaging 45°."
- **[tangential]** Tufte prescribes that sparklines be embedded within running text or tabular/graphic context rather than displayed as isolated charts — bearing on item (8) integration of text and graphics.
  > "Sparklines should be "embedded in a sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet, graphic" rather than standalone."

#### Source `aa7b414c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2017-07-07
- **[central]** In a controlled visual-search experiment, high data density nearly doubled mean response time versus low density (4.7 s -> 9.9 s), a large, statistically significant effect (p<0.001, partial eta-squared=0.82) — direct evidence of a legibility/clutter ceiling where adding more items on a display degrades search performance rather than improving it.
  > "high data density resulted in a significant increase in mean RT, from 4.7 s (SD = 0.72) in low data density to 9.9 s (SD = 1.9) in high data density [F(1,19) = 88.2, p < 0.001, partial eta-squared = 0.82]."
- **[central]** Good display organization (logical grouping of items) significantly moderates the density penalty: high density added 6.0 s in the poorly organized condition but only 4.2 s in the well-organized condition, showing that structure/layering — not item count alone — drives clutter's performance cost.
  > "this effect was more pronounced in the poor-organization condition (an increase of 6.0 s in high data density), as opposed to the good-organization condition (an increase of 4.2 s in high data density)."
- **[supporting]** Both high data density and poor organization significantly raised error (miss) rate, with organization (odds ratio 7.05) an even stronger predictor of misses than data density (odds ratio 5.46) in a binary logistic regression.
  > "data density [odds ratio OR = 5.46, p = 0.013, 95% confidence interval CI = [1.43, 20.71]] and organization [OR = 7.05, p = 0.009, 95% CI = [1.62, 30.53]] were significant predictors of misses."
- **[supporting]** Time-pressure stress did NOT degrade visual-search performance overall; it significantly sped responses (7.8 s no-stress -> 6.7 s stress) — contradicting the intuitive assumption that stress uniformly worsens performance on cluttered displays (though stress did raise errors in the high-density, poorly-organized condition).
  > "Stress, on the other hand, resulted in a significant overall decrease in RT, from 7.8 s (SD = 3.4) in the no stress condition to 6.7 s (SD = 2.3) in the stress condition [F(1,19) = 10.6, p = 0.004]."
- **[supporting]** Treating clutter purely as data/item density is an oversimplification; the same number of items can be more or less cluttered depending on organization, so density and organization are distinct contributors that must be assessed separately.
  > "the view of clutter as display density is also somewhat oversimplified and incomplete. Increasing the number or density of items may increase clutter, but good organization may mitigate the performance effects of density, whereas poor organization exacerbates it."

#### Source `aab7d4a9` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018
- **[central]** In a controlled online experiment on line and bar charts, charts that included redundant data labels (numeric values printed on the chart in addition to axes) were read significantly more accurately and faster than charts without them, and were also perceived as more efficient. This is direct empirical evidence that adding non-minimal, redundant labeling can improve comprehension rather than hinder it.
  > "users perceive charts with redundant labels as significantly more efficient and answer related questions significantly more accurate and faster, which the authors explain by the help of cognitive fit theory"
- **[supporting]** The accuracy/speed benefit of redundant labels is task-dependent: cognitive fit theory predicts redundancy helps only for certain task types, so the gain is conditional rather than universal. This qualifies any blanket rule that more labeling is always better.
  > "cognitive fit theory predicts increased efficiency for some task types that should be solved by means of the visualization"
- **[supporting]** The empirical result runs against the cognitive-load / data-ink-minimization prediction that duplicate (redundant) information should reduce chart efficiency; instead, redundancy increased efficiency for the tested tasks, contradicting a strict 'remove non-essential ink' stance.
  > "While cognitive load theory assumes redundant labels generally reduce visualizations' efficiency due to increased cognitive load, cognitive fit theory predicts increased efficiency for some task types"
- **[supporting]** The authors explicitly recommend that chart designers treat redundant on-chart elements as a deliberate technique to raise reading efficiency under specific conditions, framing redundancy as a design tool rather than clutter to be eliminated.
  > "The researchers encourage graph designers to consider redundant elements as a possible means to increase efficiency under particular circumstances."

#### Source `ab391fd8` — quality: **unreliable** · published: 1991

#### Source `ab9be337` — quality: **primary** · published: 2024-05-11
- **[central]** In a controlled study of grouped bar charts, for tasks that involve comparing or estimating the absolute difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis (not starting at zero) does NOT measurably degrade task performance/accuracy.
  > "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."
- **[central]** For tasks of comparing/estimating relative growth (percentage change) between bars, truncation that preserves the data's underlying relations (monotonic) performs about as well as no truncation, whereas truncation that does not preserve those relations (non-monotonic) is very likely to impair performance.
  > "For comparing or estimating the relative growth between two bars, truncating monotonically has similar performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance."
- **[central]** The blanket 'bar charts must always start at zero' rule is not supported as universal; the authors recommend truncating the y-axis when the goal is to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size on difference tasks, and only avoid truncation (or use monotonic truncation) for percentage-change tasks.
  > "if the designer wants to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size in the viewers, our results corroborate existing recommendations [12,31,44] to truncate the y-axis to show the desired effect size. However, for tasks such as comparing or estimating the percentage change between bars... we suggest that the designer perform no truncation or monotonic truncation for these tasks."
- **[supporting]** Prior empirical work establishes that y-axis truncation produces quantifiable shifts in how viewers subjectively interpret the size and significance of effects, and that broken-axis or other visual interventions do not eliminate this bias.
  > "y-axis truncation results in quantifiable differences in how people subjectively interpret the size and significance of effects, even when behavioral and visual interventions (e.g. broken axes) are employed"
- **[supporting]** This study's empirical findings are scoped to grouped bar charts (two data-generating distributions and four tasks); it does not test line charts, so its conclusions do not directly settle the bar-vs-line truncation distinction.
  > "providing a systematic analysis of y-axis truncation on grouped bar charts."

#### Source `ad1219b6` — quality: **blog** · published: 2025-12-12
- **[central]** The Lie Factor is defined as (size of effect shown in graphic) / (size of effect in data), with 1.0 indicating an honest graphic; the article asserts values between 0.95 and 1.05 are 'acceptable.'
  > "Lie Factor = (Size of effect in graphic) / (Size of effect in data) ... A Lie Factor of 1.0 indicates an honest graphic. Values between 0.95 and 1.05 are acceptable."
- **[central]** Truncating or breaking the y-axis baseline exaggerates small differences, making minor variations appear dramatic, and is a common source of high Lie Factors.
  > "Breaking the y-axis is a common technique to exaggerate small differences. By truncating the baseline, minor variations appear dramatic."
- **[central]** Using area (a two-dimensional encoding) to represent one-dimensional data produces a squared visual effect that misleads viewers about magnitude.
  > "using area to represent one-dimensional data (creates a squared effect)"
- **[supporting]** The article presents a list of six 'principles' under the banner of graphical integrity — Show Data in Comparison, Demonstrate Causality, Show Multivariate Data, Integrate Evidence, Document Everything, Content Above All — which are actually Tufte's principles of analytical design from Beautiful Evidence, not his six graphical-integrity principles.
  > "1. Show Data in Comparison 2. Demonstrate Causality 3. Show Multivariate Data 4. Integrate Evidence 5. Document Everything 6. Content Above All"

#### Source `ae08c665` — quality: **primary** · published: 2007-12-01
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment, participants preferred standard non-minimalist bar graphs over Tufte's high-data-ink-ratio minimalist versions of the same data — directly contradicting the popular claim that maximizing the data-ink ratio is universally better.
  > "People did not like Tufte's minimalist design of bar-graphs; they seem to prefer 'chartjunk' instead."
- **[central]** The study found a clear overall preference for non-minimalist graphs, which the authors interpret as low acceptance of the high-data-ink-ratio principle.
  > "a clear preference of non-minimalist bar-graphs, suggesting low acceptance of minimalist design principles such as high data-ink ratio."
- **[supporting]** The experiment measured subjective preference/attitude (not comprehension, accuracy, or recall), using 87 students split across three experimental conditions rating a standard bar graph versus a minimalist Tufte-style version of identical information.
  > "Eighty seven students, divided into three experimental conditions"
- **[supporting]** The authors caveat that participants had no prior exposure to minimalist graphs, so unfamiliarity (not inferior design) may explain the preference for the standard 'chartjunk' versions.
  > "Subjects had no prior experience with the minimalist graph and therefore familiarity might have an effect on the results."
- **[supporting]** The paper frames Tufte's data-ink/minimalism stance as a largely theoretical or ideological position that had not been empirically tested for user preference before this study.
  > "empirical results on people's preferences to the mostly theoretical/ideological debate over approaches to the presentation of quantitative information."

#### Source `ae68cf47` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment, time limits affected comprehension and short-term recall, while chart type (embellished vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall — meaning embellishment's effect was conditional, not a uniform help or harm. This challenges any blanket claim that removing non-data ink (chartjunk) reliably improves comprehension or recall.
  > "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."
- **[supporting]** The study tested embellished charts drawn from Nigel Holmes against plain grayscale charts, restricted to larger data sets of 10 or more data points and viewed under different time limits — an explicit extension of the Bateman et al. (2010) 'Useful Junk?' paradigm into denser charts and time-constrained viewing.
  > "compared embellished charts (selected from the work of Nigel Holmes) and plain, grayscale charts, focusing on those that consisted of larger data sets (10 or more data points)"
- **[tangential]** The paper frames a tension central to the data-ink debate: minimalism is advocated by well-cited visualization guidelines (e.g., Tufte's data-ink doctrine), yet practicing designers routinely add visual embellishment — motivating empirical rather than doctrinal resolution of the chartjunk question.
  > "Although many well-cited theories or guidelines for visualization design advocate 'minimalism', designers tend to include a wide variety of visual embellishments in their charts."

#### Source `ae9f695a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010-04 (CHI 2010, April 10–15, 2010, Atlanta, GA, USA)
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment (20 participants, 14 charts: Nigel Holmes embellished 'chartjunk' versions vs. plain bar/line equivalents), interpretation accuracy for embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts on chart subject, categories, and trend — directly contradicting the minimalist claim that decoration impairs comprehension.
  > "We found that people’s accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts... There was no significant difference between plain and image charts for interactive interpretation accuracy (i.e., when the charts were visible)."
- **[central]** After a 2-3 week delay, recall of both chart topic and details (categories and trend) was significantly better for the embellished Holmes charts than for plain charts (long-term group: subject t9=2.56 p=.015; categories t9=5.03 p≈.000; trend t9=1.95 p=.042; value message t9=2.41 p=.020).
  > "After a long-term gap (2-3 weeks), recall of both the chart topic and the details (categories and trend) was significantly better for Holmes charts."
- **[supporting]** The memorability advantage of embellished charts did NOT appear immediately — there was no significant difference in recall accuracy between embellished and plain charts after a five-minute gap; the benefit emerged only at the long-term (2-3 week) delay.
  > "There was also no significant difference in recall accuracy after a five-minute gap."
- **[supporting]** Participants extracted the chart's intended 'value message' significantly more often from embellished charts than from plain ones, both when describing (t19=3.37, p=.003) and in immediate recall (t9=2.24, p=.026) — suggesting embellishment can aid, not just decorate, message communication.
  > "There was a difference in the description of a value message of the charts (t19=3.37, p=.003), with better descriptions of a value message for the Holmes charts than the plain charts"
- **[supporting]** Participants significantly preferred the embellished charts: they most enjoyed them (χ²=8.9, p=.003), found them most attractive (χ²=11.8, p=.001), and rated them easiest to remember (χ²=15.2, p≈.000). A key caveat: viewing time was self-paced/unconstrained, so accuracy parity may not hold under time pressure.
  > "participants most enjoyed charts with visual embellishments (χ²=8.9, p=.003) and found them to be the most attractive (χ²=11.8, p=.001)."

#### Source `afa4cee2` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-04-18
- **[central]** In a controlled reading study, the presence of a word-scale visualization (sparkline) did NOT improve answer accuracy over text alone: accuracy was 0.82 with the visualization vs 0.86 without it, a 0.04 difference the authors call too small to be practical. This contradicts the popular claim that sparklines improve comprehension/recall versus equivalent text.
  > "We could also not con[fi]rm H2. Figure 6 shows a breakdown of the accuracy rates for the two cases... No clear di[ff]erence between the two cases is evident. The di[ff]erence between the case 'word-scale visualization' (GM 0.82; CI [0.69, 0.87]) and the case 'no word-scale visualization' (GM 0.86; CI [0.64, 0.94]) was only 0.04|also too small to likely make a practical di[ff]erence."
- **[central]** Information encoded in a sparkline was more prominent and more easily remembered than the same information written in text: in sentences where the visualization contradicted the text, readers who missed the contradiction pulled their answer from the visualization (0.45) far more often than from the sentence (0.17).
  > "We found that the information encoded in the visualizations is more prominent and easily remembered than information in the written text... When participants did not identify the ambiguity, they tended to pull their answer most often from the word-scale visualization (GM 0.45; CI [0.27, 0.66]) rather than the sentence (GM 0.17; CI [0.07, 0.39]). This partially con[fi]rms H3, in that when readers did not spot the ambiguities word-scale visualizations were used more."
- **[supporting]** Where a sparkline is placed in text (above the word, to its right, or revealed interactively on overlay) had no practical effect on reading speed: the spread between fastest ('right', 7s) and slowest ('no visualization', 7.46s) placements was only 0.46s, and even the interactive overlay (7.14s) showed no detrimental effect.
  > "Surprisingly, we were unable to con[fi]rm H1 that placement had an e[ff]ect on reading performance... the di[ff]erence to the slowest placement position 'no word-scale visualization' (GM 7.46s; CI [6.7, 8.5]) was only 0.46s|too small to likely make a practical di[ff]erence. It is particularly interesting to see that interaction had no practical detrimental e[ff]ect with the 'overlay' condition's GM at 7.14s (CI [5.6, 8.1])."
- **[supporting]** These findings come from a small, preliminary study (a CHI Extended Abstract, not a full archival paper): 12 participants, all PhD students with HCI/InfoVis expertise, reading single sentences from a fictional dataset — so the evidence is suggestive rather than definitive and limits generalizability to general readers and longer texts.
  > "We recruited twelve (four females) participants with their age ranging from 20{30. They were all PhD students at the Computer Science department and had expertise in Human-Computer Interaction (HCI) and Information Visualization (InfoVis). One had minor dyslexia."
- **[tangential]** The study adopts Tufte's own framing, defining word-scale visualizations as a generalization of his sparkline ('a small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution'), and cites Tufte's Beautiful Evidence (2006) as the origin of the concept being tested.
  > "these visualizations... are a generalization of the more well-known term sparkline: 'a small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution' [11]... [11] Tufte, E. R. Beautiful Evidence. Graphics Press, Cheshire, CT, 2006."

#### Source `aff4e085` — quality: **primary** · published: 2012-12 (IEEE TVCG 18(12):2759-2768)
- **[central]** A controlled empirical study (Borgo et al., IEEE TVCG 2012) found that visual embellishments can improve memory retention of the information depicted in a visualization, directly contradicting a strict reading of Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk minimalism that all non-data ink should be removed.
  > "visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization"
- **[central]** The same study found visual embellishments carry a measurable cost: they slow visual search performance, meaning embellishments help some tasks (memory) while hurting others (search speed) rather than being uniformly good or bad.
  > "visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search"
- **[supporting]** The study found no clean benefit of embellishments for concept comprehension; the effect on grasping key concepts was mixed/inconclusive rather than positive.
  > "a complex pattern as to the benefits of visual embellishments in helping participants grasp key concepts from visualization"
- **[supporting]** The study used a dual-task (divided-attention) experimental design intended to approximate real-world viewing conditions, rather than single-task focused attention, which is the methodological basis for its claims about memory and search.
  > "The dual-task methodology was crucial, as it created "divided attention" conditions more representative of real-world viewing contexts where full focus isn't maintained."

### Run B — sibling (105 agents)

#### Source `a0063b95` — quality: **unreliable** · published: 1991

#### Source `a0471867` — quality: **primary** · published: 1992
- **[central]** Coll's controlled study found a task-by-format interaction: tables outperformed bar graphs for retrieving specific values, while bar graphs outperformed tables for retrieving relational/comparison information. Neither format is universally superior; superiority depends on task type.
  > "In the table condition, specific value retrieval scored higher than relational information retrieval, while in the graph condition, the reverse was true."
- **[supporting]** The study was a controlled two-factor experiment crossing display type (table vs. bar graph) with task type (retrieve relative/relational information vs. retrieve specific values), establishing point-value lookup vs. relational reading as the operative task distinction.
  > "A two-factor experiment was performed with two levels of Display Presentation Type (table versus bar graph) and two levels of Task Type (retrieve relative information versus retrieve specific values)."
- **[supporting]** This is primary empirical (controlled-experiment) support, published 1992 in Information & Management (author Joan H. Coll), for the 'match representation to task' principle — relevant to the claim that tables can beat graphics depending on the task, rather than tables or graphics being categorically better.
  > "An experimental study of the efficacy of tables versus bar graphs with respect to type of task"

#### Source `a0c561d1` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020 (CHI 2020; arXiv preprint July 2019, revised January 2020)
- **[central]** Truncating a chart's y-axis (not starting at zero) significantly increases the perceived magnitude/severity of an effect — empirically demonstrated, not just asserted.
  > "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)"
- **[central]** The exaggeration from truncation was NOT significantly different between bar charts and line charts — both chart types were distorted, challenging the common convention that only bars must start at zero.
  > "There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50)"
- **[central]** Adding explicit visual cues that signal the axis is truncated (broken-axis marks, gradient fades) did NOT reduce the perceived exaggeration — a popular 'just mark the break' fix is contradicted by the evidence.
  > "there was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05)"
- **[supporting]** The truncation-induced overestimation of effect size persisted even when participants could accurately estimate the underlying numeric values, indicating a perceptual rather than purely numeric-reading bias.
  > "perceived severity was significantly different across levels of truncation (F(1,20)=11, p=0.003)"
- **[supporting]** Rather than a blanket zero-baseline rule, the authors recommend matching axis range to the scale of the meaningful effect, independent of chart encoding.
  > "designers consider the scale of the meaningful effect sizes and variation they intend to communicate, regardless of the visual encoding"

#### Source `a14ecff4` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019 (arXiv preprint July 2019; published IEEE Transactions on Visualization and Computer Graphics / IEEE VIS 2019)
- **[central]** A controlled study comparing three geo-temporal visualization techniques (juxtaposition/small-multiples-style layouts) found that no single technique is universally best for identifying correlation between two variables over space and time; effectiveness depends strongly on the specific task. This is direct empirical evidence against any blanket claim that small-multiples/juxtaposition always wins for comparison tasks.
  > "Our results show that a visualization's effectiveness depends strongly on the task to be carried out. Based on these findings we present a set of design guidelines about geo-temporal visualization techniques for communicating correlation."
- **[supporting]** The study operationalized the design choice as juxtaposition strategy plus encoding channel: either juxtaposing all locations for a given time step, or juxtaposing all time steps for a given location, and encoding the thematic attributes either with overlaid symbols or with visual channels of the map features themselves. These juxtaposition layouts are the empirical instantiation of Tufte's small-multiples idea applied to maps over time.
  > "either juxtaposing all locations for a given time step, or juxtaposing all time steps for a given location; and encoding thematic attributes either using symbols overlaid on top of map features, or using visual channels of the map features themselves"
- **[supporting]** Task difficulty/effectiveness was systematically modulated by granularity along both the time dimension (all time steps, a subrange, or one step) and the space dimension (all locations, a subregion, or one location) — establishing that the right visualization choice is contingent on the scope of the comparison, not fixed.
  > "Tasks varied in granularity for both dimensions: time (all time steps, a subrange of steps, one step only) and space (all locations, locations in a subregion, one location only)."
- **[tangential]** Prior to this 2019 study, the effectiveness of visualization techniques for communicating correlation between two variables that evolve jointly over space and time had not been empirically tested; earlier work only covered a single variable over space/time or two variables at one point in time. This marks the question of small-multiples-for-correlation as comparatively under-evidenced.
  > "But how effective visualization techniques are at communicating correlation between two variables that evolve over space and time remains to be investigated."

#### Source `a20ca9d8` — quality: **primary** · published: 2002
- **[central]** Blasio & Bisantz's controlled monitoring-task study found high data-ink displays produced significantly FASTER identification of out-of-range values than medium or low data-ink displays — partial empirical support for Tufte's data-ink maximization, extended for the first time to dynamic on-screen displays (not just static print graphs).
  > "participants being significantly faster in identifying out-of-range values using high data-ink displays as opposed to medium or low data-ink displays"
- **[central]** The apparent 'high data-ink' speed advantage is confounded: the high data-ink display was purely numerical/digital while the medium and low displays were graphical gauges, so the result may reflect the superiority of digital readouts for reading exact values rather than data-ink ratio per se. This undercuts using the study as clean proof that removing non-data ink improves comprehension.
  > "the high data-ink display was purely numerical (or "digital") while the medium and low data-ink displays were graphical in nature"
- **[supporting]** The authors framed the result as only 'some support' (not strong/conclusive support) for the data-ink maximization principle, in a simulated industrial-gauge monitoring context — making this qualified, not decisive, evidence for chartjunk minimalism.
  > "found some support for the principle of data-ink maximization in a simulated monitoring task using dynamic displays which emulated industrial gauges"
- **[supporting]** The study was a small-N laboratory experiment: 24 participants performed a simulated process-monitoring task tracking nine dynamic variables — limiting the strength and generalizability of the data-ink finding.
  > "Twenty-four participants ... perform a simulated process monitoring task in which nine dynamic variables were to be monitored"
- **[tangential]** Citation correction relevant to the fact-check: this source is Blasio, A.J. & Bisantz, A.M. (2002), International Journal of Industrial Ergonomics 30(2):89-102 — NOT 'Gillan & Sorensen (2009, Applied Ergonomics)' as labeled in the research brief.
  > "Blasio, Anthony J.; Bisantz, Ann M. ... International journal of industrial ergonomics: IE ... Vol. 30, No. 2, pp. 89-102 ... Year: 2002"

#### Source `a2c9e1f8` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-04 (CHI 2015; DOI 10.1145/2702123.2702608)
- **[central]** This is the first controlled experiment to empirically test whether classic graphical-integrity distortion techniques (Tufte's 'lie factor', Huff's 'How to Lie with Statistics') actually deceive viewers; a 250-participant crowdsourced study found all tested distortions significantly biased responses, with distorted charts producing responses 58.5% to 129.5% larger than undistorted controls.
  > "The results conrm that these techniques do lead to major misinterpretation from the readers side and that the effects are also rather large. When asked to compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5, the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition."
- **[central]** Truncating a bar chart's axis (using a non-zero baseline) empirically and significantly exaggerates the perceived magnitude difference between bars: deceptive bars had a higher mean rank (48.60) than control bars (31.08), Mann-Whitney U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003, r=0.37 — controlled-experiment support for the convention that bar charts should start at zero.
  > "The treatments that involved bar-charts (affected by the "truncated axis" distortion), the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37."
- **[central]** Encoding a quantity by the radius of a circle (so visual area grows with the square of the value) significantly inflates the perceived difference versus encoding the value as area directly; in bubble charts the deceptive (radius-mapped) condition raised mean rank to 48.52 vs 32.47 control, U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34.
  > "from Figure 3 one may conclude that Y is "a lot" bigger than X, when the quantity is mapped to the radius of the circle (in the deceptive visualization)... When the quantity is mapped to the area, as in the control condition, the visualization is less susceptible to deception."
- **[central]** Manipulating a line chart's aspect ratio (widening one axis to change the slope/angle of the trend) produced the largest exaggeration effect of all techniques tested: control line mean rank 24.42 vs deceptive 55.04, U=1409, Z=5.88, p<0.0001, r=0.66 — making aspect ratio the strongest single distortion in the study.
  > "for the "aspect ratio" distortion type, the mean ranks of Line (Control) and Line (Deceptive) were 24.42 and 55.04, respectively; U = 1409, Z = 5.88, p <0.0001, r = 0.66."
- **[supporting]** Inverting the y-axis causes outright reversal of the message (not mere exaggeration): of 38 participants who saw the inverted-axis chart, 30 (78.95%) read the trend backwards and only 7 (18.42%) correctly, versus 39 of 40 (97.5%) correct in the control; Freeman-Halton/Fisher exact test p<0.0001.
  > "Out of the 38 selected participants who saw the deceptive visualization, 30 responded incorrectly, 7 correctly and one chose the uncertain response. For the 40 participants who saw the control condition, 39 responded correctly, 1 responded incorrectly and no participant reported uncertainty."

#### Source `a2ded9ac` — quality: **blog** · published: 2011 (April, May, and June 2011)
- **[central]** Few reports that the Bateman et al. (2010) "Useful Junk?" study found embellished/chartjunk charts were comprehended as accurately as plain charts, and produced better recall of the chart's subject and pattern after two-to-three weeks but not better recall immediately afterwards.
  > "a reader will comprehend the message as well as he would had the graph lacked embellishment and will also better recall the basic subject of the chart ... pattern in the data two or three weeks later, but no better soon afterwards."
- **[central]** The Bateman study's authors themselves concluded their results challenge Tufte-style data-ink minimalism, stating the findings question premises of the minimalist approach to chart design.
  > "cautious about recommending that all charts be produced in this style, our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach to chart design."
- **[central]** Few's central counter-argument is that the Bateman experiment is methodologically weak — relying on uncontrolled factors and small, unrepresentative test groups — and therefore does not actually prove that chartjunk aids comprehension or recall.
  > "Methodological Shortcomings ... Unconsidered and Uncontrolled Factors ... Small, Unrepresentative Test Groups"
- **[supporting]** Few endorses Tufte's position that decorative chartjunk does not improve a graphic's effectiveness; graphics succeed or fail on their data content, not ornamentation.
  > "Chartjunk does not achieve the goals of its propagators. The overwhelming fact of data graphics is that they stand or fall on their content, gracefully displayed."

#### Source `a32e1031` — quality: **primary** · published: 2012-12
- **[central]** Visual embellishments (chartjunk) improved memorability — participants better remembered the information depicted in embellished visualizations, directly contradicting the pure data-ink/minimalism prescription that non-data ink should always be removed.
  > "Visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization."
- **[central]** Visual embellishments carry a measurable cost: they slow visual search, so the memorability benefit is not free — it trades against efficiency of finding information in the display.
  > "Visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search."
- **[supporting]** Embellishments do not uniformly aid comprehension; the effect on grasping key concepts is mixed rather than a clear win or loss, so claims that chartjunk universally improves (or degrades) understanding are unsupported.
  > "The results show a complex pattern as to the benefits of visual embellishments in helping participants grasp key concepts."
- **[supporting]** The study used a dual-task (divided-attention) methodology to simulate real-world viewing such as meetings and lectures, which strengthens external validity of the embellishment-vs-comprehension/memory findings beyond idealized single-task lab conditions.
  > "This approach made embellishment effects more observable and provided "additional masking in memory-based trials.""

#### Source `a3683ac4` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-04 (ACM CHI 2015; also issued as NYU Public Law & Legal Theory Working Paper, Feb 2015)
- **[central]** In a controlled crowdsourced experiment (n=250 for the exaggeration study), truncating a bar chart's axis (non-zero baseline) significantly inflated the perceived difference between bars: mean Likert response rose from 1.45 (control) to 2.77 (deceptive), a statistically significant effect (Mann-Whitney U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003, r=0.37; p<0.001 overall). This is direct empirical support for the convention that bar charts should start at zero.
  > "The treatments that involved bar-charts (affected by the "truncated axis" distortion), the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37."
- **[central]** Encoding a quantity by the radius (rather than area) of a circle significantly exaggerated perceived magnitude (bubble mean response 1.71 control vs 2.71 deceptive; U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34), empirically confirming Tufte's graphical-integrity principle that the graphical area should map one-to-one to the underlying data.
  > "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."
- **[central]** An inverted axis on a line-area chart reversed the message viewers took away: ~79% of participants shown the deceptive version gave the wrong interpretation (30 of 38 incorrect) versus only 2.5% in the control (1 of 40 incorrect); the difference was highly significant (Fisher's exact / Freeman-Halton, p<0.0001).
  > "Out of the 38 selected participants who saw the deceptive visualization, 30 responded incorrectly, 7 correctly and one chose the uncertain response. For the 40 participants who saw the control condition, 39 responded correctly, 1 responded incorrectly and no participant reported uncertainty."
- **[supporting]** Distorting a line chart's aspect ratio produced the largest deceptive effect of all techniques tested (mean response 1.39 control vs 3.19 deceptive; U=1409, Z=5.88, p<0.0001, r=0.66 — a large effect size), indicating line-chart aspect ratio is a potent vehicle for exaggerating perceived rates of change.
  > "for the "aspect ratio" distortion type, the mean ranks of Line (Control) and Line (Deceptive) were 24.42 and 55.04, respectively; U = 1409, Z = 5.88, p <0.0001, r = 0.66."
- **[supporting]** Aggregated across the message-exaggeration conditions, deceptive charts drove viewer responses 58.5% to 129.5% higher than the matched control charts, quantifying that Tufte-flagged distortions (truncated axes, area/radius encoding, aspect ratio) produce large, not marginal, perceptual biases.
  > "When asked to compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5, the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition."

#### Source `a4ac4405` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014
- **[central]** An extension of the Bateman et al. 'useful junk' work, using larger data sets (10+ data points) and comparing Nigel Holmes embellished charts against plain grayscale charts, found that chart type (embellished vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall — i.e., embellishment is not cost-free once data sets grow, complicating the clean 'no comprehension penalty' result of Bateman 2010.
  > "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."
- **[supporting]** Imposing a time limit on chart viewing affected both comprehension and short-term recall performance, indicating that any benefit or cost of visual embellishment is moderated by how much viewing time the reader has.
  > "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."
- **[supporting]** Embellished (Holmes) charts changed how long readers needed to study the chart while answering questions, suggesting embellishment carries a reading/processing-time cost rather than being purely decorative and free.
  > "The type of chart affected the time needed to review the chart while answering the questions."
- **[supporting]** Participants rated the embellished Holmes charts as more attractive and more memorable than plain charts, replicating the subjective-preference finding from Bateman et al. 2010 — but this is a preference/aesthetic measure, distinct from objective comprehension accuracy.
  > "Additionally, participants found Holmes charts more attractive and memorable."

#### Source `a4eae72c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2023-04 (CHI 2023; lab blog dated 2023-04-17)
- **[central]** In a real-world corpus of COVID-19 chart tweets, the dominant way people misled was NOT visualization design violations: of misleading visualization posts, only about 11% violated common visualization design guidelines (axis truncation, chartjunk, dual axes, etc.). This empirically undercuts the popular assumption that visual tricks are the main chart-deception mechanism.
  > "84% [of posts offering interpretations] are misleading, yet only 11% of such misleading visualization posts violate common visualization design guidelines."
- **[central]** Visual misreading (being fooled by axis tricks, area encoding, etc.) was vanishingly rare in the wild: less than 1% of cases involved actual visual misinterpretation, i.e., almost no viewers were fooled by the visual design itself. The deception happened through reasoning/framing, not the chart's pixels.
  > "almost none of them actually fooled anyone"
- **[supporting]** Charts that follow graphical-integrity rules (faithfully/correctly plotted data) can still be used to mislead, meaning compliance with Tufte-style integrity guidelines (Lie Factor, zero baselines, honest axes) is not sufficient to prevent deception.
  > "even faithfully plotted data visualizations can be used to support misinformation online"
- **[supporting]** The study identifies six reasoning-based deception patterns as the actual mechanisms of misleading charts — cherry-picking time frames, arbitrary thresholds, false causal inference, data validity issues, statistical nuance failures, and scientific misrepresentation — rather than design-guideline violations.
  > "Cherry-picking – selective time frames or incomplete datasets; Arbitrary thresholds – subjective significance judgments without expert basis; Causal inference – assuming causation from visual correlation patterns"
- **[central]** The paper's central thesis explicitly rejects the conventional framing that flawed visual design is the primary route to chart-based deception.
  > "violations of visualization design guidelines are not the dominant way people mislead with charts"

#### Source `a5417c51` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020-01-08
- **[central]** In a controlled within-subjects experiment (n=40), y-axis truncation inflated perceived effect size equally for bar charts and line charts — there was no significant difference between the two chart types. This contradicts the popular rule (Cairo, Bergstrom & West, Tufte-for-time-series) that the zero-baseline requirement applies to bars but exempts line charts.
  > "Our results fail to support our second hypothesis. There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50). [...] This qualitative bias occurs in both line charts and bar charts, as well as in bar charts that visually indicate either broken axes or the continuation of bars beyond the bounds of the chart."
- **[central]** Greater y-axis truncation produced significantly greater perceived severity of the effect size, with all three truncation levels (axis starting at 0%, 25%, 50%) significantly different from each other; the effect was large and statistically robust.
  > "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001). A post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other."
- **[central]** Explicit visual cues that a y-axis is truncated — broken-axis glyphs and gradient-fill bars — did NOT reduce the exaggeration of perceived effect size. This undercuts the common guideline that marking the axis break (e.g. a 'torn paper' or broken-axis symbol) makes truncation acceptable/honest.
  > "Our results fail to support our first hypothesis: there was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05). [...] Merely indicating that truncation has occurred, even in a prominent and unambiguous way, may not be sufficient to 'de-bias' viewers of truncated charts."
- **[supporting]** The truncation bias is perceptual, not a misreading of numbers: in Experiment Three (n=25) the inflated perceived severity persisted even when participants accurately estimated the underlying values, and error in estimating the trend did not differ across truncation levels.
  > "The accurate estimation of values does not seem to counteract the visual magnification of difference. Subjective judgments about effect size appear to be visual rather than mathematical or statistical judgments."
- **[central]** Despite finding that truncation reliably exaggerates effect size, the authors explicitly reject the universal rule that all charts must start at zero and reject the binary 'honest vs dishonest' framing; they argue there is no domain-agnostic ground truth for how large an effect should look, so the designer must choose the y-axis range to match the meaningful effect size.
  > "we resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0. [...] There is no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe, important, or meaningful an effect size ought to be. [...] We reject the unequivocal dichotomy of 'honest' and 'dishonest' charts."

#### Source `a6807ffe` — quality: **primary** · published: 2024
- **[central]** In a controlled within-subject experiment, bar graphs with a truncated y-axis (y-axis starting above zero) had a negligible deceptive effect (odds ratio ~1.439), and line graphs with a truncated y-axis were similarly negligible (OR ~1.508) - ranking among the LEAST misleading of the 14 graph types tested. This challenges the conventional dogma that bars must always start at zero.
  > "Additionally, when participants received a line graph with truncated y-axis (OR » 1.508), a bar graph with a truncated y-axis (OR » 1.439), or a map graph with an inverted color scale (OR » 1.122), their answers were also less likely to be accurate than receiving the non-misleading counterpart, although the size of deceptive impact was negligible compared to other misleading graph types in other large, medium, and small groups"
