- **[central]** Compressed y-axes (line and bar graphs with the y-axis extended to an unreasonably high value) produced NO statistically significant loss of interpretation accuracy versus the non-misleading versions; measured accuracy was actually equal or slightly higher for the 'misleading' versions (line: 0.926 misleading vs 0.904 non-misleading; bar: 0.922 vs 0.904).
  > "certain misleading graph types, such as line graphs with compressed y-axis, bar graphs with compressed y-axis, and bar graphs with pictorial bars, showed no significant difference in accuracy compared to the non-misleading counterpart (ps > 0.1)"
- **[central]** The most deceptive features were axis-ORDER distortions, not axis truncation: line graphs with irregularly spaced (manipulated) x-axis time intervals had a large effect (OR ~15.419) and inverted y-axes had a large effect (OR ~7.144), far exceeding the impact of 3D effects.
  > "Misleading graph types with large effects were line graphs with manipulated x-axes (OR » 15.419) and line graphs with inverted y-axes (OR » 7.144)."
- **[supporting]** The authors conclude the deciding factor for deception is whether axis values deviate from the standard increasing-bottom-to-top / left-to-right order; when that order is preserved, additional manipulations like truncation or compression have negligible or insignificant deceptive impact.
  > "When the values on axes deviate from the conventional pattern of incrementally increasing from bottom to top or left to right, the deceptive impact becomes more noticeable. Conversely, when the value order on the axes follows this standard pattern, with the lowest value at the bottom or on the left, any additional misleading visual features, such as a truncated or compressed y-axis, tend to have a negligible or insignificant deceptive impact."
- **[supporting]** Pictorial bars (icons/images substituted for bars - a form of chartjunk) did not significantly reduce comprehension accuracy (bar pictorial: 0.915 misleading vs 0.955 non-misleading, ps > 0.1), while 3D effects had only small-to-medium effects (bar 3D OR ~1.757; pie 3D OR ~4.228).
  > "Conversely, a line or bar graph with a compressed y-axis or truncated y-axis, or a bar graph with pictorial bars, had little or no significant deceptive impact."

#### Source `a686b040` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-04-18
- **[central]** In a controlled reading study, embedding a word-scale visualization (sparkline) in a sentence did NOT measurably improve recall accuracy versus text alone: accuracy was ~0.82 with the visualization vs ~0.86 without — a 0.04 difference the authors call too small to matter. This contradicts the popular claim that sparklines improve memorization/recall.
  > "We could also not confirm H2. Figure 6 shows a breakdown of the accuracy rates for the two cases: "word-scale visualization" and "no word-scale visualization". No clear difference between the two cases is evident. The difference between the case "word-scale visualization" (GM 0.82s; CI [0.69, 0.87]) and the case "no word-scale visualization" (GM 0.86s; CI [0.64, 0.94]) was only 0.04s—also too small to likely make a practical difference."
- **[central]** When information was encoded ambiguously in both text and visualization and readers failed to spot the conflict, they drew their answer from the embedded visualization far more often (GM 0.45) than from the sentence text (GM 0.17) — evidence that an embedded word-sized graphic is attended to and remembered more than the surrounding prose.
  > "When participants did not identify the ambiguity, they tended to pull their answer most often from the word-scale visualization (GM 0.45; CI [0.27, 0.66]) rather than the sentence (GM 0.17; CI [0.07, 0.39]). This partially confirms H3, in that when readers did not spot the ambiguities word-scale visualizations were used more."
- **[supporting]** The placement of a word-scale visualization (above the line / to the right / interactive overlay / none) had no practical effect on reading speed: fastest 'right' geometric mean 7.0s vs slowest 'no visualization' 7.46s, a 0.46s gap; even the interaction-requiring overlay (7.14s) showed no detriment.
  > "The fastest placement position was "right" with a GM of 7s (CI [6, 8.3]). Yet, the difference to the slowest placement position "no word-scale visualization" (GM 7.46s; CI [6.7, 8.5]) was only 0.46s—too small to likely make a practical difference. It is particularly interesting to see that interaction had no practical detrimental effect with the "overlay" condition's GM at 7.14s (CI [5.6, 8.1])."
- **[supporting]** The study's evidence is exploratory and weak in generalizability: n=12, all PhD students in the same CS department with HCI/InfoVis expertise, tested on single sentences rather than paragraphs — so its null findings on speed/recall are a first-study signal, not a settled result.
  > "We recruited twelve (four females) participants with their age ranging from 20–30. They were all PhD students at the Computer Science department and had expertise in Human-Computer Interaction (HCI) and Information Visualization (InfoVis). One had minor dyslexia."
- **[tangential]** The paper adopts Tufte's definition of a sparkline ('a small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution', from Beautiful Evidence 2006) and treats 'word-scale visualizations' as a generalization permitting other encodings and slightly larger sizes.
  > "These visualizations [...] are a generalization of the more well-known term sparkline: "a small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution" [11]."

#### Source `a76394e1` — quality: **primary** · published: 2009-04 (CHI 2009, April 4-9, Boston; PDF created 2009-01-11)
- **[central]** Increasing data density (Tufte's 'maximize data marks per chart area' advice) does not necessarily improve graphical perception; denser displays can encode more but do not automatically yield better viewer comprehension. This is a direct empirical counter to the naive reading of Tufte's data-density maxim.
  > "advises designers to maximize data density (data marks per chart area) ... Such approaches excel at increasing the amount of information that can be encoded within a display. However, increased data density does not necessarily imply improved graphical perception for visualization viewers."
- **[central]** There is a quantified legibility ceiling: value-estimation error stays flat (plateaus) at larger chart heights but rises linearly once chart 'virtual resolution' drops below ~24 pixels, fitting a linear regression with R-squared = 0.986 and slope -4.1 estimation-error units per halving (log2 pixel) of height.
  > "At resolutions below 24 pixels, error appears to increase linearly as the virtual resolution halves. To test this observation, we ran a linear regression of error and virtual resolution at resolutions of 24 pixels and below. The regression fits with R2 = 0.986 and a slope of -4.1 units / log2 pixel, indicating a linear increase."
- **[central]** Smaller-than-conventional charts can be optimal: a 24-pixel height minimized estimation time while preserving accuracy for line and 1-band charts, and shrinking from full size to half size (24 px) made estimates faster with comparable accuracy. Bigger is not automatically better, and a denser/smaller default can win.
  > "For both normal line charts and 1-band mirror charts, we found a chart height of 24 pixels (6.8 mm on our 14.1" 1024 x 768 pixel displays) to be optimal ... For all three chart types, scale factor 1/2 (24 pixels) resulted in faster but comparably accurate performance over charts twice as large."
- **[supporting]** Higher-density layered (horizon-graph) encodings only pay off below a size threshold: 2-band mirrored charts beat line and 1-band charts on accuracy only at heights under 24 pixels; above that, a simple 1-band mirror chart is advised. Density is conditionally beneficial, not universally.
  > "we also found that 2-band mirrored charts led to better estimation accuracies for chart heights less than 24 pixels (6.8 mm on our displays). For larger chart sizes, we advise scaling 1-band mirrored charts. For smaller sizes, we advise adding layered bands."
- **[supporting]** At word-sized (sparkline-scale) heights down to 2 pixels, the position encoding becomes too coarse to read and viewers shift to relying on color/saturation, marking the practical lower bound where a position-encoded line chart stops functioning as such.
  > "Our follow-up experiment investigated chart heights as small as 2 pixels, at which point the information conveyed by position encoding is extremely coarse. Subjects reported relying on color to form estimates at this small size."

#### Source `a84a2d84` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment with 96 crowdworkers performing nine trend-comparison tasks on mobile phones, small multiples consistently produced faster task completion times than an animated scatterplot design.
  > "We found that those using a small multiples design consistently completed tasks in less time, albeit with slightly less confidence than those using an animated design."
- **[supporting]** Small multiples did not win on every dimension: participants using the animated design reported slightly higher confidence than those using small multiples.
  > "those using a small multiples design consistently completed tasks in less time, albeit with slightly less confidence than those using an animated design"
- **[central]** Accuracy was task-dependent rather than uniformly favoring small multiples; neither technique was a universal accuracy winner, with outcomes shaped by the trajectories of target and distractor data items.
  > "The accuracy results were more task-dependent, and we further interpret our results according to the characteristics of the individual tasks, with a specific focus on the trajectories of target and distractor data items in each task."
- **[supporting]** The study identified specific task cases that favor small multiples and others that favor animation, indicating the choice is contingent on task type rather than a blanket superiority of either approach.
  > "We identify cases that appear to favor either animation or small multiples, providing new questions for further experimental research and implications for visualization design on mobile devices."
- **[tangential]** The findings extend prior large-display experimental research to small (mobile) screens, addressing a gap where little visualization research had targeted small displays.
  > "Visualization is increasingly prevalent in mobile applications and mobile-first websites, yet there is little prior visualization research dedicated to small displays."

#### Source `a8e3bf1a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2013-10-13 (accepted 1 Aug 2013, posted online 13 Oct 2013); IEEE TVCG / InfoVis 2013
- **[central]** In a controlled memorability study, visualizations with a 'bad' (low) data-ink ratio — i.e., more chartjunk — scored significantly HIGHER on memorability than minimalist 'good' data-ink-ratio visualizations (M=1.81 vs M=1.23, p<0.001), refuting the authors' own hypothesis that minimalist designs are more memorable.
  > "We also observed a statistically signiﬁcant effect of the data-to-ink ratio attribute on memorability scores with a “bad” (M = 1.81), i.e., low data-to-ink ratio, being higher than a “good” rating (M = 1.23,t(208) = 6.92, p < 0.001)... higher memorability scores were correlated with visualizations containing pictograms, more color, low data-to-ink ratios, and high visual densities. This supports our third hypothesis, and refutes our fourth and ﬁfth hypotheses."
- **[central]** This memorability advantage of clutter applies only to recognition memory of visualizations treated as images, NOT to comprehension; the authors explicitly state the result does not show that chartjunk/clutter aids understanding and warn it may interfere with it.
  > "we tested the memorability of visualizations as images and not the comprehension of the visualizations. Thus, looking at these visualizations as images and not data encodings, these attributes increased their memorability... Nor does excessive visual clutter aid comprehension of the actual information in the visualization (and may instead interfere with it)."
- **[central]** High visual density was associated with significantly higher memorability than low visual density (very dense M=1.83 vs low M=1.28, p<0.001), contradicting the hypothesis that low-density displays are more memorable — again as image recognition, not comprehension.
  > "we observed a statistically signiﬁcant effect of visual density on memorability scores with a high visual density rating of “3” (M = 1.83), i.e., very dense, being greater than a low visual density rating of “1” (M = 1.28,t(115) = 6.08, p < 0.001)"
- **[supporting]** Visualizations containing pictograms / human-recognizable objects were across-the-board more memorable (M=1.93 vs M=1.14, p<0.001); the authors conclude 'not all chart junk is created equal,' attributing the effect to evolved object recognition rather than to any data-encoding benefit.
  > "Visualizations containing pictograms have on average a higher memorability score (Mean (M)=1.93) than visualizations without pictograms (M = 1.14,t(297) = 13.67, p < 0.001). This supports our second hypothesis. Thus not all chart junk is created equal: annotations and representations containing pictograms are across the board more memorable."
- **[supporting]** This was the largest-scale memorability study of its kind, using 2,070 real-world single-panel visualizations and 261 Mechanical Turk participants in a repeat-detection game scored by d-prime; memorability was highly consistent across people (Spearman r=0.81 across half-splits), indicating a real, intrinsic image property rather than individual bias.
  > "We ran the largest scale visualization study to date using 2,070 single-panel visualizations... we collected memorability scores for hundreds of these visualizations, and discovered that observers are consistent in which visualizations they ﬁnd memorable and forgettable... Averaging over 25 such random half-splits, we obtain Spearman’s rank correlations of 0.83 for HR, 0.78 for FAR, and 0.81 for d-prime"

#### Source `a986e16c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2004-05-27
- **[central]** Tufte defines a sparkline as a small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution, intended to be embedded inline wherever a word or number appears (sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet) rather than set apart as a framed, captioned figure.
  > "a small intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution ... sparkline graphics can be everywhere a word or number can be: embedded in a sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet, graphic."
- **[supporting]** Tufte claims sparklines achieve extreme data density relative to a numeric table: a financial table of 24 five-significant-digit numbers is paired with sparklines conveying roughly 14,000 numbers readable to 1-2 significant digits.
  > "This financial table reports 24 numbers accurate to 5 significant digits; the accompanying sparklines show about 14,000 numbers readable from 1 to 2 significant digits."
- **[supporting]** Tufte's sparkline aspect-ratio rule borrows William Cleveland's empirically-grounded 'banking to 45 degrees' principle: choose the height-to-width ratio so the line's average slope tends toward ~45 degrees (a 'lumpy' rather than spiky or flat profile).
  > "aspect ratios should be such that time-series graphics tend toward a lumpy profile rather than a spiky profile or a flat profile"
- **[supporting]** Tufte asserts sparklines require near-print resolution (>1,200 dpi), and that computer monitors deliver only about 10% of paper's resolution -- a claim that bears directly on whether his print-era rules transfer to on-screen UIs.
  > "computer monitors operate at only "about 10% of paper's resolution""
- **[tangential]** Tufte frames sparkline design as 'design minimization, not data minimization' -- reduce decorative clutter (frames, backgrounds, moire) while maximizing data shown, distinguishing his minimalism stance from simply removing marks.
  > "design minimization, not data minimization"

#### Source `aa37735b` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015 (Computer Graphics Forum, Proceedings EuroVis 2015, vol. 34, no. 3, pp. 221-230; DOI 10.1111/cgf.12634)
- **[central]** In relative-comparison tasks (estimating one bar as a percentage of another), every tested embellishment EXCEPT bars extended below zero significantly increased reading error versus the plain baseline bar chart — including minor cosmetic changes like merely rounding the tops of bars. This directly counters any blanket claim that 'chartjunk'/embellishment is harmless to comprehension accuracy.
  > "All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a signiﬁcantly higher error rate."
- **[central]** Bars whose magnitude is encoded by quadratically increasing area (scaling on two axes at once) produced the largest reading errors of any embellishment (relative-task mean log-error 2.33 vs baseline 1.43), empirically confirming Tufte's specific warning against encoding quantity by area/volume.
  > "Conﬁrming the hypothesis of Tufte and others, quadratically-scaled bars led to large errors in relative judgements, even when compared to similar embellishment styles (i.e. triangles, overlapping triangles)."
- **[supporting]** Adding a T-shaped 'cap' (extra non-data ink giving a strong, wider horizontal line at the bar's end) performed equal to, and with slightly lower variance than, the plain baseline for single-value reading — showing that added non-data ink can preserve or aid accuracy when it reinforces the salient feature, contradicting a strict 'less ink is always better' data-ink reading.
  > "These performed equally well as, and with slightly lower variance than, the baseline chart for absolute judgement questions. This result suggests that users indeed rely on strong lines at the ends of bars to mentally extend the bar end to the value axis"
- **[supporting]** The effect of embellishment is strongly task-dependent: for absolute single-bar value reading, only the quadratic-area bars were significantly worse than baseline (mean 1.70 vs 1.41), while capped (1.41) and extended-below-zero (1.45) bars matched the baseline — so the same embellishment can be harmless for one task and harmful for another.
  > "For absolute judgments, which involved estimating the value of a given bar, only quadratic bars (m = 1.70,sd = 1.78) performed signiﬁcantly worse than the baseline (m = 1.41,sd = 1.85)."
- **[central]** The authors explicitly state that Tufte's negative, data-ink-ratio-driven view of chart embellishment rests on a general lack of empirical evidence, yet has nonetheless driven the visualization field's minimalism culture — and note the data-ink ratio taken to its logical end would absurdly reduce a bar to a single-pixel line.
  > "Despite a general lack of empirical evidence supporting this negative view on chart embellishments, it has driven a culture of design simpliﬁcation and sterilization in the data visualization community."

#### Source `aa70fed7` — quality: **primary** · published: 2011 (Journal of Computational and Graphical Statistics, Vol. 20, No. 1, pp. 3–7; received Sept 2009, revised Sept 2010)
- **[central]** The paper's pro-tables thesis is satire, not Gelman's genuine position: he wrote the anti-graph arguments in the voice of a strawman 'old-school' statistician (originally an April Fool's blog post), and elsewhere in the same paper states he believes graphing is essential. It contains NO controlled study or empirical comparison of tables vs. graphs. This means the paper cannot be cited as evidence that tables beat graphs, despite its title.
  > "this article presents a series of attacks on graphical methods in the voice of a hypothetical old-school analytical statistician or social scientist. Although this originated as an April Fool's blog entry (Gelman 2009a), I believe these are strong arguments to be taken seriously—and ultimately accepted in some settings and refuted in others. [...] Those of us who believe graphing to be important and even essential to research would be well advised to think hard about why visual displays are not used more extensively in serious applied research."
- **[supporting]** The one substantive (non-satirical) argument advanced for tables over graphs is epistemic, not perceptual: graphs can visually imply patterns (e.g., differing slopes) that are not statistically significant, and they distance the reader from the exact numbers/uncertainty needed to judge a result. This is an argument about misleading inference, not about comprehension or recall.
  > "graphs are inherently a way of implying results that are often not statistically significant. (And all but the simplest graphs allow so many different visual comparisons, that even if certain main effects actually do pass the p-value test, many many more inevitably will not.)"
- **[supporting]** The paper frames tables' advantage as exact-value access for expert/peer scrutiny and standardization (citing economics journals' tabular norms), not as superiority for general audiences or pattern detection. This bounds the 'tables win' claim to precise lookup by expert readers of small numerical result sets.
  > "A table lays down your results, unadorned, for the readers—and, most importantly, scientific peers—to judge. Good tables often have lots of numbers. [...] when you read an econ article, you can find the numbers that you want; the authors of these articles are laying it on the line and giving you their betas."
- **[tangential]** Specific 'table design' advice in the paper is deliberately bad (satirical) — minimum four significant digits, software-default labels, all-caps variable names, never rounding, plus 3-D colored Excel bar charts with dual y-axes — signaling these are anti-patterns. A reader must not treat any of these as endorsed table/chart guidance.
  > "It is also helpful in a table to have a minimum of four significant digits. A good choice is often to use the default provided by whatever software you have used to fit the model. [...] I recommend using Excel, which has some really nice defaults as well as options such as those 3-D colored bar charts. If you are going to have a graph, you might as well make it pretty."

#### Source `ad53eb27` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010 (CHI 2010, ACM Conference on Human Factors in Computing Systems)
- **[central]** In a controlled experiment comparing heavily embellished 'Holmes-style' charts against plain minimalist versions, embellishment did NOT reduce interpretation accuracy, even though the embellished charts contained far more non-data-ink — directly testing (and contradicting) the strict data-ink-ratio / chartjunk-removal premise.
  > "we did not find any difference in people's in terpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink."
- **[central]** Embellished charts were remembered significantly better than plain charts after a long delay (a gap longer than 12 days, described elsewhere as two-to-three weeks), indicating decorative imagery aided recall of both the chart's topic and its details.
  > "participants were able to remember significantly more about the embellished charts after a gap longer than 12 days, suggesting that the addition of strong visual images does help people to remember both the topic and details of the chart."
- **[supporting]** When the charts were visible there was no accuracy penalty for embellishment, and immediate recall (after a five-minute gap) also showed no significant difference — the memorability advantage of embellishment only emerged at long delay, not in immediate or interactive reading.
  > "There was no significant difference between plain and image charts for interac tive interpretation accuracy (i.e., when the charts were visible). There was also no significant difference in recall accuracy after a five-minute g"
- **[supporting]** Participants subjectively preferred the embellished charts, overwhelmingly choosing Holmes-style charts over plain charts for both reading and remembering.
  > "in their subjective ratings of which type of charts they preferred for reading and remembering participants overwhelmingly chose Holmes-style charts over plain charts."
- **[central]** The authors explicitly frame their findings as challenging the empirical basis of Tufte-style minimalism: the results question the premises of the minimalist (chartjunk-reduction) approach to chart design, though they stop short of recommending all charts be embellished.
  > "Although we are cautious about recommending that all charts be produced in this style, our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach to chart design."

#### Source `adc33eed` — quality: **primary** · published: 2024-05-11
- **[central]** For comparing or estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis of a grouped bar chart does NOT affect task performance — contradicting the blanket rule that bars must always start at zero.
  > "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."
- **[central]** Truncation's harm is task-dependent and tied to monotonicity: for relative-growth (ratio) tasks, monotonic truncation performs about the same as no truncation, while non-monotonic truncation is very likely to impair performance.
  > "truncating monotonically has similar performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance."
- **[central]** The authors' evidence-based recommendation: when a task is unaffected by truncation (e.g., comparing gaps) a designer may truncate to align visuals to the task, but for ratio/growth tasks they should use monotonic truncation rather than no truncation or non-monotonic truncation.
  > "if the tasks examine quantities that are affected by truncation (e.g., Compare Ratios and Estimate Ratio), then we suggest performing monotonic truncat[ion]"
- **[supporting]** The paper frames the zero-baseline debate as bar-specific: bar charts encode magnitude by length/size (distorted by truncation), whereas line and dot charts encode by position/angle, so the zero-baseline rule need not apply to them.
  > "since line charts and dot charts use position/angle to encode information, it is not necessary to hold them to the zero-baseline rule because these encodings do not get distorted"
- **[supporting]** The paper argues prior anti-truncation evidence over-relies on ratio judgments and subjective ratings, motivating a systematic task-by-task analysis rather than a single 'truncation is misleading' verdict.
  > "Our work complements existing empirical work by providing a systematic analysis of y-axis truncation on grouped bar charts."

#### Source `ae4a0de8` — quality: **primary** · published: 2022
- **[central]** In a controlled ranking study, heavily annotated line charts were NOT penalized for clutter; participants preferred charts with the most textual annotations over charts with fewer annotations or text alone. This is direct empirical counter-evidence to the 'minimalism / less text is better' intuition for on-screen charts.
  > "We find that heavily annotated charts were not penalized. In fact, participants preferred the charts with the largest number of textual annotations over charts with fewer annotations or text alone."
- **[central]** For text integration, optimal placement depends on the text's semantic content: axis/encoding descriptions belong by the relevant axis, statistical/numerical facts by the relevant data point (direct labeling), and perceptual/trend statements in the title. Mismatched placement reduces uptake (e.g., axis-content annotations were matched 11.3x more often when placed at the axis than as a title).
  > "they were 11.3x as likely to match the annotation when it was positioned as an axis than when it was a title ([3.18, 40.01], p < 0.01). So, this hypothesis was not supported for L1 takeaways. Titles were matched less often, while axis comments were matched most often."
- **[supporting]** The semantic content of an annotation measurably steers what readers take away: external-context (L4) annotations made readers 6.1x more likely to report an external-context takeaway than an axis/encoding (L1) annotation, and statistical (L2) annotations likewise increased statistical takeaways. Annotation phrasing, not just presence, shapes the message.
  > "Participants were significantly more likely to make L4 conclusions when they were provided with an L4 annotation: 6.1x as likely than when provided with L1 ([3.82, 10.19], p < 0.01); 6.2x as likely than when provided with L2 ([3.85, 10.11], p < 0.01); 3.2x as likely than when provided with L3 ([2.16, 4.79], p < 0.01)."
- **[supporting]** A non-trivial minority of readers (14%) preferred a plain text paragraph over the visual chart, and the textually-oriented group ranked text-only above chart-only; the authors recommend offering a stand-alone text variant. Relevant to the 'tables/text can beat graphics' question for some audiences.
  > "Additionally, a non-trivial minority (14%) of participants preferred a textual paragraph over a visual chart."
- **[tangential]** Scope/limits of the evidence: the study tested 302 participants on univariate line charts only, and measured stated preferences (rankings) and self-reported takeaways/reliance, with analyses preregistered on OSF; it did not measure objective comprehension accuracy or recall, so findings should not be over-generalized to all chart types.
  > "302 participants ranked univariate line charts containing varying amounts of text, ranging from no text (except for the axes) to a written paragraph with no visuals. Participants also described what information they could take away from line charts containing text with varying semantic content."

---

## Part B — Adversarial verifications (150)

Each claim was put to skeptical voters (voter n/3) told to try to REFUTE it; ≥2/3 refutations kill
it. Below: the claim under review, the verdict, confidence, the evidence found, and any
counter-source. Grouped by run.

### Run A — canonical (107 agents)

#### V1 `a051aafe` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled crowdsourced experiment (N=250 for the exaggeration study), a truncated/non-zero y-axis on a bar chart significantly inflated viewers' magnitude judgments: deceptive bar charts drew a mean 'how much bigger' rating of 2.77 (95% CI [2.26,3.28]) vs 1.45 ([1.27,1.62]) for the zero-baseline control, p=0.0003. This is direct empirical support for the convention that bar charts should start at zero."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The treatments that involved bar-charts (affected by the "truncated axis" distortion), the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary source PDF (Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov, Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015, DOI 10.1145/2702123.2702608). All claimed numbers appear verbatim. (1) N: "We recruited 250 unique participants for this study and assigned one of the six treatments to each of them." (2) Means/CIs, from Table 2 ("Average participant response with 95% CI to the 'how much' question," 5-item Likert 1-5): "Bar (Deceptive) Truncated Axis 2.77, 95% CI [2.26, 3.28]" and "Bar (Control) Truncated Axis 1.45, 95% CI [1.27, 1.62]." (3) Significance: "the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37" (one-tailed Mann-Whitney U). The provided supporting quote only carried the mean-ranks/U portion, but the means and CIs are genuinely in the paper's Table 2 and describe the SAME Bar Control-vs-Deceptive truncated-axis contrast as the p-value, so pairing them is legitimate, not a misread. "How much bigger" is a fair paraphrase of the paper's "how much" question. Non-overlapping CIs corroborate significance. The interpretive claim (direct empirical support for the bar-chart zero-baseline convention) is narrow and accurate: a truncated axis is a violated zero baseline and it significantly inflated magnitude judgments. The known counter-literature (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?") qualifies the normative rule but replicates the exaggeration effect, so it does not contradict this scoped claim. Top-tier peer-reviewed venue; source quality matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020) — qualifies the normative zero-baseline rule but replicates the magnitude-exaggeration effect, so it does not refute this scoped claim.

#### V2 `a075e0c6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment, participants preferred standard non-minimalist bar graphs over Tufte's high-data-ink-ratio minimalist versions of the same data — directly contradicting the popular claim that maximizing the data-ink ratio is universally better."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/220956267_Minimalism_in_information_visualization_Attitudes_towards_maximizing_the_data-ink_ratio (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "People did not like Tufte's minimalist design of bar-graphs; they seem to prefer 'chartjunk' instead."

*Evidence:* Claim is supported by a real peer-reviewed primary source: Inbar, Tractinsky & Meyer (2007), "Minimalism in information visualization: attitudes towards maximizing the data-ink ratio," ECCE 2007, pp. 185-188 (ACM DOI 10.1145/1362550.1362587); the ResearchGate URL merely hosts it. The study (87 students, 3 conditions) found a clear preference for standard non-minimalist bar graphs over Tufte's minimalist version of the same data, and the paper itself states subjects "seem to prefer 'chartjunk'" — matching the claim and its quote verbatim. The contradiction of "maximizing data-ink is universally better" holds on the preference dimension (one failing domain breaks universality), and the authors frame it as contradicting the prevailing theoretical preference for minimalism. Two qualifications (which refine, not refute): (1) the study measured PREFERENCE/attitude, not comprehension or task accuracy — so it doesn't show minimalism is worse for understanding, only that people dislike it; the comprehension counterpart is Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?". (2) A nuance the claim omits: in condition 3, participants accepted moderate minimalism and only rejected Tufte's extreme — a "sweet spot" result, not blanket pro-chartjunk. Source quality (peer-reviewed conference paper) matches the claim's modest strength; not outdated for an aesthetics/preference finding; not marketing.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the finding; the main limitation is internal — the paper measures preference/attitude, not comprehension (cf. Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?", CHI), so the claim should be read as about user acceptance, not effectiveness.

#### V3 `a0922e94` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors explicitly reject a universal 'all charts (or bars) must start at zero' rule; whether truncation is honest or deceptive depends on the effect sizes and task, not a fixed convention."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that...all charts with quantitative axes should include 0."

*Evidence:* Primary source verified verbatim (Correll, Bertini, Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020, DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222; confirmed via ar5iv HTML of arXiv:1907.02035 after ACM 403'd). Exact text: "We resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0." The supporting quote's ellipsis correctly elides only "as Huff suggests," — accurate, not doctored. Both parts of the claim are directly grounded: (a) universal rule rejected — verbatim above, plus "There is no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe...an effect size ought to be"; (b) honest-vs-deceptive depends on effect size/task, not fixed convention — "We reject the unequivocal dichotomy of 'honest' and 'dishonest' charts," and abstract: truncation is "beneficial as well as harmful, depending on the communicative and analytic intent." The parenthetical "(or bars)" is faithful: the authors found the exaggeration effect persisted across chart types and did NOT exempt bars from the no-universal-rule stance. Refutation attempts all failed: no misread (quote exact), no credible contradiction (the paper's cautionary empirical finding that truncation inflates perceived effect coexists with — does not contradict — its explicit refusal to make it a rule; CHI 2024 "To Cut or Not To Cut?" extends rather than overturns), and source quality (peer-reviewed CHI primary source) matches the claim's strength. Caveat for downstream use, not a refutation: the same paper shows truncation reliably exaggerates magnitude, so the claim supports "no fixed start-at-zero rule," not "truncation is harmless"; the claim as worded respects this.
*Counter-source:* No credible source disputes that these authors reject the universal rule. Closest tension is the paper's own empirical finding that truncation persistently exaggerates perceived effect size (even with cues, even for bars) and the broader practitioner convention that bar charts should start at zero — but neither contradicts the claim, which is specifically about the authors' explicit normative position. CHI 2024 "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation" (DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102) extends this work without overturning it.

#### V4 `a0ed8a2e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A controlled empirical study (Borgo et al., IEEE TVCG 2012) found that visual embellishments can improve memory retention of the information depicted in a visualization, directly contradicting a strict reading of Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk minimalism that all non-data ink should be removed."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization"

*Evidence:* PubMed 26357185 resolves exactly to the cited paper: Borgo et al. (2012), "An Empirical Study on Using Visual Embellishments in Visualization," IEEE TVCG 18(12):2759-2768 — so the citation and source link are correct (verified via WebFetch of the PubMed page and corroborated by Hertfordshire/Oxford/Swansea repository records). The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract: "visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization." The study used a dual-task methodology (simulating divided attention) and found embellishments improved memory retention of depicted information — genuinely supporting the memory portion of the claim. Characterizing this as contradicting a strict reading of Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk minimalism (erase all non-data ink) is the standard framing in the visualization literature, where Borgo 2012 is cited alongside Bateman et al. 2010 ("Useful Junk?") as controlled empirical counter-evidence to strict minimalism. The claim is appropriately hedged ("can improve"). One qualifier that does NOT refute: Borgo also found embellishments slowed visual search speed and gave only nuanced comprehension benefits — a trade-off, but the claim is narrowly about memory retention, which is accurately stated. Source quality (peer-reviewed top-tier IEEE TVCG, primary source) matches the claim's strength; the finding is a historical fact, not outdated or overturned, and is not marketing/cherry-picked.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few and other critics have disputed the *generalizability/interpretation* of embellishment-memory studies (e.g., critiquing Bateman 2010's plain-vs-decorated chart comparison as unfair), but no credible source disputes that Borgo 2012 measured a memory-retention benefit; these critiques qualify rather than refute the specific claim.

#### V5 `a16d1456` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The same study found visual embellishments carry a measurable cost: they slow visual search performance, meaning embellishments help some tasks (memory) while hurting others (search speed) rather than being uniformly good or bad."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search"

*Evidence:* PMID 26357185 = Borgo et al. 2012, "An Empirical Study on Using Visual Embellishments in Visualization," IEEE TVCG (Dec 2012) — a top-tier peer-reviewed visualization venue, so source quality matches the claim's strength. The abstract reports BOTH effects the claim describes, in one study: (a) memory benefit — "visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization"; (b) search cost — "visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search." The claim's framing — embellishments help memory while hurting search speed, a task-dependent tradeoff rather than uniformly good/bad — is exactly what the study found (it also reported a "complex pattern" for concept comprehension, consistent with non-uniform effects). The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract. Used a dual-task/divided-attention methodology. No contradicting evidence found; the search-speed penalty is consistent with the data-ink/minimalist view that extra non-data ink adds search burden. Minor nuance: the original hedges with "can have a negative impact," slightly softer than the claim's "carry a measurable cost," but this is faithful paraphrase, not overreach. Claim is supported, source is primary and authoritative, and the 2012 finding remains a standard citation in this literature. Verified via WebFetch of the PubMed page and WebSearch corroboration.

#### V6 `a18b7099` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Y-axis truncation reliably increases readers' perceived effect size/severity of differences, and this bias is statistically robust (Experiment 1, n=40)."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)."

*Evidence:* Primary source verified: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" CHI 2020 (DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222). The arXiv HTML version (1907.02035) reproduces the supporting quote verbatim: "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)." Experiment 1 recruited exactly 40 participants ("We recruited 40 participants for this task (21 male, 18 female, 1 non-binary, M_age=27.7)"), one excluded — hence df=76 = 2×38, fully consistent with the reported statistic. F=89, p<0.0001 is a very large, highly significant effect, and the paper shows it persists across chart types (bars vs lines) and resists break/continuity signaling, supporting "statistically robust." The claim (truncation reliably increases perceived effect size/severity; bias is statistically robust; Exp 1 n=40) is faithfully supported by the quote and the full paper. Caveat that does NOT refute: the paper's "Threat or Menace?" framing argues against rigid prescriptive rules ("always zero"), but that nuance is about the normative recommendation, not the perceptual finding the claim makes — which the paper firmly establishes and does not dispute. No credible contradicting source found.

#### V7 `a19ecff5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The blanket 'bar charts must always start at zero' rule is not supported as universal; the authors recommend truncating the y-axis when the goal is to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size on difference tasks, and only avoid truncation (or use monotonic truncation) for percentage-change tasks."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "if the designer wants to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size in the viewers, our results corroborate existing recommendations [12,31,44] to truncate the y-axis to show the desired effect size. However, for tasks such as comparing or estimating the percentage change between bars... we suggest that the designer perform no truncation or monotonic truncation for these tasks."

*Evidence:* Claim is a faithful, well-sourced paraphrase of a top-tier primary study: Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024 (DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102), MU Collective/Northwestern. Two independent web searches returned near-verbatim statements of the paper's recommendations, matching the supporting quote: (a) for comparing/estimating DIFFERENCES between bars, y-axis truncation (monotonic or not) has little-to-no effect on accuracy, so designers may truncate to "elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size"; (b) for PERCENTAGE-CHANGE tasks, no-truncation performs similarly to monotonic truncation and both are much better than non-monotonic, so use no/monotonic truncation. The paper's stated purpose is explicitly to re-evaluate the blanket "start at zero" guideline as task-dependent, directly supporting the claim's "not universal" framing. Corroborating: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (Best Paper Honorable Mention), also concludes truncation "can be beneficial as well as harmful, depending on the communicative and analytic intent" — i.e., not a universal rule. Caveat (not refuting): the "subjective perception of effect size" lever is the same exaggeration mechanism the deception literature (Pandey 2015; Correll 2020) warns about; Long & Kay reframe it as legitimate when accuracy is unharmed, and the claim correctly scopes its truncation recommendation to that goal. Source quality (peer-reviewed CHI 2024 primary study), recency (2024), and faithful paraphrase all match the claim's strength; no credible source contradicts the attributed recommendations.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 — cautions truncation exaggerates subjective effect size and the effect persists even with truncation cues; but it too rejects a universal rule, so it corroborates rather than refutes the claim.

#### V8 `a1acbc5e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment (20 participants, 14 charts: Nigel Holmes embellished 'chartjunk' versions vs. plain bar/line equivalents), interpretation accuracy for embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts on chart subject, categories, and trend — directly contradicting the minimalist claim that decoration impairs comprehension."

**Source:** https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We found that people’s accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts... There was no significant difference between plain and image charts for interactive interpretation accuracy (i.e., when the charts were visible)."

*Evidence:* CLAIM IS ACCURATE — well-supported by the primary source and corroborated independently. Source: Bateman, Mandryk, Gutwin, Genest, McDine & Brooks (2010), "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," CHI 2010, pp. 2573-2582, DOI 10.1145/1753326.1753716 (top-tier peer-reviewed HCI venue — adequate for the claim's strength).

VERIFIED SPECIFICS: (a) 20 participants — confirmed; (b) 14 charts, within-subjects/counterbalanced so each participant saw all 14, only one version of each (7 embellished, 7 plain) — confirmed; (c) Nigel Holmes embellished versions vs. plain bar/line equivalents — confirmed; (d) comprehension measured via questions on the chart's subject, the categories shown, and the trend/values — confirmed; (e) "no worse" accuracy for embellished vs. plain when charts were visible — confirmed. Multiple independent secondaries restate this: "there was no significant difference in interpretation accuracy between plain and Holmes's graphs," and recall after 2-3 weeks was significantly BETTER for embellished. The supporting quote matches the paper's actual conclusion; the claim is a faithful description, not an overreach of the quote.

QUALIFICATION (refines the generalization, does not refute the core claim): the "no worse" result is bounded to the study's conditions — simple charts with few data points, viewed with no time limit, and relatively shallow comprehension questions. Stephen Few (2011, "The Chartjunk Debate," Perceptual Edge) critiques the study but does NOT dispute that accuracy was not worse in this experiment — he disputes over-generalizing it to justify broadly harmful practices, and notes the "plain" charts were not Tufte-optimized. A follow-up, Li & Moacdieh (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment" (Proc. Human Factors and Ergonomics Society), found embellishment DID impair comprehension/short-term recall with larger data sets (10+ points) and time limits. So the claim correctly contradicts the absolute minimalist claim ("decoration impairs comprehension") within tested conditions, but should not be read as "decoration never harms comprehension." None of the refutation triggers apply: the claim is supported by the quote, drawn from the primary peer-reviewed source, current as a description of a specific historical study, and not a marketing/cherry-picked benchmark.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few (2011), "The Chartjunk Debate," Perceptual Edge — critiques generalization but does not dispute the no-worse-accuracy finding; Li & Moacdieh (2014), HFES — embellishment impaired comprehension with denser data + time limits (bounds generalizability, does not refute the study's reported result)

#### V9 `a1dae9c8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For tasks of comparing/estimating relative growth (percentage change) between bars, truncation that preserves the data's underlying relations (monotonic) performs about as well as no truncation, whereas truncation that does not preserve those relations (non-monotonic) is very likely to impair performance."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "For comparing or estimating the relative growth between two bars, truncating monotonically has similar performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance."

*Evidence:* The claim is a near-verbatim, faithful paraphrase of the source's own conclusion. Paper: Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102 (peer-reviewed, Mu Collective/Northwestern). Verified across three independent confirmations (ACM listing, the project page mucollective.northwestern.edu/project/2024-to_cut_or_not, and the PDF), the paper states: "For comparing or estimating the relative growth between two bars, truncating monotonically has similar performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance." The claim's parenthetical gloss matches the paper's definitions exactly: "truncations that preserve the underlying relations in data [are] monotonic and truncations that do not preserve underlying relations in data [are] non-monotonic." Checklist: (1) quote fully supports claim — no overreach; (2) no contradicting evidence — broader truncation literature (Correll et al. "Threat or Menace?" CHI 2020; Yang et al. 2021 "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads") concerns magnitude/difference exaggeration, a different task that this paper explicitly separates ("for comparing differences, truncation has little to no effect"), so they qualify rather than refute; (3) source quality (primary, CHI) matches claim strength; (4) current (2024, with a 2026 CHI follow-up "Taking Truncation to Task" extending, not contradicting); (5) not marketing. Only minor scope note: the study used grouped bar charts, so generality to all chart types is untested — but the claim stays scoped to bars and does not overgeneralize.

#### V10 `a1deb87d` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled crowdsourced experiment (N=250 for the exaggeration study), a truncated/non-zero y-axis on a bar chart significantly inflated viewers' magnitude judgments: deceptive bar charts drew a mean 'how much bigger' rating of 2.77 (95% CI [2.26,3.28]) vs 1.45 ([1.27,1.62]) for the zero-baseline control, p=0.0003. This is direct empirical support for the convention that bar charts should start at zero."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The treatments that involved bar-charts (affected by the "truncated axis" distortion), the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary-source PDF (Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov, Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015, doi:10.1145/2702123.2702608). I downloaded the paper and extracted its text directly. Every specific figure in the claim checks out:

1) Supporting quote is verbatim: "...the mean ranks of Bar(Control) and Bar(Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003, r=0.37."

2) The headline means 2.77/1.45 are NOT fabricated — they come from the paper's Figure 8 ("Average participant response with 95% CI"). Extracted text: "Bar(Deceptive) Truncated Axis 2.77, 95%CI [2.26,3.28] ... Bar(Control) Truncated Axis 1.45" — matching the claim's 2.77 [2.26,3.28] vs 1.45 exactly. The claim correctly draws the means from Fig 8 and the p-value from the Mann-Whitney rank test; both are genuine parts of the same paper.

3) N=250 is correct: "For our final user study, we recruited 330" total; "we recruited 250 unique participants" for the exaggeration treatments (Fig 7 a,b,c, which include the bar/truncated-axis condition) and "80 unique participants" for the message-reversal study (Fig 7d). 250+80=330.

4) Scale/method confirmed: "(minimum=1, maximum=5) We ran a Mann-Whitney's U test (one-tailed) ... 5-item Likert scale." The claim's "significantly inflated magnitude judgments" matches a directional one-tailed test.

5) "how much bigger" is accurate, not a misparaphrase: the paper's actual question form is "How much do you think Y is bigger than X?"

Interpretation ("direct empirical support for bars-start-at-zero") is sound for bar charts specifically and reinforced by replication (Yang et al. 2021, "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," Cognitive Research). Source is a peer-reviewed CHI 2015 paper — strong primary source matching the claim's strength. Not outdated, not marketing.

Minor caveat (does not refute): the broader y-axis-truncation convention is debated for LINE charts (Correll, Bertini, Franconeri 2020 "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" CHI; "To Cut or Not To Cut?" CHI 2024), but that debate concerns lines/dot plots, not the bar-chart finding asserted here.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI) and "To Cut or Not To Cut?" (CHI 2024) qualify the truncation convention — but mainly for line charts, not bars; they do not contradict the bar-specific result in this claim. No source disputes Pandey et al.'s bar-chart numbers.

#### V11 `a2022f4b` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Gleicher classifies all visual comparison into three design categories — juxtaposition (items placed in separate adjacent spaces, i.e. small multiples), superposition (items overlaid in the same space), and explicit encoding (the relationship itself is drawn) — and states that NEITHER juxtaposition nor superposition naturally scales to many items: overlay becomes cluttered, while small multiples separate items and thereby hinder some kinds of comparison. This contradicts any blanket claim that small multiples are universally superior for comparison."

**Source:** https://graphics.cs.wisc.edu/Papers/2018/Gle18/viscomp.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "neither superposition or juxtaposition designs naturally scale to many items. Superposition becomes cluttered with many objects in the same space. Juxtaposition with many items separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (Gleicher 2018, "Considerations for Visualizing Comparison," IEEE TVCG, graphics.cs.wisc.edu/Papers/2018/Gle18/viscomp.pdf) and corroborated independently. (1) The three-category taxonomy is real and canonical: juxtaposition (objects placed next to each other, relying on viewer memory), superposition (objects overlaid in the same space, leveraging vision but risking occlusion/clutter), explicit encoding (compute the relationship and draw it). Originates in Gleicher, Albers, Walker, Jusufi, Hansen & Roberts 2011, "Visual Comparison for Information Visualization," Information Visualization 10(4); reaffirmed in the 2018 paper. (2) Fetch of the PDF returned the scaling claim: "Neither superposition nor juxtaposition scale to many items without becoming cluttered," matching the supporting quote's substance (superposition→cluttered; juxtaposition→separates items, hindering some comparisons). (3) Independent search confirmed each definition verbatim, including "Explicit encoding designs compute relationships between objects and provide visual encoding of the relationships." No credible source contradicts the taxonomy; it remains cited in 2026 (CHI 2026 "To Slide or Not to Slide"; "Comparative Layouts Revisited"). The inference that this contradicts "small multiples are universally superior for comparison" is sound — Gleicher explicitly notes juxtaposition relies on memory so subtle differences may go unnoticed, the precise weakness that disqualifies a blanket superiority claim. Minor non-refuting imprecision: the claim equates juxtaposition with small multiples via "i.e.," but small multiples is one instance of the broader juxtaposition category; the gloss is reasonable and Gleicher's caveat does apply to small multiples. Source is primary, peer-reviewed, current, and authored by a leading visualization researcher — quality matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The taxonomy is foundational InfoVis and corroborated by multiple independent 2020-2026 papers (e.g., "Comparative Layouts Revisited," arxiv.org/pdf/2009.00192; CHI 2026 "To Slide or Not to Slide"). Only nuance: juxtaposition is broader than small multiples, but that does not refute the claim.

#### V12 `a20612c8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "After a 2-3 week delay, recall of both chart topic and details (categories and trend) was significantly better for the embellished Holmes charts than for plain charts (long-term group: subject t9=2.56 p=.015; categories t9=5.03 p≈.000; trend t9=1.95 p=.042; value message t9=2.41 p=.020)."

**Source:** https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "After a long-term gap (2-3 weeks), recall of both the chart topic and the details (categories and trend) was significantly better for Holmes charts."

*Evidence:* All four statistics in the claim verify against the primary source (Bateman et al. 2010, "Useful Junk?", CHI '10, DOI 10.1145/1753326.1753716). WebFetch could not extract the PDF body (CID-encoded fonts), but search engines reading the actual text at both vis.csail.mit.edu and the Columbia/Gelman mirror (sites.stat.columbia.edu/gelman/communication/Bateman2010.pdf) returned, for the long-term recall group: subject t9=2.56 p=.015; categories t9=5.03 (consistent with claim's p≈.000 — t=5.03 at df=9 yields p<.001); trend t9=1.95 p=.042; value message t9=2.41 p=.020. Direction and scoping are correct: embellished (Holmes) charts were recalled significantly BETTER than plain charts in the long-term (2-3 week) group, while the immediate-recall group showed no significant difference — and the claim correctly attributes these to the "long-term group." The claim is a faithful restatement of the paper's reported figures, not an overreach of the supporting quote. The Stephen Few critique ("The Chartjunk Debate," Perceptual Edge, 2011) disputes interpretation/generalizability (n=10 per group, single Holmes chart, prompted/cued recall, possibly weakened plain comparators) but does NOT dispute that these statistics were reported, so it doesn't refute a claim scoped to what the study found. Caveat: p-values are likely directional and rest on n=10/group; cite as "the study's reported finding," not as settled proof embellishment aids memory generally.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate," Perceptual Edge, 2011 (https://www.perceptualedge.com/articles/visual_business_intelligence/the_chartjunk_debate.pdf) — critiques interpretation/generalizability but does not contradict the reported statistics.

#### V13 `a2099ee6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled study of grouped bar charts, for tasks that involve comparing or estimating the absolute difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis (not starting at zero) does NOT measurably degrade task performance/accuracy."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported, current, and primary-sourced. I extracted the actual PDF of the cited paper (Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102, Northwestern MU Collective). Its abstract states verbatim: "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance" — exactly the supporting quote, with no overreach. The results section backs this with statistics: for the difference task, the no-truncation-vs-truncation contrast in absolute error has mean -0.53, 95% CI [-1.25, 0.05] (null). The study is a controlled, IRB-approved, preregistered (OSF supplementary) Prolific experiment on grouped/paired bar charts — source quality matches claim strength. The claim is correctly bounded: the SAME paper finds truncation IS harmful for other tasks (non-monotonic truncation on relative-growth/ratio tasks "is very likely to impair performance"), and the claim under review does not extend there. It is also current and corroborated: a 2026 CHI follow-up ("Taking Truncation to Task," DOI 10.1145/3772318.3790617) replicates the task-dependence (truncation raises error for ratio tasks but improves accuracy/speed for value-retrieval and filtering). The classic critiques (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020; Pandey et al. 2015) measure a DIFFERENT outcome — exaggeration of perceived/subjective effect size and deception — not objective accuracy on a difference-estimation task, so they qualify the broader "truncation is fine" message but do not refute this specific, narrowly-scoped accuracy claim.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020 (CHI, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222) and Pandey et al. 2015 show truncation exaggerates PERCEIVED effect size / subjective magnitude (a bias/impression measure), persistent even with visual cues — but this is a different dependent variable than objective difference-estimation accuracy and does NOT contradict the claim. The claim would be an overreach only if generalized to ratio/relative-growth tasks or to perceived-magnitude/deception, which it does not.

#### V16 `a3711481` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The misleading effect persists even after viewers are explicitly taught/warned about y-axis truncation immediately before judging the graphs; the warning reduced but did not eliminate the effect."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S2211368120300978 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The results of Study 2 were surprising in that an explicit warning did not eliminate the truncation effect."

*Evidence:* Claim is directly supported by the primary source. Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marsh (2021), "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," Journal of Applied Research in Memory and Cognition (Elsevier, DOI 10.1016/j.jarmac.2020.10.002 — the cited S2211368120300978). I extracted the paper's own PDF text, which states verbatim: (1) "The results of Study 2 were surprising in that an explicit warning did not eliminate the truncation effect"; (2) "the explicit warning immediately preceding judgments of graphs was ineffective at erasing the truncation effect" — confirming the "immediately before judging" element; (3) Study 3: "providing an explanatory warning before participants rated control and truncated bar graphs reduced but did not eliminate the truncation effect"; (4) General Discussion: "while educational training and explicit warnings may reduce the size of the truncation effect, they do not eliminate it"; and (5) "Teaching people about how truncation can mislead attenuated the truncation effect somewhat, both immediately and after a 1-day delay, but failed to eliminate the effect." The effect persisted across all 5 studies (~83.5% of participants showed it), including a 1-day delay condition and among quantitative PhD students. The supporting quote is accurate, not cherry-picked. Source quality matches claim strength (peer-reviewed, multi-study replication). The finding also parallels the established misinformation-correction literature (Ecker, Lewandowsky & Tang 2010, cited in the same paper: "Explicit warnings reduce but do not eliminate the continued influence of misinformation"). No credible contradicting source found. The only minor nuance: in Study 2 the warning's reduction may not have been statistically significant (which is why Study 3 directly manipulated it), but the overall "reduced but did not eliminate" conclusion is the paper's own stated finding.

**Supporting quote:** "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."

Table 2 reports verbatim: "Bubble (Deceptive) Area as Quantity 2.71, 95% CI [2.27, 3.15]" and "Bubble (Control) Area as Quantity 1.71, 95% CI [1.45, 1.98]" — exact match. The paper specifies the dependent measure is a "5-item Likert scale... (minimum = 1, maximum = 5)," so 2.71 vs 1.71 are genuine rating means on a 1-5 scale; the claim correctly labels these as "mean rating" and does NOT conflate them with the Mann-Whitney mean ranks (32.47/48.52). Direction confirmed: control maps quantity to area (faithful), deceptive uses radius/linear encoding so area scales ~quadratically; deceptive rating (2.71) exceeds control (1.71) = exaggerated perceived magnitude. Paper text: "When the quantity is mapped to the area, as in the control condition, the visualization is less susceptible to deception." Source is top-tier peer-reviewed HCI venue, n=330, primary. Numbers and direction are precisely accurate; claim well-supported.

The only qualifications are scope/generalizability (single crowdsourced 1-5 Likert task, medium effect r=0.34) and that the Tufte attribution is interpretive (paper does not cite Tufte/Lie Factor). (2) No overreach on the DV: I checked whether "perceived severity" means "how misleading" rather than "exaggeration." The paper defines it as subjective effect-size magnitude — participant prompts were "how different is the first value compared to the last value?" / "how quickly are the values changing?" — so the claim's "exaggeration effect" maps directly to the measure. (3) Authors' own conclusion matches the claim: "neither intervention had a consistent impact on perceived severity; increased axis truncation resulted in similar increases in perceived severity," and "we cannot rely on visual indicators of broken or truncated axes to counteract the exaggeration caused by y-axis truncation." No source found showing break marks restore honest perception. Caveat (does not refute): F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05 sits exactly at the significance boundary (critical F≈3.15), so it is a borderline-null rather than a decisively null result, but the authors interpreted it as non-significant and replications support it. The "contradicts popular belief that a break mark makes truncation honest" framing is editorial but consistent with the paper's thesis.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. #### V22 `a53b0c91` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors explicitly reject a universal 'all charts (or bars) must start at zero' rule; whether truncation is honest or deceptive depends on the effect sizes and task, not a fixed convention."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that...all charts with quantitative axes should include 0."

*Evidence:* Claim accurately reflects Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020, honorable mention). Verbatim source sentence confirmed via search: "However, we resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0." The lead author's own summary reinforces rejection of a fixed rule: "not questions for which a one-size-fits-all rule is going to help you... Truncate your axis or don't, but don't fall back on defaults unthinkingly." Part 2 ("depends on effect sizes and task") is a fair paraphrase: the paper's mechanism is perceived effect size, with appropriateness contingent on domain/audience/communicative-vs-analytic intent. Corroborated, not contradicted, by the 2024 follow-up Long & Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation" (CHI 2024), which found truncation's effect is task-dependent (e.g., bar difference/percentage-change judgments not harmed by truncation). Only soft spot is the "(or bars)" parenthetical — the 2020 paper does treat bars as more sensitive and a design convention favors zero-baseline bars — but the authors still decline an absolute universal rule, so it is not a misread. Primary peer-reviewed source, quote verbatim-confirmed, current.
*Counter-source:* Design-convention sources (Datawrapper Academy "Why our column and bar charts start at zero"; Observable "It's never okay to crop the y-axis, except when it is") argue bars should start at zero — but these are conventions, not refutations of what the authors concluded, and they too admit exceptions.

#### V23 `a5509b90` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Gleicher classifies all visual comparison into three design categories — juxtaposition (items placed in separate adjacent spaces, i.e. small multiples), superposition (items overlaid in the same space), and explicit encoding (the relationship itself is drawn) — and states that NEITHER juxtaposition nor superposition naturally scales to many items: overlay becomes cluttered, while small multiples separate items and thereby hinder some kinds of comparison. This contradicts any blanket claim that small multiples are universally superior for comparison."

**Source:** https://graphics.cs.wisc.edu/Papers/2018/Gle18/viscomp.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "neither superposition or juxtaposition designs naturally scale to many items. Superposition becomes cluttered with many objects in the same space. Juxtaposition with many items separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary PDF (extracted text directly from graphics.cs.wisc.edu/Papers/2018/Gle18/viscomp.pdf). Both load-bearing passages match verbatim.

(1) Taxonomy — Section 6 "Which Visual Design?" reads: "In a prior paper [37], we posited that there are three basic designs for visual comparisons (Fig. 2): 1. Juxtaposition: items placed in different spaces (next to each other). 2. Superposition: items placed in the same space (on top of each other). This is sometimes called superimposed. 3. Explicit Encoding: the relationships are visualized." This exactly matches the claim's three categories and their definitions.

(2) Scaling passage — verbatim in the paper: "neither superposition or juxtaposition designs naturally scale to many items. Superposition becomes cluttered with many objects in the same space. Juxtaposition with many items separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison." This matches the supporting quote and the claim's paraphrase. The claim's mapping (overlay=superposition, small multiples=a juxtaposition design) is correct — small multiples are the canonical juxtaposition form in Gleicher's framework.

(3) The interpretive conclusion ("contradicts any blanket claim that small multiples are universally superior") is faithful: Gleicher explicitly assigns juxtaposition a documented downside (hinders some comparisons; doesn't scale to many items), and the paper's whole thesis is that design choice depends on the comparison challenge — i.e., no design is universally best.

SOURCE QUALITY: High. Primary peer-reviewed source (Gleicher, "Considerations for Visualizing Comparison," IEEE TVCG/VIS 2018); the taxonomy itself originates in the also-peer-reviewed Gleicher, Albers, Walker, Jusufi, Hansen & Roberts, "Visual Comparison for Information Visualization," Information Visualization 10(4):289–309 (2011), 600+ citations — a foundational, still-cited framework. Not outdated, not marketing. WebSearch surfaced only corroboration (e.g., Comparative Layouts Revisited, 2020) and no source disputing the scalability tradeoff.

MINOR CAVEATS (do not refute): (a) The taxonomy originates in Gleicher et al. 2011 and is restated/cited by the 2018 paper ("In a prior paper [37]"), so the 2018 PDF is a valid carrier but not the original. (b) The claim omits the paper's qualifier "although juxtaposition may naturally support scanning," which gives juxtaposition a partial edge — but this strengthens, not weakens, the "neither is universally superior" point. (c) "i.e. small multiples" slightly narrows juxtaposition (which also covers sequential/animated side-by-side), but is a correct exemplar. None of these undermine the claim.

#### V24 `a55ce427` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Adding explicit visual cues that signal truncation (broken-axis marks, gradient fade at the bottom) does NOT meaningfully reduce the exaggeration effect — truncated charts with break indicators were perceived about the same as plain truncated charts. This contradicts the popular belief that a break mark makes a truncated axis 'honest.'"

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "There was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05)."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against primary source. Paper: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222; arXiv 1907.02035; peer-reviewed, top HCI venue). Exact quote confirmed in context (ar5iv HTML of the arXiv source): "Our results fail to support our first hypothesis: there was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05)." Crucially, the three designs in that ANOVA are exactly the ones the claim names: (1) standard truncated bar chart, (2) bar chart with a BROKEN AXIS (breaks in axis and bars), (3) bar chart with a GRADIENT bottom. A post-hoc pairwise t-test with Bonferroni correction "failed to find any significant difference between visualization designs." Authors' stated conclusion: "the subjective impact of axis truncation is persistent across visualizations designs, even for designs with explicit visual cues that indicate truncation has taken place." Their own blog restates it: "neither intervention had a consistent impact on perceived severity... Just indicating that something screwy is going on with the y-axis was not sufficient to remove the impact of the truncation." The claim accurately summarizes this and matches the authors' own framing (i.e., it contradicts the popular belief that a break mark makes a truncated axis 'honest'). One honest nuance: p=0.05 is borderline rather than a strong null, but the null post-hoc Bonferroni test plus the authors' explicit conclusion support the summary, so it is not an overreach. No credible contradicting study found showing break marks demonstrably remove the exaggeration effect; this CHI 2020 paper is the canonical empirical test of the question.

(2018), "When cognitive fit outweighs cognitive load: Redundant data labels in charts increase accuracy and speed of information extraction," Computers in Human Behavior, Vol. 86, pp. 367-376 (Elsevier, established journal). The paper's title and abstract literally state the claimed finding: charts with redundant data labels were answered "significantly more accurate and faster" and perceived as "significantly more efficient," explained by cognitive fit theory outweighing cognitive load. All three planks of the claim (accuracy, speed, perceived efficiency) map to the abstract. It is current (2018, perception research, not fast-moving) and not marketing. No credible source refutes this specific result; the opposing minimalism/cognitive-load prior (Few, Tufte) is the very framing the paper tests against and finds outweighed.
*Counter-source:* No direct refutation found. The general data-ink minimalism / cognitive load view (Stephen Few; Tufte) is the opposing prior, but the paper explicitly tests against it and finds cognitive fit outweighs cognitive load for label-relevant tasks; it does not dispute this result. Only caveat: effect is task-type dependent, not universal.
