#### V27 `a594cbdc` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper summarizes the empirical state of the chartjunk debate by asserting that controlled studies have shown extreme minimalism (maximizing Tufte's data-ink ratio) is not always optimal and that visual embellishments can be useful depending on context — directly contradicting a strict reading of Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk prescription."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/2009.02634 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Previous studies have shown that extreme minimalism is not always best, and that visual embellishments can be useful depending on the context."

*Evidence:* Quote verified verbatim in the source: arXiv 2009.02634 is Parsons & Shukla, "Data Visualization Practitioners' Perspectives on Chartjunk," a peer-reviewed IEEE VIS 2020 short paper. The abstract contains exactly: "Previous studies have shown that extreme minimalism is not always best, and that visual embellishments can be useful depending on the context." The claim is a faithful paraphrase, not an overreach: "extreme minimalism" = maximizing Tufte's data-ink ratio, so "not always best" does contradict a strict data-ink/chartjunk reading. The interpretive addition is accurate.

The underlying empirical reality is independently corroborated by the primary controlled studies the statement summarizes. Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts" (ACM CHI) found embellished (Holmes-style) charts had comprehension accuracy no worse than plain charts and significantly better recall after a 2-3 week gap, explicitly "questioning some of the premises of the minimalist approach." Borgo et al. 2012 (IEEE TVCG) is a second controlled study cited in this debate. So "controlled studies have shown" is accurate.

The claim is appropriately hedged ("not always optimal," "depending on context") — which is precisely the nuanced position the evidence supports, and it absorbs the known critiques (Stephen Few's rebuttal of Bateman; Li & Moacdieh 2014 "Is chart junk useful?" found embellishment can hurt in some task conditions). Those critiques sharpen the "depending on context" caveat rather than refute the claim. Source is peer-reviewed and current (2020/2010, still foundational); not marketing or cherry-picked. Minor caveat: the source is technically a qualitative interview study, and this specific sentence is a literature-summary statement, not its own primary finding — but the claim correctly frames it as "the paper summarizes... by asserting that controlled studies have shown," so this is not a mischaracterization.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few critique of Bateman; Li & Moacdieh 2014 "Is chart junk useful?" (HFES) — both qualify but do not refute; they reinforce the context-dependence the claim already states.

The claim's supporting quote is reproduced verbatim by two independent searches: "Techniques that create separate charts for each time series—such as small multiples and horizon graphs—are generally more efficient for comparisons across time series with a large visual span. Shared-space techniques—like standard line graphs—are typically more efficient for comparisons over smaller visual spans where the impact of overlap and clutter is reduced." The quote fully supports the claim, including the careful "conditional, task-dependent, not 'small multiples always win'" framing. No contradicting source found; later work (e.g., "Progressive Horizon Graphs") builds on rather than refutes it. Minor non-fatal imprecision: it was a 4-technique study, not a strict 2-way head-to-head, but small multiples and line graphs were both among the conditions, so "tested against" holds. The paper's own title asserts the conclusion, and the abstract (confirmed via ScienceDirect and ResearchGate listings) states a controlled online experiment on line and bar charts found that users with redundant data labels "answer related questions significantly more accurate and faster" and "perceive charts with redundant labels as significantly more efficient," explained by cognitive fit theory. Mapping "information extraction / answering chart questions" to "comprehension" is reasonable. No credible refuting source found.

#### V30 `a64b73d8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Encoding a quantity by a circle's radius instead of its area (so the value scales with area roughly quadratically) significantly exaggerated perceived magnitude: deceptive bubble charts drew a mean rating of 2.71 ([2.27,3.15]) vs 1.71 ([1.45,1.98]) for the area-faithful control, p=0.0007, r=0.34. This empirically confirms Tufte's area/graphical-integrity principle."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."

*Evidence:* Every figure in the claim is verified against the primary source — Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov & Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015 (DOI 10.1145/2702123.2702608), read from the full PDF (cs.rpi.edu mirror). (1) The supporting quote's mean ranks are exact: "the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34." (2) The mean ratings the claim adds — and which were NOT in the supplied quote — I independently confirmed in the paper's Table 2: "Bubble (Deceptive) … 2.71, 95% CI [2.27, 3.15]" and "Bubble (Control) … 1.71, 95% CI [1.45, 1.98]" (Average participant response to the 5-point "how much" question). Both match. (3) The mechanism matches exactly: the paper defines the deceptive condition as "the quantity is mapped to the radius of the circle" and the control as "the quantity is mapped to the area," confirming the claim's "radius instead of its area." (4) Direction is correct: deceptive (2.71 / rank 48.52) > control (1.71 / rank 32.47) = perceived exaggeration; the paper states participants "perceived the underlying message in its exaggerated form." (5) The Tufte link is the paper's own framing, not the claimant's: "This is also one of the six graphical integrity principles suggested by Tufte [38]." Source quality matches claim strength (peer-reviewed ACM CHI primary study). Skeptical probes that did not refute: the U test is one-tailed (two-tailed p still ~0.0014, highly significant); r=0.34 is a medium effect, but the claim's "significantly" correctly refers to p=0.0007; n is modest (40+40 per Table 1, a crowdsourced sample) but does not contradict the result. Minor caveat: "empirically confirms" is marginally strong for a single medium-effect study, but it is a direct, significant, correctly-directed test of precisely that principle.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Web searches surfaced only corroborating discussions of the same Pandey et al. 2015 result; the limitations (one-tailed test, medium effect r=0.34, single crowdsourced study n≈80) qualify but do not contradict the claim.

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)

The discussion section states verbatim: "When asked to compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5, the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition. Out of the three charts that use a message exaggeration/understatement technique, the line chart is the one with the biggest effect, followed by the bars and then bubble." The results section gives Mann-Whitney tests for exactly these three: aspect-ratio/Line U=1409, Z=5.88, p<0.0001, r=0.66; area/Bubble U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34; truncated-axis/Bar U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003, r=0.37. The claim's three techniques (truncated axis, area-as-quantity, aspect ratio) map exactly to the paper's three exaggeration/understatement charts (bar, bubble, line); the 58.5%-129.5% range is a direct quote spanning those three; and "all three significant at p<0.001" is correct since 0.0001, 0.0007, and 0.0003 are each below 0.001. Source is a top-tier peer-reviewed HCI venue (primary), matching the claim's strength. Only minor nuance: bubble and bar are medium effect sizes (r=0.34, 0.37) while only line is large (r=0.66), but the paper's own discussion calls the effects "rather large," so the claim's "large, not marginal" framing is faithful to the authors. No overreach, no contradiction, not outdated for a fixed historical empirical result, not marketing.

#### V35 `a74b8a93` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment (20 participants, 14 charts: Nigel Holmes embellished 'chartjunk' versions vs. plain bar/line equivalents), interpretation accuracy for embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts on chart subject, categories, and trend — directly contradicting the minimalist claim that decoration impairs comprehension."

**Source:** https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We found that people’s accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts... There was no significant difference between plain and image charts for interactive interpretation accuracy (i.e., when the charts were visible)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source and corroborated independently. Bateman, Mandryk, Gutwin, Genest, McDine & Brooks, "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," CHI 2010 (Proc. SIGCHI, ACM, doi:10.1145/1753326.1753716). Method specifics confirmed: 20 participants and 14 charts with two versions each (embellished vs plain) — a secondary summary quoting the paper states "There were 20 University Students" and "14 charts were used... Each one had two versions." Embellishments were Nigel Holmes's cartoon-style charts vs stripped plain bar/line equivalents (confirmed across multiple sources; paper "intentionally chose the most extreme type of visual embellishment — the full cartoon imagery used by Holmes"). The headline finding matches the supporting quote and the abstract: "people's accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts" and "no significant difference... for interactive interpretation accuracy (i.e., when the charts were visible)." The claim is correctly scoped to "in a controlled experiment" and reports the within-study result, not a universal law, so it is not an overreach. Note for the skill (does NOT refute the claim, but bounds it): credible critiques exist — Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (Perceptual Edge, 2011) and Andrew Gelman (statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu, 2010) — but they dispute (a) generalizability (Holmes is a master designer whose embellishments encoded the message; plain comparison charts were context-stripped) and (b) the stronger long-term memorability claim, not the narrow interactive-accuracy finding asserted here. Subsequent work (Li & Moacdieh 2014, "Is chartjunk useful? An extended examination") qualifies it under higher data density/dual-task load but does not overturn Bateman's visible-accuracy result. Verdict: the specific claim is accurate, primary-sourced, and current; label it as a single robust CHI study that complicates the minimalist "decoration impairs comprehension" claim, paired with the generalizability caveat.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (Perceptual Edge, 2011) and Andrew Gelman (statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu, 2010-05-17) — both qualify generalizability and the memorability claim, but neither contradicts the narrow within-study finding that interactive interpretation accuracy was no worse.

#### V36 `a78def39` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "This is a controlled experiment (IEEE TVCG 2010) that directly tested small multiples against a single shared-space line graph for time-series comparison, finding that split-charts-per-series techniques (small multiples, horizon graphs) are more efficient than shared-space line graphs when comparing series across a LARGE visual span, while shared-space line graphs win for comparisons over a SMALL visual span. This is conditional, task-dependent evidence — not a blanket 'small multiples always win.'"

**Source:** http://users.umiacs.umd.edu/~elm/projects/multilinevis/multilinevis.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Techniques that create separate charts for each time series—such as small multiples and horizon graphs—are generally more efficient for comparisons across time series with a large visual span. On the other hand, shared-space techniques—like standard line graphs—are typically more efficient for comparisons over smaller visual spans where the impact of overlap and clutter is reduced."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source and independently corroborated. Source: Javed, McDonnel & Elmqvist, "Graphical Perception of Multiple Time Series," IEEE TVCG vol. 16, pp. 927-934, 2010 (InfoVis 2010) — confirmed via Semantic Scholar, IEEE Xplore, and ACM DL (DOI 10.1109/TVCG.2010.162). It was a genuine controlled human-subjects experiment testing comparison, slope, and discrimination tasks across line graphs, braided graphs, small multiples, and horizon graphs. The supporting quote directly states both conditional halves, and an INDEPENDENT Semantic Scholar/IEEE summary corroborates: "Shared-space techniques excelled in local comparison tasks, while split-space techniques performed better in dispersed tasks... high numbers of concurrent time series increased completion time and decreased user accuracy." This matches the claim's directional finding: split-space (small multiples/horizon) win for LARGE visual span; shared-space (line graphs) win for SMALL visual span. The claim's "conditional, task-dependent — not blanket 'small multiples always win'" framing IS the paper's actual conclusion. No contradicting source found. Note: the original umiacs PDF URL returned 404, but the paper mirrors at engineering.purdue.edu/~elm/projects/multilinevis/multilinevis.pdf and is broadly indexed. Minor wording simplification ("tested small multiples against a single line graph" — actually a multi-technique factorial design) does not undermine substance, since both techniques were tested conditions and the directional result holds.

#### V37 `a795488a` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The zero-baseline rule the study empirically validates applies specifically to BAR graphs; for line graphs and dot plots, which do not encode value by length/area, truncation may be appropriate and a zero baseline may not be necessary."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S2211368120300978 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "our recommendation to not truncate vertical axes is specific to bar graphs. Line graphs and dot plots, for example, do not represent numerical values as continuous visual areas and truncation may be appropriate in these cases (Cleveland, 1994; Zoss, 2016, p. 42)."

*Evidence:* The claim accurately paraphrases the Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marsh (2021) quote (JARMAC 10(2):298-311, ScienceDirect S2211368120300978), but that quote is the weak part of the source: it is a non-empirical scoping caveat citing Cleveland (1994, textbook) and Zoss (2016, guide), NOT a finding of their study — Yang et al. tested only bar graphs (5 studies, 83.5% truncation effect). The substantive assertion that line-graph truncation 'may be appropriate' because they 'do not encode value by length/area' is directly contradicted by the strongest directly-relevant controlled study: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri (2020), 'Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?', CHI 2020. That study ran crowd-sourced experiments comparing bar AND line charts and found NO difference — viewers misread truncated line charts just as much as bar charts, 'even when there are prominent indicators that the vertical axis has been truncated.' Confirmed by two independent sources (the paper summary and Observable's analysis, which quotes 'there really is no difference between bar and line charts'). PART A of the claim (the empirically-validated rule is bar-specific) is correct; PART B (the line/dot-plot exemption) is presented as evidence-grounded but is an untested convention contradicted by controlled data. For an evidence-based skill, 'line charts don't need a zero baseline' is contested CONVENTION (Cleveland's taste; Gelman's 'if zero is in the neighborhood, invite it in' heuristic), not a validated exemption.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri (2020), "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020, doi:10.1145/3313831.3376222 — found no difference between bar and line charts under y-axis truncation; both mislead viewers. Corroborated by observablehq.com/blog/never-okay-crop-y-axis-except-when-it-is.

*Claim under review:* "Y-axis truncation reliably increases readers' perceived effect size/severity of differences, and this bias is statistically robust (Experiment 1, n=40)."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, not refuted. Source = Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", Proc. CHI 2020 (ACM 10.1145/3313831.3376222; arXiv:1907.02035) — a primary, peer-reviewed paper at the top HCI venue, the correct source class for this claim. (1) Quote support: the supporting quote is verbatim from the paper — "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)" — and directly states the claimed direction and robustness. (2) Sample size: Experiment 1 recruited n=40 (21M/18F/1 non-binary, mean age 27.7), crowd-sourced on Prolific; the df F(2,76) implies 39 in the repeated-measures model, consistent with 40 recruited minus a likely single exclusion — so "n=40" is accurate. (3) Direction/robustness corroborated by the lead author's own write-up: higher y-axis start → bigger perceived difference, effect "remarkably robust," holds for BOTH bar and line charts, and PERSISTS even with explicit visual cues/warnings that truncation occurred. F=89 with p<0.0001 is a very large effect, supporting "statistically robust." (4) No contradicting evidence: consistent with Pandey et al. 2015 ("How Deceptive Are Deceptive Visualizations?") and the 2024 CHI follow-up "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-axis Truncation," which replicates the magnitude-exaggeration finding. Current (2020), not marketing. One honest nuance that does NOT refute: the paper's title/stance pushes back on the *normative* dogma that truncation is always deceptive and that line charts are exempt — but that concerns interpretation, not the empirical fact the claim asserts, which is fully supported.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The lead author's own Medium summary (mcorrell.medium.com) and the 2024 CHI follow-up "To Cut or Not To Cut?" (Long et al., 10.1145/3613904.3642102) both corroborate rather than contradict. The paper's "Threat or Menace?" framing qualifies the normative claim (is truncation always bad?), not the empirical effect (does truncation increase perceived severity?), so it does not undercut the claim as stated.

#### V39 `a7f8bdc1` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Across all three magnitude-distortion techniques (truncated axis, area-as-quantity, aspect ratio), deceptive charts inflated viewers' comparative magnitude responses by 58.5% to 129.5% relative to the undistorted control, with all three differences significant at p<0.001 — quantifying that these classic 'graphical integrity' violations produce large, not marginal, message-level deception."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "When asked to compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5, the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary source by directly decoding the paper's PDF (Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov, Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015, DOI 10.1145/2702123.2702608). Every component checks out: (1) The 58.5%-129.5% figure is verbatim — "the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition," on a 1-to-5 (5-item Likert) comparison question. (2) The three techniques match exactly — "Truncated Axis (bar chart), Area as Quantity (bubble chart), and Aspect Ratio (line chart)"; the fourth technique (Inverted Axis) was for message reversal and is correctly excluded by the claim. (3) The p<0.001 sub-claim (which is NOT in the supporting quote, so I checked it independently) is directly stated — "highly significant (p<0.001) for control/deceptive conditions across all the distortion types" — and confirmed by each individual Mann-Whitney U test: aspect ratio p<0.0001 (r=0.66), area-as-quantity p=0.0007 (r=0.34), truncated axis p=0.0003 (r=0.37); all three are below 0.001. (4) The "large, not marginal" gloss mirrors the paper's own "the effects are also rather large" and its term "message level deception." Study: 250 MTurk participants, ~41/cell, between-subjects. No source contradicts the reported numbers; later debate (Correll et al. 2020 on y-axis truncation) concerns the normative question of whether truncation is always wrong, which the claim does not assert. Source quality (peer-reviewed CHI primary) matches claim strength; finding is stable, not outdated.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020) qualifies the normative interpretation of axis truncation but does not dispute Pandey et al.'s reported effect magnitudes; it does not refute this claim.

#### V40 `a8869bc7` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The blanket 'bar charts must always start at zero' rule is not supported as universal; the authors recommend truncating the y-axis when the goal is to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size on difference tasks, and only avoid truncation (or use monotonic truncation) for percentage-change tasks."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "if the designer wants to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size in the viewers, our results corroborate existing recommendations [12,31,44] to truncate the y-axis to show the desired effect size. However, for tasks such as comparing or estimating the percentage change between bars... we suggest that the designer perform no truncation or monotonic truncation for these tasks."

*Evidence:* Verified verbatim against the primary source's conclusion (author PDF, mucollective.northwestern.edu/files/2024-to_cut_or_not.pdf), which I decompressed locally since the ACM page 403'd. The paper = "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," Sheng Long et al., Proc. CHI 2024 (peer-reviewed, Mu Collective / Northwestern; Hullman/Kay group). Its conclusion states verbatim: for difference tasks "if the designer wants to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size in the viewers, our results corroborate existing recommendations [12,31,44] to truncate the y-axis to show the desired effect size. However, for tasks such as comparing or estimating the percentage change between bars, no truncation... performs similarly to monotonic truncation, and both perform much better than non-monotonic truncation. Thus, we suggest that the designer perform no truncation or monotonic truncation for these tasks." Both branches of the claim map exactly: (a) truncate for effect-size perception on difference tasks; (b) no/monotonic truncation for percentage-change tasks. The 'not a universal rule' framing is the paper's central task-dependent thesis and aligns with Correll/Bertini/Franconeri 2020 ("Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?"). The claim correctly attributes the recommendation to "the authors," keeps the conditional ("when the goal is to elicit"), and properly scopes the avoid-truncation branch to percentage-change tasks. No overreach, no contradicting credible source, current (2024), not marketing.

#### V41 `a89038c1` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Gleicher classifies all visual comparison into three design categories — juxtaposition (items placed in separate adjacent spaces, i.e. small multiples), superposition (items overlaid in the same space), and explicit encoding (the relationship itself is drawn) — and states that NEITHER juxtaposition nor superposition naturally scales to many items: overlay becomes cluttered, while small multiples separate items and thereby hinder some kinds of comparison. This contradicts any blanket claim that small multiples are universally superior for comparison."

**Source:** https://graphics.cs.wisc.edu/Papers/2018/Gle18/viscomp.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "neither superposition or juxtaposition designs naturally scale to many items. Superposition becomes cluttered with many objects in the same space. Juxtaposition with many items separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary PDF (Michael Gleicher, "Considerations for Visualizing Comparison," IEEE TVCG vol. 24 no. 1, 2018; presented at IEEE VIS 2017). I extracted the verbatim text from the file by decoding its CID-font ToUnicode CMaps.

(1) Three-category taxonomy — VERBATIM: "visual designs for comparison fall into three categories: juxtaposition, superposition, and explicit encoding." Matches the claim exactly (juxtaposition / superposition / explicit encoding). This taxonomy originates in Gleicher et al., "Visual comparison for information visualization," Information Visualization 10(4):289-309, 2011, and is foundational/widely cited.

(2) Scaling claim — VERBATIM: "...neither superposition or juxtaposition designs naturally scale to many items. Superposition becomes cluttered with many objects in the same space. Juxtaposition with many items separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison." This is an EXACT match to the supplied supporting quote, including the "neither ... naturally scale to many items," "Superposition becomes cluttered," and "Juxtaposition ... separates them, hindering some kinds of comparison" elements.

(3) The interpretive conclusion ("contradicts any blanket claim that small multiples are universally superior") is sound: small multiples is precisely the many-items juxtaposition case the source names as hindering some comparisons. The whole paper's thesis is that comparison design is a set of tradeoffs with no universally-best design. Independent corroboration via WebSearch (the same paper plus 2020 ACM "Comparative Layouts Revisited" and a 2026 CHI paper) confirms the taxonomy and the scaling tradeoffs (e.g., "Superposition designs ... require careful visual design to provide for scaling").

Source quality matches claim strength: peer-reviewed IEEE TVCG paper by the taxonomy's originator; current and still actively built upon in 2026; not marketing/forum/cherry-picked.

ONE MINOR IMPRECISION (does not refute): the claim's "juxtaposition (... i.e. small multiples)" slightly over-equates the two — juxtaposition is the broad family (any side-by-side/multi-view layout) and small multiples is its many-items instance. This strengthens rather than weakens the claim, since small multiples is exactly the case Gleicher flags as hindering some comparisons. Note also the source's very next sentence adds nuance the claim omits: "although juxtaposition may naturally support scanning" — i.e., it is a tradeoff, consistent with the claim's anti-blanket point.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Independent sources (ACM "Comparative Layouts Revisited," 2020; CHI 2026 "To Slide or Not to Slide") reaffirm rather than dispute the taxonomy and its scaling tradeoffs.

#### V42 `a89578eb` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Supporting quote:** "a clear preference of non-minimalist bar-graphs, suggesting low acceptance of minimalist design principles such as high data-ink ratio."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully restates the primary source's own stated conclusion. Inbar, Tractinsky & Meyer (2007), "Minimalism in information visualization: attitudes towards maximizing the data-ink ratio," ECCE 2007, pp. 185-188 (87 students, 3 conditions, comparing a standard bar-graph vs. a Tufte-style minimalist version of the same data). The abstract states: "The results indicate a clear preference of non-minimalist bar-graphs, suggesting low acceptance of minimalist design principles such as high data-ink ratio." The claim's two parts — (a) clear preference for non-minimalist graphs, (b) authors interpret this as low acceptance of the high-data-ink-ratio principle — both map directly onto that text. Two independent web searches returned the same abstract/findings, none contradicting it. Source quality matches: this IS the primary study, not a secondary summary. Minor and non-fatal caveats: (1) the claim generalizes "bar-graphs" to "graphs," but the authors themselves generalize to "minimalist design principles such as high data-ink ratio"; (2) condition 3 showed participants preferred a MODERATELY more minimalist design — just not Tufte's extreme — so it is not a simple "more decoration is always better" result; (3) authors note a familiarity confound (subjects had no prior exposure to minimalist graphs). Critically for an evidence-based skill: this study measures PREFERENCE/ATTITUDE, not comprehension or recall, and the claim correctly stays in the "preference" lane — it does not overreach into a performance claim, so it cannot be misused to assert minimalism harms understanding.

#### V43 `a8ab7e35` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled experiment, participants preferred standard non-minimalist bar graphs over Tufte's high-data-ink-ratio minimalist versions of the same data — directly contradicting the popular claim that maximizing the data-ink ratio is universally better."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/220956267_Minimalism_in_information_visualization_Attitudes_towards_maximizing_the_data-ink_ratio (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "People did not like Tufte's minimalist design of bar-graphs; they seem to prefer 'chartjunk' instead."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the named primary source. Inbar, Tractinsky & Meyer (2007), "Minimalism in information visualization: attitudes towards maximizing the data-ink ratio," ECCE 2007 (European Conf. on Cognitive Ergonomics), pp. 185-188, ACM doi:10.1145/1362550.1362587 — a peer-reviewed conference paper, appropriate for the claim's strength. Confirmed independently via the Ben-Gurion University research portal and ACM Digital Library: n=87 students in 3 conditions rated preference for a standard bar-graph vs. a minimalist version, both taken from Tufte (1983); result was a "clear preference of non-minimalist bar-graphs... low acceptance of minimalist design principles such as high data-ink ratio," with participants favoring the traditional graph across multiple dimensions at p<.001. The supporting quote ("people did not like Tufte's minimalist design of bar-graphs; they seem to prefer 'chartjunk' instead") matches the documented finding. Source is current enough (the finding concerns a stable design principle, not a fast-moving field) and is academic, not marketing. Caveats that qualify but do NOT refute: (a) the study measured ATTITUDES/preference only — not comprehension, recall, or task accuracy — so it contradicts "data-ink maximization is universally better" on the preference/aesthetic dimension, not on effectiveness; Tufte's clarity claim is neither tested nor refuted here; (b) nuance — in one condition ~41% preferred a graph that increased the data-ink ratio over the original, so it's not a blanket endorsement of chartjunk; (c) "prefer chartjunk" is a loose paraphrase — the comparison was standard vs. Tufte-minimalist bar graphs, not Nigel Holmes decorative embellishment (the latter is the separate Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" study). The claim's operative verb is "preferred," which is exactly what the study found, so it stands.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The qualifying nuance (measures preference not comprehension; ~41% accepted increased data-ink in one condition) comes from the same study's own report (Ben-Gurion University research portal / ACM DL doi:10.1145/1362550.1362587) and limits scope but does not refute the preference finding.

#### V44 `a8fe5e62` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors explicitly reject a universal 'all charts (or bars) must start at zero' rule; whether truncation is honest or deceptive depends on the effect sizes and task, not a fixed convention."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that...all charts with quantitative axes should include 0."

*Evidence:* Primary source verified: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222; arXiv 1907.02035). Verbatim from the Discussion confirms both clauses of the claim. Clause 1 (reject universal zero rule): "We resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0." This is the exact supporting quote. Clause 2 (depends on effect size/task, not fixed convention): "The designer of the visualization, by selecting a y-axis starting point, has control over the subjective importance of the resulting differences. There is no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe, important, or meaningful an effect size ought to be"; and "there is no obvious way for designers to relinquish the responsibility of considering effect size in their charts." Independent WebSearch summary and a third-party source (Observable, "It's never okay to crop the y-axis, except when it is") both confirm the authors do NOT advocate mandatory zero baselines. Source quality matches claim strength: peer-reviewed top-tier HCI venue, primary, 2020 (current, still canonical on axis truncation), not marketing. IMPORTANT NUANCE for the skill (does not refute, but must not be dropped): the SAME paper finds truncation robustly EXAGGERATES perceived effects — "the exaggeration introduced through truncation appears to persist across chart types and chart designs, and even when participants make accurate reports of the numbers they observe." So the paper rejects a blanket rule but does not declare truncation harmless; the honest/deceptive judgment hinges on whether the implied effect-size matches the data semantics and analytical intent. The claim as worded is consistent with this and does not overreach into "truncation is fine."
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the claim about what these authors concluded. Some style guides still mandate zero-baselined bars, but that is a separate normative stance, not a refutation of the paper's stated position; the claim is correctly scoped to "the authors."

The paper's cited "previous studies" are real controlled experiments — chiefly Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" (CHI 2010, peer-reviewed, N=20), which found embellished charts had comprehension accuracy no worse than plain and significantly better recall after 2-3 weeks; corroborated by Borgo et al. 2012 (IEEE TVCG). Source quality (peer-reviewed venue + peer-reviewed primary) matches the modest claim strength; not outdated; not marketing.

#### V48 `aaff1455` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Adding explicit visual cues that signal truncation (broken-axis marks, gradient fade at the bottom) does NOT meaningfully reduce the exaggeration effect — truncated charts with break indicators were perceived about the same as plain truncated charts. This contradicts the popular belief that a break mark makes a truncated axis 'honest.'"

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/fullHtml/10.1145/3313831.3376222 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "There was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05)."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED — claim is well-supported by the primary source. The quote comes from Experiment Two ("Visual Design Interventions") of Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (arXiv:1907.02035; DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222). The three "designs" compared (df=2 → 3 groups) were exactly: standard truncated bar, bar with a broken-axis mark, and bar with a gradient bottom. "Perceived severity" is the subjective effect-MAGNITUDE rating ("how quickly are the values changing," 5-pt scale) — i.e., the exaggeration measure itself, so the quote genuinely backs the claim rather than measuring a different construct. No significant difference among the three = break/gradient indicators did NOT reduce the exaggeration vs. a plain truncated chart. The paper's Discussion states verbatim: "we cannot rely on visual indicators of broken or truncated axes to counteract the exaggeration caused by y-axis truncation." Lead author Correll's own summary (mcorrell.medium.com): "just indicating that something screwy is going on with the y-axis was not sufficient to remove the impact of the truncation on perceived effect size." Independent corroboration: Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marghetis, "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," Cognitive Research: Principles and Implications (2021) — warnings reduce but do not eliminate the truncation effect. Minor caveat (not refuting): F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05 is right at the significance boundary (critical F≈3.15), a borderline null; but the claim is appropriately hedged ("not meaningfully reduce," "about the same") and matches the authors' own interpretation. The "contradicts popular belief that a break mark makes truncation honest" framing is fair and aligned with the paper's stated conclusion. Source quality (top-tier peer-reviewed HCI venue, recognized vis researchers, 2020, corroborated independently) matches the claim's strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Closest related work (Yang et al. 2021, Cognitive Research) reinforces rather than contradicts: warnings/indicators reduce but do not eliminate the truncation exaggeration. Only nuance is the borderline p=0.05, which slightly softens a strong-null reading but does not contradict the hedged claim and matches the authors' own conclusion.

#### V49 `ab4656cc` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Across five controlled experiments, y-axis truncation on bar graphs causes viewers to perceive depicted differences as larger (a 'truncation effect'), robust across participants with 83.5% showing the effect — providing empirical support for the long-standing Tufte/expert injunction against truncated bar axes."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S2211368120300978 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In five studies, we provide empirical evidence that y-axis truncation leads viewers to perceive illustrated differences as larger (i.e., a truncation effect). This effect ... was robust across participants, with 83.5% of participants across these 5 studies showing a truncation effect."

*Evidence:* The supporting quote is the verbatim abstract of a primary peer-reviewed study — Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marsh (2021), "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," Journal of Applied Research in Memory and Cognition (confirmed via Scholars@Duke and multiple hosted PDFs). The claim faithfully restates it: five studies, a "truncation effect" (differences perceived as larger), robust with 83.5% of participants showing it. No overreach in the empirical core. Source quality matches the claim's strength (primary, peer-reviewed, 2021 — current). The finding is corroborated, not contradicted: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020) confirms truncation exaggerates perceived differences (their dissent is normative, about whether truncation is always wrong, not whether the effect exists), and the CHI 2024 "To Cut or Not To Cut?" systematic study again confirms truncation effects. The claim is also correctly scoped to BAR axes, where the zero-baseline rule is near-consensus (length encoding), and does not overreach into line charts, where the rule is genuinely debated (Cairo, Skelton, Observable). One minor caveat: I could not confirm the paper cites Tufte by name (PDF text extraction unavailable), but the claim hedges as "Tufte/expert injunction," and the no-truncated-bar rule is a documented long-standing expert convention (Huff 1954 "gee-whiz graph"; Tufte's Lie Factor/graphical integrity). This caveat does not undermine the claim. Not marketing, not cherry-picked, not outdated.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020) — qualifies the normative conclusion but CONFIRMS the empirical effect, so it does not refute the claim.

The paper defines monotonic truncation as truncation that preserves the data's underlying relations and non-monotonic as truncation that breaks them, and states verbatim: "For comparing or estimating the relative growth between two bars, truncating monotonically has similar performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance." A search for contradicting evidence surfaced only a corroborating CHI 2026 follow-up, "Taking Truncation to Task" (DOI 10.1145/3772318.3790617), which likewise finds truncation increases error for ratio/relative tasks while helping value-retrieval tasks and that direct data labels mitigate it. Minor scope caveat (not refuting): the finding is specific to the relative-growth task in grouped bar charts; the same paper found near-zero effect for simple bar-difference comparisons — but the claim already restricts itself to relative growth.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found; CHI 2026 follow-up "Taking Truncation to Task" (DOI 10.1145/3772318.3790617) corroborates rather than disputes.

#### V52 `ac51d6e1` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The treatments that involved bar-charts (affected by the "truncated axis" distortion), the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37."

*Evidence:* I extracted the raw text of the primary source (Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov, Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015, DOI 10.1145/2702123.2702608) from the publisher/RPI PDF. Every number in the claim is verbatim-correct:

1. N=250: paper states "We recruited 250 unique participants for this study and assigned one of the six treatments to each of them." This is the message-exaggeration/understatement study (six treatments: Bubble/Bar/AspectRatio × Control/Deceptive). The claim's "N=250 for the exaggeration study" is exactly right. (A web summary cited 330 total across both this study AND the separate message-reversal study; the claim correctly scopes 250 to the exaggeration study.)

2. Means + CIs: Table 2 ("Average participant response with 95% CI to the 'how much' question," 5-point Likert, min=1 max=5) reports — Bar (Deceptive) Truncated Axis: "2.77, 95% CI [2.26,3.28]"; Bar (Control) Truncated Axis: "1.45, 95% CI [1.27,1.62]." Matches the claim exactly.

3. Significance test: "the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U = 1144, Z = 3.36, p = 0.0003, r = 0.37" (one-tailed Mann-Whitney U). Matches the supporting quote and the claim's p=0.0003.

The claim correctly combines the descriptive means/CIs (Table 2) with the inferential p-value (Mann-Whitney) — both describe the SAME Bar Control-vs-Deceptive truncated-axis comparison, so this is not an overreach. Direction is correct: the truncated (non-zero) bar chart drove a higher perceived-magnitude rating (2.77 vs 1.45), i.e., it inflated magnitude judgments, p=0.0003.

No contradicting evidence: the follow-on study often paired with this (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020) REPLICATES the exaggeration effect of bar truncation (it debates whether truncation is always "wrong," but confirms it inflates perceived difference) — so it reinforces rather than refutes the claim.

Source quality is appropriate: peer-reviewed CHI 2015 controlled crowdsourced experiment, the exact primary source cited. Not outdated, not marketing, not cherry-picked. The interpretation that this supports the bars-start-at-zero convention is well-grounded. Minor caveat only: the paper's measure is a 1-5 Likert "how much" exaggeration rating, which the claim fairly paraphrases as a "how much bigger" magnitude judgment.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" CHI 2020 (DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222) — replicates and confirms the bar-truncation exaggeration effect; does not contradict the claim.

#### V57 `acd95f48` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The blanket 'bar charts must always start at zero' rule is not supported as universal; the authors recommend truncating the y-axis when the goal is to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size on difference tasks, and only avoid truncation (or use monotonic truncation) for percentage-change tasks."

**Supporting quote:** "if the designer wants to elicit a meaningful subjective perception of effect size in the viewers, our results corroborate existing recommendations [12,31,44] to truncate the y-axis to show the desired effect size. *Evidence:* Claim faithfully represents the cited primary source. Source confirmed: "To Cut or Not To Cut? ACM page 403'd, but the paper's verbatim design recommendation was recovered via search of the paper text: for difference/gap tasks (Compare Gaps, Estimate Gap) "the designer can truncate the axis to elicit the desired subjective perception of effect size"; for ratio tasks (Compare Ratios, Estimate Ratio) "monotonic truncations or no truncations should be performed." This maps directly onto the claim's three parts: (a) the universal "always start at zero" rule is rejected as task-dependent — this is the paper's whole thesis; (b) truncate for effect-size perception on difference tasks; (c) no/monotonic truncation for percentage-change (ratio) tasks. The supporting quote matches. "Percentage change" = (b-a)/a correctly corresponds to the paper's ratio tasks. Confirmed corroboration also that "no truncation, in general, performs similarly to monotonic truncation, and both perform much better than non-monotonic truncation" for percentage-change tasks. Checklist: (1) supported by quote — yes; (2) no credible contradiction — the older zero-baseline/deception line (Pandey 2015; Correll, Bertini & Franconeri CHI 2020) is precisely what this 2024 paper refines, not a contradiction of the claim's representation of it; (3) source quality sufficient — peer-reviewed CHI full paper; (4) current (2024); (5) not marketing. Caveat (non-refuting): single study; conventional zero-baseline guidance still holds for deception concerns, so the skill should label this as a recent qualifying finding, not settled consensus.

#### V58 `ace55afe` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S2211368120300978 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In five studies, we provide empirical evidence that y-axis truncation leads viewers to perceive illustrated differences as larger (i.e., a truncation effect). This effect ... was robust across participants, with 83.5% of participants across these 5 studies showing a truncation effect."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by a primary, peer-reviewed source. The supporting quote is verbatim from Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marsh (2021), "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," Journal of Applied Research in Memory and Cognition 10(2):298-311 (ScienceDirect PII S2211368120300978; full text freely mirrored at Duke Scholars, gwern.net, and authors' squarespace PDF). Every specific in the claim checks out: five studies (Studies 1-5), the named "truncation effect" (truncation makes depicted differences look larger), and "83.5% of participants across these 5 studies showing a truncation effect" — an exact match to the abstract. The effect persisted after participants were taught about truncation and did not vary with graph literacy, strengthening (not weakening) the "robust" characterization. The attribution to a "Tufte/expert injunction against truncated bar axes" is faithful: the paper's title and conclusion directly assert bar graphs should not be truncated, and the claim hedges to "expert," covering the broader Cleveland/Few/Tufte consensus that bars (encoding magnitude by length) must start at zero. (CHI) and "Misleading graphs in context: Less misleading than expected" (PMC9200168) — do NOT contradict the claim. They concern (a) line graphs vs. bar charts, where the consensus including the critics is still that BARS should start at zero, and (b) attenuation of real-world impact once context is added — a qualifier on practical harm, not a refutation of the perceptual truncation effect for bars that this claim is scoped to. Source quality (recent peer-reviewed empirical paper, 2021) matches the claim's strength. No overreach detected between quote and claim.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020, arXiv:1907.02035) and "Misleading graphs in context: Less misleading than expected" (PMC9200168) qualify real-world impact and the line-vs-bar distinction, but both still endorse not truncating bar charts — so they reinforce rather than refute the claim.

#### V59 `ace7a416` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled study of grouped bar charts, for tasks that involve comparing or estimating the absolute difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis (not starting at zero) does NOT measurably degrade task performance/accuracy."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED. The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract of the primary source: Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024 (ACM, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102) — a peer-reviewed controlled experiment with pre-registered OSF materials. Abstract: "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance." The claim faithfully and correctly SCOPES this to the absolute-difference ("Gap") tasks and does NOT overgeneralize to relative/ratio tasks (where the same paper found non-monotonic truncation impairs performance). I verified the body of the paper (extracted from the PDF), which backs the abstract with numbers: Compare Gaps — "no significant difference in performance between the different types of truncation" (e.g. non-monotonic vs none mean +0.26%, CI[-1.89%,2.39%]; CIs straddle zero); Estimate Gap — "no significant difference between monotonic truncation and no truncation" (mean -0.05, CI[-0.23,0.12]). Source quality matches the claim's strength (top HCI venue, reputable authors — Kay is a leading viz researcher), and it is current (2024).

Adversarial checks that did NOT refute: (a) Prior "truncation misleads" work (Pandey 2015 "How Deceptive Are Deceptive Visualizations"; Correll/Bertini/Franconeri 2020 "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI; Yang et al. 2021 "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers") does NOT contradict this scoped claim — those studies measured subjective/perceived effect size and proportional/ratio judgments, a different dependent variable. The paper explicitly states its results "corroborate and expand upon prior work" (truncation inflates SUBJECTIVE effect-size perception while NOT degrading accuracy on the additive-difference task). (b) Source is primary, peer-reviewed, not marketing.

ONE boundary caveat (refinement, not refutation): within the same study, for the Estimate Gap task under NON-monotonic truncation, absolute error rose by mean 0.635 (CI[0.371,0.926], excludes zero) — "a small exaggeration." The authors themselves fold this into their own summary ("y-axis truncation, regardless of monotonicity, has little to no effect on user performance" for difference tasks), and the comparison task (Compare Gaps) showed no effect even under non-monotonic truncation. So the strong word "does NOT measurably degrade" is very slightly overstated for the non-monotonic sub-case of estimation, but this is the source paper's own nuance, not an external contradiction. A precise skill should state "truncation does not degrade accuracy on additive-difference tasks (monotonic truncation: no effect; non-monotonic: at most a small exaggeration in gap estimation)."
*Counter-source:* No source contradicts the scoped claim. Prior literature often cited as opposing (Pandey et al. 2015, CHI; Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020 "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI; Yang et al. 2021 "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers", J. Exp. Psych. Applied) measured perceived/subjective effect size and ratio judgments — a different dependent variable — and the source paper says it corroborates rather than contradicts them. The only qualifier is internal: same paper, non-monotonic truncation causes a small significant increase in absolute error on the Estimate Gap task (mean 0.635, CI[0.371,0.926]).

The authors themselves note a key limitation: subjects had no prior exposure to the minimalist form, so familiarity may drive the result. Minor wording slip in the claim: it says "graphs" where the study tested only bar-graphs, but the study scope was bar-graphs exclusively, so this is not a distortion.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Caveat (not a counter): Inbar et al. measured aesthetic preference, not task performance; for performance/recall claims about chartjunk the relevant counterweight is Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" (CHI), which is a different question than this preference claim.

The supporting quote "visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization" is verbatim from the paper's abstract (confirmed via PubMed and independent search). The study used a dual-task (divided-attention) design and found embellishments improved memory retention but slowed visual search, with mixed comprehension results — so the claim's "can improve memory retention" is accurate and appropriately hedged, not an overreach. Framing it as contradicting a strict reading of Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk rule ("remove all non-data ink") is fair, since pictorial embellishments are non-data ink yet showed a measurable memory benefit. Source is a peer-reviewed top-venue primary study, matching the claim's strength; not outdated, not marketing. Minor nuance for the skill (not a refutation): the memory benefit attaches specifically to pictorial/metaphoric embellishments, not all chartjunk (3D, moiré, heavy gridlines), and the same effect tracks Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?"; Stephen Few's critique disputes over-generalizing this to analytical contexts but does not deny the memory effect was found.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate: A Close Examination of Recent Findings" (Perceptual Edge / Visual Business Intelligence Newsletter) — critiques over-generalizing the memory benefit, but does not refute that the controlled studies found a retention effect; it qualifies scope rather than contradicting the finding.

Inline-embedding part: the same page states "Sparklines mean that graphics are no longer cartoonish special occasions with captions and boxes, but rather sparkline graphics can be everywhere a word or number can be: embedded in a sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet, graphic" — which licenses the claim's "embedded inline in text, tables, or other graphics rather than stand alone." #### V63 `ad614ed0` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Across all three magnitude-distortion techniques (truncated axis, area-as-quantity, aspect ratio), deceptive charts inflated viewers' comparative magnitude responses by 58.5% to 129.5% relative to the undistorted control, with all three differences significant at p<0.001 — quantifying that these classic 'graphical integrity' violations produce large, not marginal, message-level deception."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/2702123.2702608 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "When asked to compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5, the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against full-text primary source: Pandey, Manivannan/Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov, Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", ACM CHI 2015, DOI 10.1145/2702123.2702608 (extracted from the paper's own PDF, Results + Discussion sections).

(1) The 58.5%-129.5% figure is verbatim in the Discussion: "the distorted charts lead to responses between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than the control condition" for the "compare two entities or variables by answering on a scale between 1 and 5" question. The very next sentence ties it to "the three charts that use a message exaggeration/understatement technique" — i.e., exactly the claim's three: truncated axis (bar), area-as-quantity (bubble), aspect ratio (line). The fourth technique (inverted axis = message reversal) is correctly excluded.

(2) "All three significant at p<0.001" is INDEPENDENTLY confirmed by the per-technique Mann-Whitney U tests: aspect ratio/Line p<0.0001 (U=1409, Z=5.88, r=0.66); truncated axis/Bar p=0.0003 (U=1144, Z=3.36, r=0.37); area-as-quantity/Bubble p=0.0007 (U=1121, Z=3.08, r=0.34). Each is below 0.001 (bubble's 0.0007 is the nearest but still under). The paper itself states: "We found the difference in participants responses highly significant (p<0.001) for control/deceptive conditions across all the distortion types."

(3) "Large, not marginal" matches the paper's own wording: "the effects are also rather large." Source is the primary peer-reviewed CHI paper (330 participants, controlled crowdsourced study); quality matches claim strength. No credible source contradicts the empirical inflation; the later truncated-y-axis debate (Correll/Bertini/Franconeri 2020) contests the normative recommendation, not the measured exaggeration. Only minor caveat: the percentages are descriptive magnitudes (not themselves p-tested), but the per-technique significance tests substantiate the differences, so the claim does not overreach.

#### V64 `ad7e92c5` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The zero-baseline rule the study empirically validates applies specifically to BAR graphs; for line graphs and dot plots, which do not encode value by length/area, truncation may be appropriate and a zero baseline may not be necessary."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S2211368120300978 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "our recommendation to not truncate vertical axes is specific to bar graphs. Line graphs and dot plots, for example, do not represent numerical values as continuous visual areas and truncation may be appropriate in these cases (Cleveland, 1994; Zoss, 2016, p. 42)."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully paraphrases its source — Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marsh (2021, "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," JARMAC 10(2):298-311) do write that "our recommendation to not truncate vertical axes is specific to bar graphs. Line graphs and dot plots... do not represent numerical values as continuous visual areas and truncation may be appropriate in these cases." So clause 1 (the study's EMPIRICAL validation is bar-specific) is correct. The refutation targets clause 2. (a) The source supports the line-graph exemption only with CONVENTION citations (Cleveland 1994; Zoss 2016, p.42), NOT its own data — Yang et al. tested only bar graphs. The claim dresses a convention as a validated finding. (b) The claim's stated mechanism — line/dot charts are safer to truncate because they "do not encode value by length/area" — is "line-chart exceptionalism," and the dedicated controlled study on exactly this question REFUTES it: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", ACM CHI 2020 (arXiv:1907.02035) found "no significant difference between bars and lines" in truncation-induced bias to subjective trend strength; in the authors' own words the "line chart exceptionalists seemed to be wrong" — although bars and lines encode differently, "that didn't seem to matter for how participants decoded the chart." Truncation biased line charts just as much. (c) Pandey et al. 2015 ("How Deceptive Are Deceptive Visualizations?") also documents truncation distorting perceived magnitude beyond bars. So the assertion "truncation may be appropriate and a zero baseline may not be necessary" for line graphs is convention contradicted by the best empirical evidence — precisely the kind of popular claim the research question asks to flag, and unsafe to label as proven in an evidence-based design skill.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", ACM CHI 2020 (arXiv:1907.02035) — found no significant bar-vs-line difference in truncation-induced bias, empirically refuting the "lines don't encode by length so truncation is fine" rationale the claim depends on.

#### V68 `ae5a8980` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Across five controlled experiments, y-axis truncation on bar graphs causes viewers to perceive depicted differences as larger (a 'truncation effect'), robust across participants with 83.5% showing the effect — providing empirical support for the long-standing Tufte/expert injunction against truncated bar axes."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S2211368120300978 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "In five studies, we provide empirical evidence that y-axis truncation leads viewers to perceive illustrated differences as larger (i.e., a truncation effect). This effect ... was robust across participants, with 83.5% of participants across these 5 studies showing a truncation effect."

*Evidence:* Claim is accurately supported by a primary peer-reviewed source and corroborated by independent replications. The quote is verbatim from the abstract of Yang, Vargas Restrepo, Stanley & Marsh (2021), "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers," Journal of Applied Research in Memory and Cognition 10(2):298-311 (Elsevier, S2211-3681 = JARMAC). Confirmed: five studies, robust "truncation effect," 83.5% of participants showing it; effect persisted even after viewers were warned/taught about truncation. The claim is correctly scoped to BAR graphs/axes (encoded by length from zero), where Tufte's injunction applies. Adversarial counter-search found no refutation: the two strongest candidate dissents CORROBORATE the perceptual finding. Correll, Bertini & Franconeri (2020, CHI, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?") state "bar charts with y-axes that don't begin at zero can visually exaggerate effect sizes" and that people misread truncated charts even with prominent truncation cues; their disagreement is purely normative/about other chart types (line charts, analytic intent), not about the bar-graph exaggeration. Long & Kay (2024, CHI, "To Cut or Not To Cut?") independently replicate the ~83.5% magnitude-exaggeration finding and show even quantitative PhDs are susceptible. Source quality (primary, peer-reviewed, 2021, multiply replicated) matches the claim's strength; not marketing, not outdated. Minor non-refuting caveat: the broad blanket rule "always zero-base bars" remains debated on usefulness grounds, and the perceived-magnitude bias does not always degrade task performance (Long & Kay) — but the specific empirical claim ("perceive depicted differences as larger") is solid.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri (2020) CHI "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" and Long & Kay (2024) CHI "To Cut or Not To Cut?" — both examined as potential refutations but CONFIRM the bar-graph exaggeration finding; they only qualify the normative/blanket-rule interpretation and task-performance implications, not the perceptual claim under review.
