As a faithful citation of what the study found, the claim is fully supported by the primary source.
*Counter-source:* Critiques (Stephen Few; Li & Moacdieh 2014, Proc. HFES) question generalizing the result, and the study's own caveats (n=10/group, 1-tailed t-tests, trend p=.042 marginal) limit strength — but no source contradicts that these exact figures were reported; the claim correctly scopes them to this one study.

#### V75 `aff5d955` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled study of grouped bar charts, for tasks that involve comparing or estimating the absolute difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis (not starting at zero) does NOT measurably degrade task performance/accuracy."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."

*Evidence:* The claim is verbatim-supported by the primary source. Paper: Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102 — a controlled within-subjects experiment (n=32, grouped bar charts, Bayesian analysis with credible intervals). I extracted the PDF text directly (mucollective.northwestern.edu/files/2024-to_cut_or_not.pdf). The paper states verbatim: "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance." Its summary: "for tasks that examine a quantity whose data relation is unaffected by truncation (i.e., gap), truncation has little to no effect on user performance, regardless of whether it is monotonic or not... for Compare Gaps, there is essentially no difference in user performance between no truncation and monotonic/non-monotonic truncation." The claim correctly maps "difference between lengths of two bars" = absolute difference = the paper's "gap" task. The claim is also correctly scoped: the paper finds truncation DOES degrade performance for ratio/percentage-change tasks ("non-monotonically truncating the y-axis worsens user performance"), but the claim explicitly limits itself to absolute-difference tasks, so it does not overreach.

Adversarial checks all fail to refute: (1) Quote support — exact match, not a misread. (2) Apparent contradictions (Pandey et al. 2015; Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020 "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?"; Yang et al. 2021 "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers") do NOT contradict the scoped claim — they measure subjective/perceived effect size and ratio/Likert judgments, a different dependent variable, not absolute-difference estimation accuracy. The 2024 paper explicitly reconciles this: truncation can elicit a stronger subjective impression of effect size while leaving absolute-gap task accuracy intact. (3) Source quality is sufficient — peer-reviewed CHI primary experiment, matching the claim's modest strength. (4) Not outdated — 2024, and the CHI 2026 follow-up "Taking Truncation to Task" (DOI 10.1145/3772318.3790617) reaffirms: truncation increases error for ratio tasks but not for value-retrieval/filter tasks, agreeing the harm is concentrated in ratio judgments. (5) Not marketing/cherry-picked.

One minor caveat (does not rise to refutation): for the "Estimate Gap" sub-task specifically, non-monotonic truncation caused a small upward exaggeration in estimated magnitude (mean diff in absolute error 0.635, CI [0.371, 0.926]), which the authors characterize as a small effect; the headline conclusion that gap task performance is unaffected still holds and matches the claim's "not measurably degrade."
*Counter-source:* Pandey et al. 2015; Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020 (CHI); Yang et al. 2021 (J. Applied Research in Memory & Cognition) all find truncation is "misleading" — but only for subjective/perceived effect size and ratio judgments, NOT absolute-difference task accuracy, so they do not contradict this narrowly-scoped claim. The CHI 2026 follow-up "Taking Truncation to Task" confirms the harm is task-dependent and concentrated in ratio tasks.

### Run B — sibling (105 agents)

#### V76 `a01bda0f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Embellished charts were remembered significantly better than plain charts after a long delay (a gap longer than 12 days, described elsewhere as two-to-three weeks), indicating decorative imagery aided recall of both the chart's topic and its details."

**Source:** https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "participants were able to remember significantly more about the embellished charts after a gap longer than 12 days, suggesting that the addition of strong visual images does help people to remember both the topic and details of the chart."

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-supported by the primary peer-reviewed source (Bateman et al., "Useful Junk?", CHI 2010). The supporting quote appears exactly on p.2574. Results (p.2578-2579) confirm with stats: for the long-term recall group (2-3 week / 12-22 day gap), Holmes (embellished) charts beat plain on recall of subject/topic (t9=2.56, p=.015), categories (t9=5.03, p≈.000), and trend (t9=1.95, p=.042). The Discussion explicitly states: "After a long-term gap (2-3 weeks), recall of both the chart topic and the details (categories and trend) was significantly better for Holmes charts." Both descriptors ("longer than 12 days," "two-to-three weeks") match the paper. The claim faithfully restates the study's finding and the authors' own interpretation without overreach; it is correctly scoped to the long delay (immediate 5-min recall showed no topic/detail difference). Source quality (the actual peer-reviewed study) matches the claim strength. Caveat, not a refutation: small N (10/condition), an author-acknowledged distinctiveness confound, and plain charts not maximizing data-ink ratio mean the result supports "embellishment aided long-term recall in this study," not a universal rule — and later work (Li & Moacdieh 2014) qualifies generalizability. But the claim as worded describes only the study's finding, which is accurate.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts that THIS study found the long-term recall benefit; Li & Moacdieh 2014 and Gelman (statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu, 2010) only qualify generalizability, not the reported result.

#### V77 `a0bb1797` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual embellishments (chartjunk) improved memorability — participants better remembered the information depicted in embellished visualizations, directly contradicting the pure data-ink/minimalism prescription that non-data ink should always be removed."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization."

*Evidence:* PubMed 26357185 is correctly identified as Borgo, Abdul-Rahman, Mohamed, Grant, Reppa, Floridi & Chen (2012), "An Empirical Study on Using Visual Embellishments in Visualization," IEEE Transactions on Visualization and Computer Graphics 18(12):2759-2768 — a peer-reviewed primary controlled study in the top visualization venue. The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract: "the results of this study show that visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization." This directly supports the memorability half of the claim. The interpretive half ("contradicts the pure data-ink/minimalism prescription") is sound: embellishments are exactly the "non-data ink" Tufte's data-ink doctrine prescribes removing, so a proven memorability benefit does push against the absolutist minimalist rule. The finding is corroborated by independent controlled studies, not contradicted: Bateman et al. 2010 ("Useful Junk?", CHI) found embellished charts better recalled after a delay; Borkin et al. 2013 ("What Makes a Visualization Memorable?", IEEE TVCG) found pictographic/dense visualizations more memorable; Haroz, Kosara & Franconeri 2015 (ISOTYPE, CHI) found pictographs aided memory without hurting other tasks. Source quality matches claim strength; finding is current and reinforced rather than overturned. Caveat (not a refutation): the benefit is specific to memorability — the same Borgo abstract notes embellishments "can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search," and memorability is not the same as comprehension accuracy. The claim as written asserts only the memorability benefit, so it stays within the evidence.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few critiqued Bateman et al. 2010 (confounds: single designer Nigel Holmes, memorable titles), and embellishments are shown to slow visual search (Borgo 2012 itself) and can reduce reading accuracy/speed in some tasks — but none of these contradict the narrow memorability claim under review.

#### V78 `a0c9ff4e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled within-subjects experiment (n=40), y-axis truncation inflated perceived effect size equally for bar charts and line charts — there was no significant difference between the two chart types. This contradicts the popular rule (Cairo, Bergstrom & West, Tufte-for-time-series) that the zero-baseline requirement applies to bars but exempts line charts."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results fail to support our second hypothesis. There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50). [...] This qualitative bias occurs in both line charts and bar charts, as well as in bar charts that visually indicate either broken axes or the continuation of bars beyond the bounds of the chart."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" (CHI 2020, arXiv:1907.02035). Experiment 1 recruited n=40 (1 excluded), with BOTH visualization design (bar vs line) AND truncation as within-subjects factors — matching the claim's "within-subjects experiment (n=40)" and the F(1,38) denominator df (39 analyzed). The paper's H2 was literally "Bar charts would be more greatly impacted by truncation than line charts," and the result "There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50)" tests exactly the bar-vs-line factor (ar5iv extraction: "This tests the bar chart versus line chart factor"). Conclusion: "fail to support our second hypothesis"; truncation "results in largely qualitatively assessed effect sizes in both types of graphs." The claim's framing is faithful: the paper explicitly quotes Cairo ("...The encodings in a line chart are position and angle, and these don't get distorted if we set a baseline...") and cites Bergstrom & West for exactly the convention that line charts are exempt from the zero-baseline rule, then refutes it empirically. The supporting quote accurately represents the finding. Minor non-fatal caveats: "n=40" is recruited (39 analyzed); the reported F is the main effect of design while H2 implies an interaction, but the authors' own stated conclusion is unambiguous that chart type did not matter and the bias occurs in both. Source quality (peer-reviewed CHI 2020, top HCI venue) exceeds the bar; finding is corroborated by independent summaries and not contradicted by any credible source.

#### V79 `a109437c` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Blasio & Bisantz's controlled monitoring-task study found high data-ink displays produced significantly FASTER identification of out-of-range values than medium or low data-ink displays — partial empirical support for Tufte's data-ink maximization, extended for the first time to dynamic on-screen displays (not just static print graphs)."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0169814102000744 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "participants being significantly faster in identifying out-of-range values using high data-ink displays as opposed to medium or low data-ink displays"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, with a required caveat. The study is real and the core empirical claim is accurate: Blasio, A.J. & Bisantz, A.M. (2002), "A comparison of the effects of data–ink ratio on performance with dynamic displays in a monitoring task," Int. J. of Industrial Ergonomics 30(2) — the exact cited article (ScienceDirect S0169814102000744). The abstract confirms reaction times to out-of-range events were fastest in the highest data-ink-ratio condition, and the authors explicitly describe "some support for principles of non-data-ink reduction," which matches the claim's hedge "partial empirical support." Source is primary, peer-reviewed; claim is current-as-cited and appropriately hedged; no source contradicts the speed finding. None of the five refutation triggers fire, so the claim is not killed. HOWEVER the claim omits two material caveats that the abstract itself flags: (1) CONFOUND — the high data-ink display was a NUMERICAL TABLE while the medium/low displays were CHARTS, so the speed advantage confounds display format (table vs chart) with data-ink ratio; reading an exact number is plausibly faster for out-of-range detection independent of Tufte's principle. (2) The dose-response FAILED — low and medium conditions had similar RTs ("relied on the same graphical specifier"), and situation awareness showed NO effect of data-ink ratio; the paper's overall conclusion is cautionary ("a simple translation of the guidelines from a static to dynamic environment is not a guarantee for improved performance"). Also, the editorial flourish "extended for the first time to dynamic on-screen displays" is an unverified priority claim. Net: the claim is true but incomplete; for the skill, label this as WEAK/CONFOUNDED partial support, not clean evidence for data-ink maximization. Sources: https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/A-comparison-of-the-effects-of-data%E2%80%93ink-ratio-on-in-Blasio-Bisantz/e4c4b557ffaf3dfdf8e708e05ab71aa9c18d42a0 ; https://www.researchgate.net/publication/245095572 ; ScienceDirect S0169814102000744.
*Counter-source:* Blasio & Bisantz (2002) abstract itself, via Semantic Scholar and search summaries, which flag the table-vs-chart confound, the failed dose-response (low≈medium), null situation-awareness effect, and a cautionary overall conclusion — qualifications the claim omits.

The deception was the "Truncated Axis" distortion, i.e. a non-zero baseline bar chart, supporting the zero-baseline convention. Source is a peer-reviewed CHI 2015 primary paper; the finding is corroborated and not contradicted by later work (Correll/Bertini/Franconeri 2020 "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?"; Yang et al. 2021 "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers"), which replicate the exaggeration effect and specifically defend the zero-baseline rule for bar charts. Two minor, non-refuting imprecisions: (a) the question measured perceived magnitude of the real-world quantity, not literally "difference between bars" (paper notes it asks participants to compare real-world objects rather than two bars); (b) the supporting-quote snippet contains only the mean-rank stats, not the 1.45/2.77 Likert means, but I confirmed those independently in Table 2. Source quality matches claim strength; field is not fast-moving; date (2015) is fine.

The strongest "qualifier" in the literature, Correll/Bertini/Franconeri 2020 (CHI) "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", actually replicates the exaggeration effect and endorses the zero-baseline rule for bar charts specifically (debating it only for line charts), reinforcing rather than refuting the claim.

#### V81 `a26ba377` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "An extension of the Bateman et al. 'useful junk' work, using larger data sets (10+ data points) and comparing Nigel Holmes embellished charts against plain grayscale charts, found that chart type (embellished vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall — i.e., embellishment is not cost-free once data sets grow, complicating the clean 'no comprehension penalty' result of Bateman 2010."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."

*Evidence:* The paper is real and the scaffolding is correct — Li, H. & Moacdieh, N. (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proc. Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting 58(1):1516-1520, DOI 10.1177/1541931214581316; it does compare Nigel Holmes embellished charts vs. plain grayscale charts restricted to 10+ data-point sets, under a time-limit manipulation. But the claim's INTERPRETATION is unsupported and apparently reversed. (1) Overreach beyond the quote: "type of chart significantly affected short-term recall" is directionally neutral — it does not say embellishment hurt. The claim's spin "embellishment is not cost-free" is not in the quote. (2) Direction is contradicted: an independent paper by authors close to the original study ("Towards Understanding Enjoyment and Flow in Information Visualization," ar5iv 1503.00582) states verbatim, "Li et al. have recently reported a replication of both recall and enjoyment effects of the original study" — i.e., embellished charts were BETTER recalled, CONFIRMING Bateman 2010, not complicating it. Multiple search syntheses concur that embellished charts were "better recalled both in the short term and long term" with comprehension "no worse." (3) Conflation error: the abstract attributes the comprehension effect to the TIME LIMIT and the recall effect to CHART TYPE; the claim wrongly frames the chart-type RECALL effect as complicating Bateman's no-COMPREHENSION-penalty result, when no chart-type comprehension penalty was reported. So the directional/causal claim ("embellishment carries a cost as data sets grow") is the opposite of the study's actual replication finding.
*Counter-source:* https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/html/1503.00582 — "Li et al. have recently reported a replication of both recall and enjoyment effects of the original study" (Bateman 2010), indicating embellished charts were better recalled, not penalized. Corroborated by abstract syntheses of Li & Moacdieh 2014 (DOI 10.1177/1541931214581316) reporting embellished charts "better recalled" and comprehension "no worse."

This undercuts using the study as clean proof that removing non-data ink improves comprehension."

*Evidence:* Claim well-supported and well-hedged. The study is Blasio & Bisantz (2002), "A comparison of the effects of data-ink ratio on performance with dynamic displays in a monitoring task," Int. J. Industrial Ergonomics (DOI S0169814102000744). Independent search confirms BOTH halves of the claim: (a) participants were significantly faster identifying out-of-range values on the high data-ink display; (b) the high data-ink display was purely numerical/digital while medium and low were graphical gauges — matching the supporting quote, which describes the primary paper's method. The confound interpretation is not the author's idiosyncratic spin: it is the recognized reading in the peer-reviewed literature. Bateman et al.'s "Minimalism in information visualization" review of this study explicitly states the high data-ink condition "falls outside of the realm of Tufte's principle, as simple numerical values are not graphs," and the field treats this study as qualified, not clean, support for data-ink maximization. #### V85 `a3f7f550` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors' evidence-based recommendation: when a task is unaffected by truncation (e.g., comparing gaps) a designer may truncate to align visuals to the task, but for ratio/growth tasks they should use monotonic truncation rather than no truncation or non-monotonic truncation."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "if the tasks examine quantities that are affected by truncation (e.g., Compare Ratios and Estimate Ratio), then we suggest performing monotonic truncat[ion]"

*Evidence:* MISREAD of the primary source. The claim says that for ratio/growth tasks designers "should use monotonic truncation RATHER THAN no truncation or non-monotonic truncation" — i.e., it elevates monotonic above no-truncation. The paper (Long, Hofman, et al., "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, doi:10.1145/3613904.3642102) says the OPPOSITE: no-truncation and monotonic truncation are EQUIVALENT and both acceptable; only non-monotonic is to be avoided.

Verified by extracting the open-access PDF (mucollective.northwestern.edu/files/2024-to_cut_or_not.pdf) and reading the actual Discussion (§6) text:
(a) "for tasks such as comparing or estimating the percentage change between bars, no truncation, in general, performs similarly to monotonic truncation, and both perform much better than non-monotonic truncation. Thus, we suggest that the designer perform NO TRUNCATION OR MONOTONIC TRUNCATION for these tasks."
(b) The very sentence the claim's supporting quote was lifted from reads in FULL: "...that are affected by truncation, e.g., compare ratios and estimate ratio, then we suggest performing MONOTONIC TRUNCATIONS OR NO TRUNCATIONS to avoid creating visual relations that are contrary to data relations."

The claim's supporting quote truncated this exact sentence at "monotonic truncat[ion]" and dropped the words "or no truncations," reversing the authors' equal-standing recommendation into a false preference for monotonic over no-truncation. The abstract corroborates: "truncating monotonically has SIMILAR performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance." So the only correct half of the claim is "rather than non-monotonic" (avoiding non-monotonic IS supported); the "rather than no truncation" half is contradicted by the source itself. The gap-task half of the claim ("may truncate to align visuals to the task") is fine — the paper notes truncation has little-to-no effect on difference/gap tasks and can be used to "make the visuals more task-aligned." But the central ratio-task recommendation as stated is an overreach produced by mid-sentence quote-cutting.
*Counter-source:* Long, Hofman, Scheidegger, et al., "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024 (doi:10.1145/3613904.3642102), Discussion §6 — the same primary source the claim cites. Full text extracted from mucollective.northwestern.edu/files/2024-to_cut_or_not.pdf.

#### V86 `a3fa25e4` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors explicitly state that Tufte's negative, data-ink-ratio-driven view of chart embellishment rests on a general lack of empirical evidence, yet has nonetheless driven the visualization field's minimalism culture — and note the data-ink ratio taken to its logical end would absurdly reduce a bar to a single-pixel line."

**Source:** https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Despite a general lack of empirical evidence supporting this negative view on chart embellishments, it has driven a culture of design simpliﬁcation and sterilization in the data visualization community."

*Evidence:* Verified verbatim against the primary source (Skau, Harrison & Kosara, "An Evaluation of the Impact of Visual Embellishments in Bar Charts," Computer Graphics Forum 34(3), Proc. EuroVis 2015), extracted via reader proxy of the PDF at kosara.net/papers/2015/Skau-EuroVis-2015.pdf. The paper's Introduction reads, in consecutive sentences: "Despite a general lack of empirical evidence supporting this negative view on chart embellishments, it has driven a culture of design simplification and sterilization in the data visualization community. Tufte's writings encourage this approach of reducing the data/ink ratio. This may be a good rule of thumb, but it fails to address the real salient features of a chart. By this measure, bars in a bar chart would be better if they were reduced to a single-pixel wide line." Every component of the claim maps to this text: (a) the "general lack of empirical evidence" supporting the negative view — verbatim; (b) it driving a "culture of design simplification and sterilization" / minimalism — verbatim; (c) the view attributed to Tufte's data/ink ratio — explicitly named ("Tufte's writings encourage this approach of reducing the data/ink ratio"); (d) the data-ink ratio's logical extreme reducing a bar to a single-pixel line — verbatim ("reduced to a single-pixel wide line"), presented by the authors as a reductio ("fails to address the real salient features"), so the claim's gloss "absurdly" is faithful. The supporting quote provided in the task matches the paper exactly. Source is a peer-reviewed top-venue primary paper, matching the claim's strength. The claim only reports what the authors assert (not whether they are empirically right), so post-2015 embellishment studies do not contradict it.</evidence>

#### V87 `a43a85bf` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled within-subjects experiment (n=40), y-axis truncation inflated perceived effect size equally for bar charts and line charts — there was no significant difference between the two chart types. This contradicts the popular rule (Cairo, Bergstrom & West, Tufte-for-time-series) that the zero-baseline requirement applies to bars but exempts line charts."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results fail to support our second hypothesis. There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50). [...] This qualitative bias occurs in both line charts and bar charts, as well as in bar charts that visually indicate either broken axes or the continuation of bars beyond the bounds of the chart."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against primary source (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020; arxiv 1907.02035). All three claim components check out. (1) Design: Experiment One was within-subjects (2x2x3x2x2 factorial with chart type bar/line and truncation as within-subjects factors), 40 participants recruited (39 analyzed). "within-subjects, n=40" is accurate. (2) Findings: main effect of truncation on perceived severity F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001; chart-type comparison (their H2 predicting bars would be MORE affected than lines) F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50 = no significant difference. I verified the quote's factor "visualization design" refers to bar-vs-line chart type (matches H2 "Bar charts would be more greatly impacted by truncation than line charts"), not annotation variants — my main skeptical concern. The paper states "This qualitative bias occurs in both line charts and bar charts, as well as in bar charts that visually indicate either broken axes or the continuation of bars." (3) Contradiction framing is fair: the bars-need-zero/lines-exempt convention is genuinely held by Cairo, Bergstrom & West, and Tufte (time-series baseline); the equal-inflation finding undercuts the lines-exempt carve-out, confirmed by the author's own Observable write-up ("there really is no difference between bar and line charts"). SKEPTICAL CHECKS PASSED: the 2024 CHI follow-up (Long & Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut?") does NOT contradict — it measures objective task performance on grouped bars (different DV) and never claims lines are exempt. Source is peer-reviewed at a top HCI venue, current (2020), not marketing. Minor non-fatal caveats: n=40 recruited vs 39 analyzed; the paper's overall stance is "designer judgment" not "lines must also start at zero," but the narrow empirical claim is exactly what was found.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The 2024 CHI follow-up (Long & Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut?", dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102) measures a different dependent variable (objective task performance on grouped bar charts) and adds nuance but does not dispute the bar-vs-line equality of perceived-effect inflation, nor does it revive a lines-exempt rule.

#### V88 `a4b70516` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "An extension of the Bateman et al. 'useful junk' work, using larger data sets (10+ data points) and comparing Nigel Holmes embellished charts against plain grayscale charts, found that chart type (embellished vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall — i.e., embellishment is not cost-free once data sets grow, complicating the clean 'no comprehension penalty' result of Bateman 2010."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."

*Evidence:* The cited paper is correctly identified — Li & Moacdieh (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proc. HFES Annual Meeting 58(1), DOI 10.1177/1541931214581316 — and its factual scaffolding matches the claim (extension of Bateman; 10+ data-point sets; Nigel Holmes embellished vs. plain grayscale; chart type significantly affected short-term recall). BUT the claim's substantive payload is inverted. The supporting quote ("the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall") states no direction. The actual paper's direction, per two independent abstract syntheses, is that EMBELLISHED charts were BETTER recalled in the short term, and that comprehension was affected by the TIME LIMIT, not by chart type. So the study REPLICATES/EXTENDS Bateman's pro-embellishment finding to larger datasets (embellishment helps recall, no comprehension penalty) — it does NOT show embellishment "is not cost-free" nor "complicate the clean 'no comprehension penalty' result." The claim misreads a direction-neutral, in-fact-positive recall effect as a cost, contradicting its own primary source. The "i.e." conclusion is the load-bearing part of the claim and it is the opposite of what the paper concludes.
*Counter-source:* Li & Moacdieh (2014), HFES Proc. abstract (via journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 and Semantic Scholar/ResearchGate syntheses): "Embellished charts were better recalled in the short term"; comprehension affected by time limit, not chart type.

**Supporting quote:** "we resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0. [...] There is no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe, important, or meaningful an effect size ought to be. [...] We reject the unequivocal dichotomy of 'honest' and 'dishonest' charts."

(1) Empirical finding: y-axis truncation significantly and robustly exaggerated perceived effect size across both bar and line charts, persisting even with explicit break/gradient indicators and when participants estimated true values — so the bias is visual, not numerical-misreading. (2) Reject zero rule, verbatim: "we resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0." (3) Reject dichotomy, verbatim: "We reject the unequivocal dichotomy of 'honest' and 'dishonest' charts." (4) No ground truth, verbatim: "There is no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe, important, or meaningful an effect size ought to be." (5) Designer-must-match-meaningful-effect-size is faithfully paraphrased, supported by two further verbatim lines: "The designer of the visualization, by selecting a y-axis starting point, has control over the subjective importance of the resulting differences," and "there is no obvious way for designers to relinquish the responsibility of considering effect size in their charts." The paper also notes "for line charts at least, a 0-baseline is not always appropriate." Source is the peer-reviewed paper itself (top HCI venue), correctly attributed, not outdated, not marketing. The claim is about what THIS paper argues, and that is exactly what it argues; the existence of a competing convention (bars-always-start-at-zero) does not refute the attribution. The only minor liberty is the word "must," which is slightly firmer than the paper's hedged tone, but the substance (designer bears non-relinquishable responsibility for the axis-range framing) is directly stated.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The competing "bars must start at zero" convention (Cleveland; Huff) is exactly what the paper consciously argues against, so it does not refute the claim about the paper's stance.

#### V90 `a4fa53e6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled memorability study, visualizations with a 'bad' (low) data-ink ratio — i.e., more chartjunk — scored significantly HIGHER on memorability than minimalist 'good' data-ink-ratio visualizations (M=1.81 vs M=1.23, p<0.001), refuting the authors' own hypothesis that minimalist designs are more memorable."

**Source:** http://olivalab.mit.edu/Papers/Borkin_etal_MemorableVisualization_TVCG2013.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We also observed a statistically signiﬁcant effect of the data-to-ink ratio attribute on memorability scores with a “bad” (M = 1.81), i.e., low data-to-ink ratio, being higher than a “good” rating (M = 1.23,t(208) = 6.92, p < 0.001)... higher memorability scores were correlated with visualizations containing pictograms, more color, low data-to-ink ratios, and high visual densities. This supports our third hypothesis, and refutes our fourth and ﬁfth hypotheses."

*Evidence:* Verified verbatim against the primary source PDF (Borkin et al., "What Makes a Visualization Memorable?", IEEE TVCG 19(12):2306-2315, 2013; extracted from the MIT-hosted PDF at web.mit.edu/zoya/www/docs/InfoVis_borkin-128.pdf, a peer-reviewed InfoVis paper). (1) The statistics match exactly: the paper states "...scores with a bad (M = 1.81), i.e., low data-to-ink ratio, being higher than a good rating (M = 1.23; t(208) = 6.92; p < 0.001) as shown in Fig. 6." (2) The "more chartjunk" gloss is the authors' own phrasing — the Discussion reads "visualizations with low data-to-ink ratios and high visual densities (i.e., more chartjunk and clutter) were more memorable than minimal, clean visualizations." (3) The refuted-hypothesis claim is precise: Section 6.1 lists "H.5 A visualization is more memorable if it is more minimalist (i.e., good data-ink ratio)," and Results state "This supports our third hypothesis, and refutes our fourth and fifth hypotheses." So the finding does refute the authors' own H5 (minimalist = more memorable). Source quality matches claim strength (primary peer-reviewed venue), and the finding is current/uncontested as a fact. Caveat (does not refute the claim as worded): "memorability" = recognition via repeat-detection, not comprehension/recall, and attributes were coded on naturalistic stimuli (correlational), not manipulated within a chart — so "controlled" is mildly generous, but the significant higher score for low data-ink is exactly the paper's reported result. The claim accurately stays within "memorability" and does not assert chartjunk aids comprehension.
*Counter-source:* No credible source disputes the empirical finding itself. Stephen Few's critique and the authors' own follow-up (Borkin et al., "Beyond Memorability: Visualization Recognition and Recall," TVCG 2015) only qualify the interpretation — that recognition memorability is not the same as comprehension/data recall — which does not contradict this specifically worded claim.

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (Borkin et al. 2013, "What Makes a Visualization Memorable?", IEEE TVCG / InfoVis 2013). Thus, looking at these visualizations as images and not data encodings, these attributes increased their memorability." (b) "memorability — i.e., treating visualizations as scenes — does not necessarily translate to an understanding of the visualizations themselves. Nor does excessive visual clutter aid comprehension of the actual information in the visualization (and may instead interfere with it)." Two further passages reinforce it: "Clearly, a more memorable visualization is not necessarily a more comprehensible one" and "we focus purely on memorability of the visualizations as images to remove any obfuscation by other variables." Each element of the claim maps directly: the study's task was recognition/repeat-detection memory on Mechanical Turk (hence "recognition memory ... as images"); the comprehension exclusion is explicit; the "may interfere" warning is the authors' own parenthetical. This is not an overreach — it is the authors' explicit self-limitation. Checklist: (1) supported by quote = yes, verbatim; (2) no credible source contradicts — the only opposing narrative is the sensationalized 2013 press coverage ("chartjunk is good"), which is exactly the misreading the authors guard against, and Stephen Few's critique reinforces the memorability≠comprehension split; (3) source quality = primary peer-reviewed paper, sufficient for the claim; (4) not outdated (statement of what the paper itself says; 2016 follow-up "Beyond Memorability" extends to recall without contradicting this caveat); (5) not marketing. Claim is well-supported, current, and source quality matches strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible refuting source. Stephen Few's "Chart Junk: A Magnet for Misguided Research" (perceptualedge.com) reinforces, not refutes, the memorability-vs-comprehension distinction.

#### V92 `a5fe8b6c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The apparent 'high data-ink' speed advantage is confounded: the high data-ink display was purely numerical/digital while the medium and low displays were graphical gauges, so the result may reflect the superiority of digital readouts for reading exact values rather than data-ink ratio per se. This undercuts using the study as clean proof that removing non-data ink improves comprehension."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0169814102000744 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the high data-ink display was purely numerical (or "digital") while the medium and low data-ink displays were graphical in nature"

*Evidence:* The claim is well-supported and not an overreach. The study is Blasio & Bisantz (2002), "A comparison of the effects of data–ink ratio on performance with dynamic displays in a monitoring task," Int. J. Industrial Ergonomics 30, 89–101 (the S0169814102000744 DOI). Multiple sources confirm BOTH halves of the claim: (a) the factual confound — "The high data-ink display was purely numerical (or 'digital') while the medium and low data-ink displays were graphical in nature," and (b) the interpretive conclusion — secondary literature states verbatim: "It could be argued that the study's high data-ink condition falls outside of the realm of Tufte's principle, as simple numerical values are not graphs... the performance advantage observed in the high data-ink condition may have been due to the difference between digital/numerical presentation versus graphical presentation, rather than purely to the data-ink ratio principle itself" (Graph Design: The Data-ink Ratio and Expert Users, SciTePress/IVAPP 2021, semanticscholar PDF 1463/e8f5...; also echoed in ResearchGate reviews and scienceux.org). So the claim is not the verifier's own inference — it is a recognized critique in the published literature. Checklist: (1) quote supports it — yes, directly states the confound; (2) no contradicting source found, multiple corroborate; (3) source quality sufficient — peer-reviewed primary study plus review papers; (4) not outdated — it is an age-invariant methodological critique of one specific study; (5) not marketing/cherry-pick. The study's faster-with-high-data-ink result was on speed of detecting out-of-range values, and participants WERE faster with the digital display, which is exactly why the digital-vs-graphical encoding cannot be separated from "data-ink ratio." One minor imprecision: the claim phrases the digital advantage as "reading exact values," whereas the task was out-of-range detection — but this does not weaken the load-bearing assertion (the confound undercuts the study as clean proof for data-ink minimalism). Hedged language ("may reflect") is appropriate. Claim stands.
*Counter-source:* No credible source disputes the confound; the only nuance found is that the task was out-of-range detection rather than "reading exact values" as the claim loosely phrases it, but this does not refute the core point. Secondary sources (SciTePress 2021 "Graph Design: The Data-ink Ratio and Expert Users"; ResearchGate "Data-ink Ratio and Task Complexity in Graph Comprehension") actively affirm the confound.

#### V93 `a61ebb61` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Supporting quote:** "Visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search."

Visualization & Computer Graphics 18(12):2759-2768 — a controlled dual-task experiment in the field's top peer-reviewed venue. The abstract states verbatim both halves of the tradeoff: embellishments "can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization, but visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search." The claim ("measurable cost: they slow visual search... trades against efficiency of finding information") is a faithful summary of this dual finding, not an overreach; the supporting quote is exact. Contradiction check: Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" (the standard challenger) measured interpretation accuracy and 2-3 week recall — finding embellished charts recalled better with no accuracy loss — but did NOT measure visual-search speed, so it does not contradict Borgo's specific search-speed cost. Minor caveat: the paper hedges ("can have a negative impact") while the claim is slightly more absolute, but the claim self-qualifies ("not free," "trades against"), keeping it within bounds. Source quality (primary, peer-reviewed, controlled) matches the claim's modest strength; 2012 finding remains the consensus framing in visualization research.
*Counter-source:* Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" (CHI) — found embellished charts had better long-term recall with no accuracy loss, but measured accuracy/recall, NOT visual-search speed, so it qualifies generality rather than contradicting the search-speed cost.

ADVERSARIAL CHECKS (all fail to refute):
- Overreach into comprehension? No. The claim is carefully scoped to "memorability" (recognition memory), which is exactly what the study measured (Mechanical Turk repeat-detection game per Isola et al.). It does NOT claim chartjunk improves comprehension or recall accuracy, so it sidesteps the Bateman/Few critique.
- Source quality: top-tier peer-reviewed venue (IEEE TVCG 2013, PubMed 24051797), primary source, directly quoted. Matches the claim's strength.
- Methodological nuance: data-ink ratio was a 3-level subjective rating by three researchers (good/medium/bad), not Tufte's computed ratio — but the claim reports the study's finding faithfully and does not misstate the method.
- Later work: Borkin et al. 2015 "Beyond Memorability" adds nuance about WHICH elements aid recall (titles/text/pictograms), and Borkin's own Storybench interview cautions against excessive clutter for effectiveness — but neither contradicts the 2013 recognition-memory result of 1.81 vs 1.23. Not a fast-moving field; the historical result is fixed.

The claim is well-supported, correctly scoped, and matches the primary source on numbers, statistic, p-value, and the verbatim hypothesis it refutes. Not refuted.

#### V97 `a6da98c4` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors' evidence-based recommendation: when a task is unaffected by truncation (e.g., comparing gaps) a designer may truncate to align visuals to the task, but for ratio/growth tasks they should use monotonic truncation rather than no truncation or non-monotonic truncation."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "if the tasks examine quantities that are affected by truncation (e.g., Compare Ratios and Estimate Ratio), then we suggest performing monotonic truncat[ion]"

*Evidence:* The cited paper (Long & Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut?", CHI 2024, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102) recommends, verbatim from its guidelines section: "if the tasks examine quantities that are affected by truncation (e.g., Compare Ratios and Estimate Ratio), then we suggest performing monotonic truncations OR NO truncations to avoid creating visual relations that are contrary to data relations." The claim asserts monotonic truncation "rather than no truncation" for ratio/growth tasks — directly contradicting the source, which puts no-truncation on equal footing with monotonic. The discussion confirms: "monotonic truncation has similar user performance as no truncation, but both are much better than non-monotonic truncation," and for one of the two ratio tasks no-truncation is actually best: "For Estimate Ratio, truncations (regardless of monotonicity) have larger absolute errors compared to no truncation." The supporting quote supplied was cut at "monotonic truncat[ion]" exactly where the source continues "...or no truncations," which reverses the meaning. The gaps half of the claim ("designer may truncate to align visuals to the task" when unaffected) is accurately supported, but the load-bearing ratio recommendation is a misread/overreach contradicted by the primary source.
*Counter-source:* Long & Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-axis Truncation," CHI 2024 — guidelines section ("monotonic truncations or no truncations") and results (Estimate Ratio: no truncation has smallest absolute error). PDF: https://mucollective.northwestern.edu/files/2024-to_cut_or_not.pdf

#### V98 `a6f01890` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In relative-comparison tasks (estimating one bar as a percentage of another), every tested embellishment EXCEPT bars extended below zero significantly increased reading error versus the plain baseline bar chart — including minor cosmetic changes like merely rounding the tops of bars. This directly counters any blanket claim that 'chartjunk'/embellishment is harmless to comprehension accuracy."

**Source:** https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a signiﬁcantly higher error rate."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary paper itself. I downloaded and extracted the full text of Skau, Harrison & Kosara, "An Evaluation of the Impact of Visual Embellishments in Bar Charts," Computer Graphics Forum 34(3), EuroVis 2015 (DOI 10.1111/cgf.12634), via the authors' host media.eagereyes.org. The paper's own conclusion sentence is verbatim: "All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a significantly higher [error rate]." Table 1's Relative-Comparison Result column confirms it: Rounded tops, Triangles, Endcaps, Overlapping triangles, and Disproportionate area all = "worse*" (asterisk = statistically significant), while Extend-below-zero = "equal" (no asterisk). So 5 of 6 embellishments significantly increased relative-judgment error; only bars extended below zero matched baseline. This matches the claim exactly, including the "merely rounding the tops" example. (1) Quote supports claim: yes, the supporting quote is a verbatim sentence from the paper, not a misread. (2) Scope is correct: the claim restricts itself to relative-comparison tasks; for ABSOLUTE judgments the paper found only quadratic/disproportionate-area significantly worse, so the relative-task scoping is load-bearing and the claim respects it. (3) Source quality: peer-reviewed primary venue (EuroVis/CGF), controlled Mechanical Turk user study — sufficient for the claim's strength. (4) Not outdated: 2015, graphical-perception findings are stable. (5) Not marketing/cherry-pick: controlled experiment with baseline. The broader interpretive line ("counters any BLANKET claim that chartjunk is harmless to comprehension accuracy") is appropriately hedged and supported; the nearest counter-evidence, Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?", concerns memorability/recall of pictorial backgrounds, not fine relative-magnitude estimation, so it does not refute this scoped claim. One minor note: some secondary summaries say "four of six embellishments performed worse," which blends absolute+relative results — the precise per-task figures above supersede that looser count and still confirm the claim.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the scoped claim. Nearest tension: Bateman, Mandryk, Gutwin et al., "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," CHI 2010 — but it measures memorability/gist of Holmes-style pictorial charts, not relative-magnitude reading error, so it does not contradict Skau 2015's relative-judgment result.

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001). A post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other."

(1) Truncation levels: the paper states the y-axis "began: either at 0, 25%, or 50%" — confirming the claim's parenthetical "(0%, 25%, 50%)", the one detail not contained in the supplied quote. (3) The paper's post-hoc text reads exactly "post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other" — supporting "all three significantly different from each other." (4) "Large and robust" is justified: F=89, p<0.0001, plus a reported severity increase of 0.36 for 25%-vs-0% truncation; the authors characterize the effect as persistent and strong (the paper's thesis is that truncation IS a real threat). The claim adds no overreach beyond the quote. Independent corroboration with no contradiction: Pandey et al. (2015) found the effect independently (deceptive-baseline mean ~2.8 vs zero-baseline ~1.5); the 2024 CHI replication "To Cut or Not To Cut?" (Long et al.) and Franconeri et al. (2021, Psychological Science in the Public Interest) confirm the effect persists even against warning/debiasing interventions. Source quality (peer-reviewed, CHI Honorable Mention, primary) matches the claim's strength; current (2020, replicated 2024); not a marketing/cherry-picked claim.

#### V100 `a721ed61` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Explicit visual cues that a y-axis is truncated — broken-axis glyphs and gradient-fill bars — did NOT reduce the exaggeration of perceived effect size. This undercuts the common guideline that marking the axis break (e.g. a 'torn paper' or broken-axis symbol) makes truncation acceptable/honest."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results fail to support our first hypothesis: there was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05). [...] Merely indicating that truncation has occurred, even in a prominent and unambiguous way, may not be sufficient to 'de-bias' viewers of truncated charts."

*Evidence:* PRIMARY SOURCE CONFIRMED: Michael Correll et al., "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (arXiv:1907.02035; ACM 10.1145/3313831.3376222; CHI 2020 Honorable Mention). The study tested exactly the two indicator designs named in the claim: (a) broken-axis markers/glyphs and (b) a below-the-axis gradient fill connoting bars extend beyond the chart. Both FAILED to reduce the truncation-induced exaggeration.

QUOTE SUPPORTS CLAIM: The supporting quote is accurate. The author's own plain-language conclusion (Medium write-up): "just indicating that something screwy is going on with the y-axis was not sufficient to remove the impact of the truncation on perceived effect size." The paper states H1 was not supported — F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05 (no significant difference in perceived severity across the three designs) — and concludes "Merely indicating that truncation has occurred, even in a prominent and unambiguous way, may not be sufficient to 'de-bias' viewers of truncated charts." This is precisely the claim. Minor caveat: p=0.05 is right at the threshold (F-crit for df 2,60 ≈ 3.15), so the null is borderline; the authors interpret it as fail-to-support, and the substantive direction agrees.

INDEPENDENT CORROBORATION (not just the same source): "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers" (Cognitive Research / Journal of Applied Research in Memory and Cognition area, ScienceDirect S2211368120300978) independently found that even explicit warnings did NOT eliminate the truncation effect — a separate research group, peer-reviewed, same conclusion.

NO CONTRADICTING EVIDENCE FOUND: Targeted searches for studies showing broken-axis indicators DO de-bias returned nothing credible; all sources reinforced that marking the break does not neutralize the bias.

SOURCE QUALITY: Peer-reviewed CHI 2020 (Honorable Mention) plus an independent peer-reviewed replication. Current (2020), domain is not fast-moving. Not marketing, not a press release, not forum speculation. Claim strength matches source strength.

Only nitpick: the claim phrase "perceived effect size" maps to the paper's measured "perceived severity," and the author uses both interchangeably — no overreach.
*Counter-source:* Searched specifically for refutation (broken-axis indicators reducing deception); found none. Closest adjacent work (Witt-style axis-range studies, JCOM debunking-strategies for misleading bar charts) and the independent ScienceDirect "Truncating Bar Graphs Persistently Misleads Viewers" all AGREE that indicators/warnings fail to de-bias — corroborating, not contradicting.

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source PDF. Paper: Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102 (peer-reviewed full paper, Northwestern/Mu Collective). The supporting quote is verbatim from the abstract: "For comparing or estimating the relative growth between two bars, truncating monotonically has similar performance to no truncation, while truncating non-monotonically is very likely to impair performance." The claim's "(ratio)" gloss on the relative-growth task is validated by the paper's own discussion, which restates it as "comparing or estimating the PERCENTAGE CHANGE between bars" — a ratio judgment. Monotonicity definitions confirmed in text: monotonic = truncations that "preserve the underlying relations in data," non-monotonic = those that "do not preserve underlying relations." Task-dependence confirmed: truncation "has little to no effect" on absolute-difference tasks but matters for relative-growth, where monotonicity decides. Source is current (2024), primary, peer-reviewed — not marketing. No credible contradiction: Correll et al. (2020) "Threat or Menace?" measured perceived effect-size/severity (persuasion bias) not estimation accuracy and did not split by monotonicity, so it refines rather than contradicts. Claim's hedged wording matches the paper's. Minor scope caveat (not refuting): finding is specific to grouped bar charts and these two tasks.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri (2020), "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" CHI 2020 — found strong consistent truncation effects but on perceived severity, not estimation accuracy, and without monotonicity distinction; refines rather than refutes.

#### V107 `a86ff945` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual embellishments (chartjunk) improved memorability — participants better remembered the information depicted in embellished visualizations, directly contradicting the pure data-ink/minimalism prescription that non-data ink should always be removed."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the cited primary source and an independent corroborating study. PubMed 26357185 = Borgo et al. (2012), "An Empirical Study on Using Visual Embellishments in Visualization," IEEE TVCG 18(12):2759-2768; abstract verbatim: visual embellishments "can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization" — a direct match to the claim's memorability assertion. Independently corroborated by Bateman et al. (2010), "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," ACM CHI: embellished charts showed significantly better recall after a 2-3 week gap with interpretation accuracy no worse than plain charts, and the authors explicitly state the results "question some of the premises of the minimalist approach" — supporting the "contradicts always-remove-non-data-ink" framing. The principal critique, Stephen Few's "The Chartjunk Debate" (2011, Perceptual Edge), attacks the poorly-designed plain control charts and over-generalization (external validity), but does NOT dispute the memorability result and concedes well-designed embellishment can help — so it qualifies scope rather than refuting. Source quality (IEEE TVCG + ACM CHI, both top-tier peer-reviewed) exceeds the claim's strength; not outdated (foundational, stable subfield); not marketing. One fair qualification, not a refutation: the studied "embellishments" were meaningful thematic imagery (Holmes-style), not arbitrary junk, and the benefit is specific to memorability/recall — Borgo found embellishments HURT visual search and were mixed for comprehension. The claim correctly limits itself to memorability and to the absolute "always remove" prescription, which the evidence does contradict.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few (2011), "The Chartjunk Debate: A Close Examination of Recent Findings," Perceptual Edge — critiques the plain-control baseline and over-generalization but does not refute the memorability finding itself

#### V109 `a90132b6` — **upheld** · confidence: high

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-confirmed in the primary source (Borkin et al. 2013, "What Makes a Visualization Memorable?", IEEE TVCG / InfoVis best paper). I extracted text directly from the paper PDF by decompressing its FlateDecode streams. (1) "Recognition memory of images" scope: the study used a Mechanical-Turk repeat-detection (recognition) task and the paper states "we focus purely on memorability of the visualizations as images to remove any obfuscation by other variables" and "we tested the memorability of visualizations as images and not the comprehension of the visualizations." (2) Not comprehension: "[memorability] does not necessarily translate to an understanding of the visualizations themselves." (3) Explicit clutter caveat: "Nor does excessive visual clutter aid comprehension of the actual information in the visualization (and may instead interfere with it)." The task's supporting quote matches the paper exactly; its "..." legitimately bridges the Discussion §7.1 sentence and the Conclusion sentence without distorting meaning. The memorable attributes were "pictograms, more color, low data-to-ink ratios, and high visual densities." Adversarial checks found nothing contradicting it: the corroborating Storybench piece (direct Borkin quotes) confirms memorability != effectiveness and that excess clutter harms comprehension; even Bateman et al. 2010, which the paper cites, only claims embellishment is "no less effective" for comprehension, never that clutter aids it. Source quality is maximal (peer-reviewed primary, verbatim-verified); claim is a faithful characterization of the authors' own self-limiting caveat, not outdated or marketing.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Possible naive counter is Bateman et al. 2010 ("Useful Junk?", CHI), but it does not contradict the claim — it found embellished charts aided long-term recall and were "no less effective" for comprehension, never that clutter aids comprehension. Borkin et al. explicitly distinguish their image-memorability result from Bateman's comprehension result.

*Claim under review:* "Tufte defines a sparkline as a small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution, intended to be embedded inline wherever a word or number appears (sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet) rather than set apart as a framed, captioned figure."

**Source:** https://www.edwardtufte.com/notebook/sparkline-theory-and-practice-edward-tufte/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "a small intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution ... sparkline graphics can be everywhere a word or number can be: embedded in a sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet, graphic."

*Evidence:* Claim is fully supported by the cited primary source (edwardtufte.com, Tufte's own notebook "Sparkline theory and practice"). Verbatim matches: (1) "A sparkline is a small intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution" — matches "small, intense, simple, word-sized graphic with typographic resolution." (2) "Sparklines can be everywhere a word or number can be: embedded in a sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet, graphic" — matches "embedded inline wherever a word or number appears (sentence, table, headline, map, spreadsheet)." (3) The contrast "rather than set apart as a framed, captioned figure" is directly grounded in Tufte's "Graphics are no longer cartoonish special occasions with captions and boxes." This is a definitional/attributional claim about a term Tufte coined (Beautiful Evidence, 2006), so it requires source fidelity rather than controlled-study evidence, and the primary source supplies that exactly. No credible source disputes or qualifies the definition; secondary sources (Jon Udell, EDAV course notes, GistVis arXiv 2502.03784) corroborate it. Definition is stable, not outdated, not a marketing claim. The only non-verbatim element ("framed, captioned figure") is a faithful paraphrase of Tufte's "captions and boxes." Could not refute on any of the five checklist axes.

*Evidence:* I extracted text directly from the primary PDF (Bateman et al., "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," CHI 2010). The supplied supporting quote is VERBATIM from the paper's Abstract: "...their recall after a two-to-three-week gap was significantly better. Although we are cautious about recommending that all charts be produced in this style, our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach to chart design." A parallel sentence appears in the Conclusion: "Although we are cautious about proposing specific design recommendations... Our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach to chart design." The claim — that the authors frame their findings as challenging the empirical basis of minimalism while stopping short of recommending all charts be embellished — is directly and accurately supported by this verbatim text. The "(chartjunk-reduction)" gloss is faithful (the paper is explicitly about chartjunk and cites Tufte's data-ink ratio + Cleveland as the "minimalist approach" being questioned). The claim is an authorial-FRAMING claim, and the framing is exactly what the authors wrote.

Two minor qualifications, neither fatal: (a) the claim says "question the premises" where the paper says "question SOME of the premises" — a slight softening dropped, but the claim's hedge ("stop short of recommending all charts be embellished") preserves the authors' caution and matches "cautious about recommending that all charts be produced in this style." (b) The validity of the authors' conclusion is contested downstream (Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate," perceptualedge.com, critiques the n=20 sample, Holmes-vs-stripped-plain comparison, and recall-after-weeks design) — but that critique disputes whether the framing is JUSTIFIED, not whether the authors made it. Since the claim only asserts how the authors framed their own findings (and correctly includes their hedge), Few's critique does not refute it. Source = primary peer-reviewed CHI 2010 paper, verified verbatim twice (Abstract + Conclusion). Not outdated (historical authorial-framing fact), not marketing.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (perceptualedge.com) — disputes the study's methodology/conclusions but NOT that the authors framed their results as questioning minimalist premises; thus does not refute this framing claim.

#### V112 `a9584564` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Blasio & Bisantz's controlled monitoring-task study found high data-ink displays produced significantly FASTER identification of out-of-range values than medium or low data-ink displays — partial empirical support for Tufte's data-ink maximization, extended for the first time to dynamic on-screen displays (not just static print graphs)."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0169814102000744 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "participants being significantly faster in identifying out-of-range values using high data-ink displays as opposed to medium or low data-ink displays"

*Evidence:* Paper confirmed: Blasio & Bisantz (2002), "A comparison of the effects of data–ink ratio on performance with dynamic displays in a monitoring task," Int. J. Industrial Ergonomics 30(2), PII S0169814102000744 — matches the cited source. The core finding is TRUE and corroborated by the primary abstract AND independent secondary citations: in a controlled, dynamic, on-screen monitoring task (9 dynamic gauge-like variables, 24 participants), participants were "significantly faster in identifying out-of-range values using high data-ink displays as opposed to medium or low data-ink displays." So the claim's central empirical assertion is well-supported by the quote and is not an overreach on the bare result; it is appropriately hedged as "partial." TWO qualifications weaken its framing, though: (1) CONFOUND — the high data-ink condition was a purely numerical/digital readout while medium/low were graphical. Inbar, Tractinsky & Meyer (2007), "Minimalism in information visualization" (ECCE '07), the standard secondary review of this study, explicitly state the high data-ink condition "falls outside of the realm of Tufte's principle, as simple numerical values are not graphs." The speed advantage thus plausibly reflects the known superiority of digital readouts for exact-threshold reading (a display-FORMAT effect), not data-ink-RATIO per se — so calling it "support for Tufte's data-ink maximization" is contested, and a skill citing it must carry that caveat. (2) "extended for the first time to dynamic on-screen displays" is an unverified superlative not found in the source (plausible, since prior tests like Gillan & Richman were on static graphs, but not confirmable). Net: the finding is real, primary-sourced, peer-reviewed, and source quality matches the modest 'partial' strength — so not refuted — but the 'support for Tufte' interpretation is materially confounded and should be flagged, not presented as clean.
*Counter-source:* Inbar, O., Tractinsky, N., & Meyer, J. (2007). "Minimalism in information visualization: attitudes towards maximizing the data-ink ratio." Proc. 14th European Conf. on Cognitive Ergonomics (ECCE '07), ACM — notes the high data-ink (numerical) condition "falls outside of the realm of Tufte's principle, as simple numerical values are not graphs," confounding format with data-ink ratio.

#### V113 `a9c943c2` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For comparing or estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis of a grouped bar chart does NOT affect task performance — contradicting the blanket rule that bars must always start at zero."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-supported by the primary source. The supporting quote is taken word-for-word from the abstract of Long & Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024 (ACM DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102): "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance." Source quality matches claim strength: peer-reviewed, top-tier HCI venue, controlled experiment, grounded in Algebraic Visualization Design; current (2024, with a 2026 CHI follow-up "Taking Truncation to Task," DOI 10.1145/3772318.3790617 continuing the line). The seemingly-contradicting literature (Pandey et al. 2015; Correll et al. 2020 "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020) does NOT refute it: that work measures a different dependent variable — subjective PERCEIVED magnitude/bias (truncation exaggerates how big a difference looks) — whereas the claim concerns objective TASK PERFORMANCE (accuracy). Long & Kay measure both and reconcile them: for difference tasks performance is unaffected even as perception is biased. The "contradicts the blanket always-start-at-zero rule" framing is fair because one rigorous counterexample defeats a blanket rule, and the paper itself recommends truncation-caution only for ratio/percentage-change tasks (non-monotonic truncation impairs those), not universally. Important caveat (qualification, not refutation): the claim holds ONLY because it is narrowly scoped to the length-difference task and to objective performance; it must not be generalized to "truncation is harmless," since the same paper plus Pandey/Correll show truncation biases perceived magnitude and harms percentage-change/ratio reading. The claim as written does not make that overreach.
*Counter-source:* Pandey et al. 2015 and Correll et al. 2020 ("Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020, DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222) show truncation exaggerates perceived difference — but this measures subjective perception/bias, a different DV than the objective task performance the claim addresses, so it qualifies rather than refutes the claim.

#### V114 `a9e3856e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Encoding a quantity by the radius (rather than area) of a circle significantly exaggerated perceived magnitude (bubble mean response 1.71 control vs 2.71 deceptive; U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34), empirically confirming Tufte's graphical-integrity principle that the graphical area should map one-to-one to the underlying data."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/300726103_How_Deceptive_are_Deceptive_Visualizations (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by a strong primary source: Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov & Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?: An Empirical Analysis of Common Distortion Techniques," Proc. ACM CHI 2015 (peer-reviewed; also SSRN abstract_id=2566968).

(1) Statistics match the supplied quote exactly — the bubble-chart "area as quantity" treatment gave mean ranks 32.47 (Control) vs 48.52 (Deceptive), U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34. Direction is correct: the deceptive (radius/diameter-encoded) version yielded the higher rank, i.e., exaggerated perceived magnitude.

(2) The 1.71 vs 2.71 figures are NOT in the supplied quote, but they are internally corroborated: independent reporting of the paper states the response scale was 1–5 and that distorted charts produced responses "between 58.5% and 129.5% bigger than control," with the bubble chart showing the SMALLEST effect. 2.71/1.71 = 1.585 = exactly 58.5% bigger — matching the bubble chart's reported low-end effect. So the means are consistent with the paper, not fabricated.

(3) The manipulation described ("encoding a quantity by the radius rather than area") accurately characterizes the paper's bubble-chart "area as quantity" distortion: mapping the value to radius makes area grow as the square, exaggerating differences.

(4) The interpretation — confirming Tufte's graphical-integrity / Lie-Factor principle that graphical area should be proportional to the data — is a fair and standard reading. It is further reinforced by independent classic evidence (Flannery 1971; Cleveland & McGill 1984) that circle-size judgments follow area, not radius, making this one of the least controversial findings in graphical perception.

Minor caveats (do not refute): the 1.71/2.71 numbers come from the paper but not the cited quote; r=0.34 is a "medium" effect and the bubble effect was the smallest of the three exaggeration techniques tested; and strictly the study confirms that radius-encoding deceives rather than that area-encoding is perceptually exact. None of these overturn the core claim.

Source is primary, peer-reviewed, current (2015, foundational and still cited), and not marketing. Numbers verified against the quote and cross-checked against the paper's own "58.5% bigger" statistic.

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001). A post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other."

*Evidence:* Claim fully supported by the primary source (arXiv 1907.02035, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, CHI 2020), verified via ar5iv HTML. Experiment One (Sec. 3.1): truncation conditions were axis start "at 0, 25, or 50%" (matches claim's 0%/25%/50%); "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)"; "post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other." The quote and claim align exactly on truncation levels, all-three-differ, and direction. "Large" is justified: implied partial eta² = (89*2)/(89*2+76) ≈ 0.70 (very large); "statistically robust" supported by p<0.0001 and replication in Experiments 2 and 3. Minor: the DV is "perceived severity" (how severe/important differences seemed), which the claim glosses as "perceived severity of the effect size" — faithful, not a misread. No contradicting source found; corroborated by Pandey et al. 2015. The only inference beyond the quote is the word "large," which the F-statistic's implied effect size confirms.

#### V119 `aab9c6db` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Encoding a quantity by the radius (rather than area) of a circle significantly exaggerated perceived magnitude (bubble mean response 1.71 control vs 2.71 deceptive; U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34), empirically confirming Tufte's graphical-integrity principle that the graphical area should map one-to-one to the underlying data."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/300726103_How_Deceptive_are_Deceptive_Visualizations (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED VERBATIM against the primary source (Pandey, Rall, Satterthwaite, Nov, Bertini, "How Deceptive are Deceptive Visualizations?", ACM CHI 2015; full PDF extracted from cs.rpi.edu mirror). The paper text reads exactly: "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the 'area as quantity' distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble(Control) and Bubble(Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34." All four cited statistics match exactly. The "1.71 control vs 2.71 deceptive" mean responses are confirmed in the paper's Table 4 (5-item Likert): Bubble(Control)=1.71 [95%CI 1.45,1.98], Bubble(Deceptive)=2.71 [95%CI 2.27,3.15] — NOT fabricated. The manipulation direction is correct: paper states "Mapping a numeric quantity to the radius of a circle rather than area is another popular technique that leads to distortion"; deceptive=radius encoding, control=area encoding; deceptive scored significantly higher (p<0.001), i.e. exaggerated perceived magnitude. ONE MINOR OVERREACH (not refuting): the study proves radius-encoding deceives RELATIVE TO area-encoding, but does not independently prove area-encoding yields one-to-one perceptual accuracy — the perception literature (Stevens power law; Flannery 1971) shows circle areas are systematically underestimated. The claim's "should map one-to-one to the data" correctly describes Tufte's normative encoding rule (which control honored, deceptive violated), so it is consistent with, not contradicted by, the evidence. (Caution: an intermediate WebFetch summarizer hallucinated alternate values U=747/Z=3.51/r=0.44; those do NOT appear in the actual PDF — the claim's figures are the correct ones.) *Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the finding; the only "discrepancy" was a hallucinated WebFetch summary (U=747/Z=3.51/r=0.44) that does not exist in the primary PDF. Minor scholarly caveat from graphical-perception literature (Flannery 1971; Stevens' power law) refines but does not refute: circle areas are underestimated, so area-proportional encoding is not itself perceptually one-to-one.
