The supporting quote is verbatim accurate: "we did not find any difference in people's interpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink." Abstract also states: "people's accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts." The embellished charts were "taken directly from Holmes's work" vs. plain/minimalist versions — exactly the Holmes-style-vs-minimalist comparison the claim describes, and a direct test of the data-ink/chartjunk-removal premise. The claim is carefully scoped (it says embellishment "did NOT reduce interpretation accuracy," not that it improved it), so no overreach. Checklist: (1) supported by quote — yes, verbatim; (2) contradicting evidence — Stephen Few's "The Chartjunk Debate" (Perceptual Edge, 2011) critiques the study, but his objections target GENERALIZABILITY (n=20 university students; extreme Holmes examples; simple single-message charts; "Small, Unrepresentative Test Groups"), NOT the internal finding of equal accuracy — Few does not dispute that accuracy was equal in this experiment; (3) source quality — peer-reviewed CHI 2010, sufficient for the claim's strength; (4) not outdated — still the canonical empirical reference on chartjunk; later work (Li & Moacdieh 2014; Borgo et al. 2012) extends rather than overturns it; (5) not marketing. CAVEAT (does not refute): n=20, so the claim should be presented as "one controlled study found no accuracy penalty," not as proof that minimalism is useless in general — but the claim as written does not make that broader assertion.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (Perceptual Edge, 2011) — critiques generalizability (n=20, extreme Holmes examples, simple charts) but does NOT dispute the equal-accuracy finding itself.

#### V123 `abdc414f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In relative-comparison tasks (estimating one bar as a percentage of another), every tested embellishment EXCEPT bars extended below zero significantly increased reading error versus the plain baseline bar chart — including minor cosmetic changes like merely rounding the tops of bars. This directly counters any blanket claim that 'chartjunk'/embellishment is harmless to comprehension accuracy."

**Source:** https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a signiﬁcantly higher error rate."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary paper PDF (Skau, Harrison & Kosara, "An Evaluation of the Impact of Visual Embellishments in Bar Charts," Computer Graphics Forum 34(3):221-230, EuroVis 2015; kosara.net/papers/2015/Skau-EuroVis-2015.pdf). Table 3 (Relative Judgements, Mann-Whitney-Wilcoxon vs. baseline, Bonferroni α=0.0083): baseline m=1.43; capped p=0.0013*; overlapping p<0.001*; quadratic p<0.001*; rounded p<0.001*; triangle p<0.001*; extended p=0.097 (NOT significant). So exactly every embellishment EXCEPT 'extended' (bars below zero) was significantly worse, with rounded at p<0.001 — matching the claim. The supporting quote is verbatim from the paper's Discussion: "All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a significantly higher error rate." Source is peer-reviewed, primary, methodologically sound (Mechanical Turk N~100+, non-parametric tests, multiple-comparison correction); strength matches claim. The claim is correctly scoped to relative-comparison tasks and the "counters any blanket 'embellishment is harmless' claim" framing aligns with the authors' own statement that their results "qualify findings by Borkin and Borgo suggesting that memorability can be aided by embellishment." Caveat that does NOT refute: the all-but-one result holds only for relative judgments; for absolute judgments (Table 2) only quadratic was significant and rounded (p=0.009) was just above threshold — but the claim explicitly limits itself to relative-comparison tasks, so no overreach.

#### V124 `abdf078e` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For comparing or estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis of a grouped bar chart does NOT affect task performance — contradicting the blanket rule that bars must always start at zero."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for comparing and estimating the difference between the lengths of two bars, truncating the y-axis does not affect task performance."

*Evidence:* The narrow empirical statement is true and primary-sourced (Long et al., "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102): for the specific task of comparing/estimating the difference between two bar LENGTHS, truncation does not affect task performance. But the claim's second clause — "contradicting the blanket rule that bars must always start at zero" — is an overreach unsupported by the quote and contradicted on three counts. (1) The SAME paper qualifies it: it distinguishes monotonic vs non-monotonic truncation, finds non-monotonic truncation performs much worse, and finds truncation biases ratio/percentage-change judgments — so it does NOT conclude bars need not start at zero; effects are task-dependent. (2) The zero-baseline rule guards against misleading viewers about magnitude/effect size, not accuracy on a side-by-side length task. Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222) found truncated axes make viewers substantially overestimate effect sizes, and the bias persists — the exact harm the rule addresses. (3) Pandey et al., "How Deceptive Are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015 found viewers rate truncated bar charts as showing greater differences than untruncated ones (robust distortion). So a single-task accuracy result cannot "contradict" the start-at-zero convention; the inference conflates perceptual accuracy with perceived-magnitude deception. Accepting the claim as worded would wrongly relabel "start at zero" as a debunked myth.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (DOI 10.1145/3313831.3376222); Pandey et al., "How Deceptive Are Deceptive Visualizations?", CHI 2015; and the source paper's own non-monotonic-truncation and ratio-task findings (Long et al., CHI 2024).

#### V125 `abf36655` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The apparent 'high data-ink' speed advantage is confounded: the high data-ink display was purely numerical/digital while the medium and low displays were graphical gauges, so the result may reflect the superiority of digital readouts for reading exact values rather than data-ink ratio per se. This undercuts using the study as clean proof that removing non-data ink improves comprehension."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0169814102000744 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the high data-ink display was purely numerical (or "digital") while the medium and low data-ink displays were graphical in nature"

*Evidence:* Source confirmed as Di Blasio & Bisantz (2002), "A comparison of the effects of data-ink ratio on performance with dynamic displays in a monitoring task," Int. J. Industrial Ergonomics (S0169814102000744). The supporting quote is verbatim-accurate: the study found high data-ink = fastest reaction times BUT "the high data-ink display was purely numerical (or 'digital') while the medium and low data-ink displays were graphical in nature." The claim's inference (that this confounds digital-format superiority with data-ink ratio, undercutting clean proof) is logically valid and independently corroborated by a citing review: "It could be argued that the study's high data-ink condition falls outside of the realm of Tufte's principle, as simple numerical values are not graphs." The study's own authors disclaim it as clean proof, concluding "effects of ink are highly conditional on the features of the graph and task" and "simple rules like Tufte's will not suffice." No source disputes the confound; several affirm it. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed study + corroborating reviews) matches the claim's strength. Only minor imprecision: the task was out-of-range detection, loosely described as "reading exact values," and the displays were process-monitoring graphics described as "gauges" — neither undermines the confound. Not outdated, not marketing.

#### V126 `abfcd8fe` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled within-subjects experiment (n=40), y-axis truncation inflated perceived effect size equally for bar charts and line charts — there was no significant difference between the two chart types. This contradicts the popular rule (Cairo, Bergstrom & West, Tufte-for-time-series) that the zero-baseline requirement applies to bars but exempts line charts."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results fail to support our second hypothesis. There was no significant effect of visualization design on perceived effect size (F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50). [...] This qualitative bias occurs in both line charts and bar charts, as well as in bar charts that visually indicate either broken axes or the continuation of bars beyond the bounds of the chart."

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED via the primary source (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020; arXiv 1907.02035) cross-checked through two independent fetches plus the author's own blog. (1) Experiment One was n=40, within-subjects, and directly manipulated visualization type = bar vs. line. (2) The cited statistic maps exactly to the bar-vs-line comparison: the paper's "second hypothesis" was verbatim "we expected that Bar charts would be more greatly impacted by truncation than line charts," and the ANOVA on visualization design returned no significant effect, F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50. The df (1,38)=n−2 for n=40 within-subjects confirms it is NOT a misread of a different factor (e.g., the broken-axis indicator). (3) The paper states truncation biased BOTH chart types: "This qualitative bias occurs in both line charts and bar charts, as well as in bar charts that visually indicate either broken axes or the continuation of bars." (4) It explicitly contradicts the bar-needs-zero/line-exempt rule — Correll names the "Line Chart Exceptionalists" and concludes they "seemed to be wrong... that didn't seem to matter for how our participants decoded the chart." Attribution to Cairo, Bergstrom & West, and Tufte-for-time-series for the line-exemption convention is accurate. Adversarial search found no contradicting study; the 2024 CHI follow-up ("To Cut or Not To Cut?") corroborates and refines rather than overturns it. Only minor caveat: "equally" interprets a null result that could be underpowered at n=40, but the authors themselves report "the same impact" across conditions, so the claim is faithful. Supporting quote genuinely supports the claim; source quality (peer-reviewed primary) matches the claim's strength.

#### V127 `ac33e5bf` — **upheld** · confidence: high

#### V128 `ace2404e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Despite finding that truncation reliably exaggerates effect size, the authors explicitly reject the universal rule that all charts must start at zero and reject the binary 'honest vs dishonest' framing; they argue there is no domain-agnostic ground truth for how large an effect should look, so the designer must choose the y-axis range to match the meaningful effect size."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "we resist the interpretation of our experimental results to mean that, as Huff suggests, all charts with quantitative axes should include 0. [...] There is no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe, important, or meaningful an effect size ought to be. [...] We reject the unequivocal dichotomy of 'honest' and 'dishonest' charts."

*Evidence:* Source verified as primary peer-reviewed paper: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (arXiv:1907.02035). All three legs of the claim are directly supported. (1) Found truncation exaggerates effect size: abstract states "Bar charts with y-axes that don't begin at zero can visually exaggerate effect sizes" and the effect "is persistent across visualization designs, even for designs with explicit visual cues that indicate truncation"; author's own summary confirms "the higher you start y-axis... the bigger a deal people thought the trend was." (2) Reject the universal start-at-zero rule: matches supplied quote "we resist the interpretation... all charts with quantitative axes should include 0"; author: "don't fall back on defaults unthinkingly." (3) Reject honest/dishonest dichotomy + no domain-agnostic ground truth: full-text search extraction returns verbatim that the paper "rejects the unequivocal dichotomy of 'honest' and 'dishonest' charts" and "no a priori, domain-agnostic ground truth for how severe, important, or meaningful an effect size ought to be." (4) Designer must match axis range to meaningful effect size: abstract says "designers should consider the scale of the meaningful effect sizes and variation they intend to communicate"; author: "the designer must actively take responsibility for choosing the visual size of the effect in the chart." The "despite finding X, they reject Y" framing faithfully captures the paper's actual thesis; no overreach found. Limitation: could not extract raw PDF text locally (no pdftotext/pypdf; ACM HTML returned 403), but arXiv abstract, the author's first-person Medium summary, and an independent full-text search extraction all corroborate the exact supplied quotes.

Abstract: accuracy "was no worse than for plain charts," recall after 2-3 weeks "significantly better," and "our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach to chart design." The claim is scoped precisely to "did NOT reduce accuracy," not "improves comprehension," so it survives those critiques. Source is primary, peer-reviewed; strength matches.

I don't think so" (2010) — both critique the study's generalizability (n=20, professionally designed Holmes charts, whether long-term recall reflects data vs. theme), but NEITHER disputes the specific equal-interpretation-accuracy finding the claim rests on.

#### V135 `adc11a3f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled crowdsourced experiment (n=250 for the exaggeration study), truncating a bar chart's axis (non-zero baseline) significantly inflated the perceived difference between bars: mean Likert response rose from 1.45 (control) to 2.77 (deceptive), a statistically significant effect (Mann-Whitney U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003, r=0.37; p<0.001 overall). I extracted the paper text and confirmed EVERY figure in the claim, exactly:

1. n=250: "We recruited 250 unique participants for this study and assigned one of the six treatments to each of them." (This is the message-exaggeration study; the full paper recruited 330 total, with the rest in the separate message-reversal study. So "n=250 for the exaggeration study" is precisely correct, not a misread of the 330 total.)

2. Likert means — Table 2 (5-item Likert, min=1 max=5), "how much" question: "Bar (Deceptive) Truncated Axis 2.77, 95% CI [2.26, 3.28]" and "Bar (Control) Truncated Axis 1.45, 95% CI [1.27, 1.62]." Matches 1.45 -> 2.77 exactly.

3. Mann-Whitney U (one-tailed): "the mean ranks of Bar (Control) and Bar (Deceptive) were 31.08 and 48.60, respectively; U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003, r=0.37." Matches the supporting quote and the claim verbatim. p=0.0003 < 0.001.

4. Direction/interpretation: paper frames "truncated axis" (non-zero baseline) as a distortion technique causing "message exaggeration" — participants "perceived the underlying message in its exaggerated form, and responded higher on the 5-Likert scale." Treating this as empirical support for the bars-start-at-zero convention is the standard, fair reading. Later work (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri 2020, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?") reconfirms truncation inflates perceived effect size — it qualifies the blanket rule for line charts but does NOT contradict this bar-chart finding. Source is current/durable, not outdated.

Minor wording note (not material): "p<0.001 overall" is loose phrasing — the cited p=0.0003 is the bar-specific one-tailed result, and the test is one-tailed; neither weakens the claim.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the bar-chart finding. Correll et al. 2020 (CHI, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?") reconfirms truncation exaggerates perceived effect but argues against an absolute zero-baseline rule for line charts — a qualification of scope, not a refutation of the bar-chart result cited here.

#### V136 `add82c6b` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "we did not find any difference in people's in terpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink."

*Evidence:* The claim accurately reports Bateman et al. (2010), "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," ACM CHI 2010. The supporting quote is the paper's own language and matches the claim exactly: the study found no difference in interpretation accuracy between embellished (Nigel Holmes-style) and minimalist charts despite the embellished charts containing far more non-data-ink. Independent sources confirm the abstract: "people's accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts, and their recall after a two-to-three-week gap was significantly better." This directly tests Tufte's data-ink/chartjunk-removal premise, as the authors themselves framed it. The claim does NOT overreach — it states embellishment was not WORSE for accuracy (correct), not that it was better. Source quality is sufficient: primary peer-reviewed CHI paper. Skeptical checks: critiques exist (Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate," perceptualedge.com; Kosara, eagereyes.org; Gelman, statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu; Li & Moacdieh 2014 "Is chartjunk useful? An extended examination," HFES) but they attack methodology and generalizability (n=20 sample, "plain" charts possibly poorly designed, did not test reading precise data values, memorability may reflect ignoring the data) — none contradict the measured no-accuracy-difference result the claim cites. The claim is current/foundational (still cited in 2024 surveys), well-supported, and source-matched.
*Counter-source:* Critiques qualify but do not refute: Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (perceptualedge.com/articles/visual_business_intelligence/the_chartjunk_debate.pdf); Robert Kosara, "Chart Junk Considered Useful After All" (eagereyes.org); Andrew Gelman (statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu, 2010); Li & Moacdieh (2014) HFES "Is chartjunk useful? An extended examination." These dispute generalizability (small n=20, strawman "plain" charts, untested precise-value reading), not the no-accuracy-difference finding itself.

#### V137 `addfd34a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Greater y-axis truncation produced significantly greater perceived severity of the effect size, with all three truncation levels (axis starting at 0%, 25%, 50%) significantly different from each other; the effect was large and statistically robust."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001). A post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other."

*Evidence:* Verified directly against the primary source (Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", ACM CHI 2020, arXiv:1907.02035) via ar5iv HTML rendering plus a corroborating WebSearch. All three load-bearing components of the claim are verbatim-confirmed: (a) truncation levels — "Truncation Level (3 levels): where the y-axis of the visualization began: either at 0, 25%, or 50%" (matches "0%, 25%, 50%"); (b) direction + statistic — "increased y-axis truncation results in increased perceived severity (F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001)"; (c) pairwise differences — "A post-hoc pairwise t-test with a Bonferroni correction confirmed that the perceived severity of all three levels of truncation were significantly different from each other." Source is primary and peer-reviewed (CHI 2020), appropriate for the claim's strength; the finding is corroborated, not contradicted, by related work (Pandey et al. 2015; Nature Scientific Reports 2022 COVID-forecast study). One minor caveat that does NOT refute: the paper reports F/p but no standardized effect size (no eta-squared or Cohen's d), so "the effect was large" is an inference — but F(2,76)=89 implies partial eta-squared approx 0.70, a very large effect, and the paper independently reports the effect persisted across bar/line designs and solicitation methods, supporting "statistically robust." The claim is a faithful, well-sourced characterization.

#### V138 `ae0324ac` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "participants were able to remember significantly more about the embellished charts after a gap longer than 12 days, suggesting that the addition of strong visual images does help people to remember both the topic and details of the chart."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully reports the primary source's own central finding. The supporting quote is verbatim from the actual Bateman et al. 2010 paper ("Useful Junk?", CHI 2010, ACM): "participants were able to remember significantly more about the embellished charts after a gap longer than 12 days, suggesting that the addition of strong visual images does help people to remember both the topic and details of the chart." I extracted the full paper text and confirmed every load-bearing detail: N=20 participants (9M/11F, 18-40), 14 charts taken directly from Nigel Holmes' work with matched plain versions, and "long-term participants scheduled their second part of the study for two to three weeks later" — so "gap longer than 12 days, described elsewhere as two-to-three weeks" is accurate. The paper measured recall of subject/categories/trend (i.e., topic + details) and found these significantly higher for Holmes charts in the long-term condition but NOT in the immediate (5-min) condition — exactly what the claim states ("after a long delay"). Independent corroboration: a WebSearch of secondary sources (Kosara/eagereyes, ResearchGate) reports the same result, and critically, the study's leading critic Stephen Few ("The Chartjunk Debate," 2011) restates the finding himself rather than refuting it: readers "will also better recall the basic subject of the chart and ... pattern in the data two or three weeks later, but no better soon afterwards." The published critiques (Few 2011; Gelman blog 2010; Li & Moacdieh 2014) dispute the GENERALIZABILITY of the result — small N=20, Holmes embellishments were semantically relevant rather than random "chartjunk," and the plain comparison charts were arguably weak — but none dispute that this specific long-term-recall result occurred. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed CHI paper, ~600+ citations) matches the claim's narrow strength, which is about what THIS study found, not a universal law. Minor imprecision only: "decorative imagery" understates that Holmes images were topic-relevant, not arbitrary decoration; this does not falsify the claim. The claim is well-supported and current.
*Counter-source:* Critiques exist but corroborate rather than refute the finding: Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (Perceptual Edge, 2011) restates the recall result himself; Li & Moacdieh, "Is chart junk useful? An extended examination" (HFES 2014); Andrew Gelman blog (2010) — all challenge generalizability (N=20, Holmes images semantically relevant not generic chartjunk, weak plain comparators), not whether the study observed significantly better long-term recall for embellished charts.

Every element of the claim is confirmed:

(1) NUMBERS EXACT. The relative-judgment results table reads: baseline 1.43, capped 1.70, overlapping 1.82, quadratic 2.33, rounded 1.86, triangle 1.85, extended 1.59 (all SDs ~1.7-1.85). The claim's "2.33 vs baseline 1.43" matches the paper verbatim.

(2) "LARGEST OF ANY EMBELLISHMENT" CONFIRMED. For the relative task, quadratic (2.33) is unambiguously the highest error of all seven conditions (next worst: rounded 1.86). Paper prose: "quadratic produced much higher error overall (m=2.33; sd=1.78)" while rounded (1.86) and triangle (1.85) "performed similarly." Quadratic is also the only embellishment that was significantly worse than baseline on the absolute task too (1.70 vs 1.41), so it is the worst embellishment overall.

(3) TUFTE FRAMING IS THE AUTHORS' OWN. Conclusion states verbatim: "Confirming the hypothesis of Tufte and others, quadratically-scaled bars led to large errors in relative judgements, even when compared to similar embellishment styles (i.e. triangles, overlapping triangles)." The paper explicitly ties the quadratic-area condition to Tufte's classic 2D-area distortion example ("Shrinking Family Doctor," cited [TGM83] = Tufte 1983, The Visual Display of Quantitative Information).

(4) SOURCE QUALITY MATCHES CLAIM STRENGTH. Peer-reviewed controlled user study (Amazon Mechanical Turk), top-tier venue (EuroVis/CGF). Primary source; not marketing or forum speculation. Widely cited; finding is not outdated.

The supporting quote alone does not state the "2.33/1.43" figures or "largest of any," but I confirmed both directly from the paper's data tables, so the fuller claim is fully backed by the primary source rather than an overreach of the snippet.

Minor, non-refuting caveat for the skill author: the study tested 2D AREA scaling only, not 3D VOLUME, so "area/volume" reflects Tufte's warning category (which covers both) with the area portion directly tested and the volume portion by extension. Also note the same paper elsewhere pushes back on Tufte's broader data-ink/chartjunk stance ("[reducing data/ink] fails to address the real salient features of a chart") — so this is a narrow confirmation of one specific Tufte warning, not a blanket endorsement of Tufte. Neither caveat contradicts the claim as stated.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The only nuances (volume not directly tested; the paper disputes Tufte's chartjunk view elsewhere) qualify scope but do not refute the area-encoding finding, which the authors themselves state confirms Tufte.

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/300726103_How_Deceptive_are_Deceptive_Visualizations (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "for the treatments with bubble-charts where the "area as quantity" distortion plays a role, the mean ranks of Bubble (Control) and Bubble (Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52, respectively; U = 1121, Z = 3.08, p = 0.0007, r = 0.34."

Table 2: Bubble (Control) Area-as-Quantity mean response = 1.71, 95% CI [1.45,1.98]; Bubble (Deceptive) = 2.71, 95% CI [2.27,3.15] — matches "1.71 control vs 2.71 deceptive." The distortion mechanism matches: "when the quantity is mapped to the radius of the circle (in the deceptive visualization)... When the quantity is mapped to the area, as in the control condition, the visualization is less susceptible to deception." The Tufte attribution is the paper's own framing: "a one-to-one mapping between the data and the graphical area is least prone to distortion. This is also one of the six graphical integrity principles suggested by Tufte." The response measured perceived magnitude ("how much better do you think is [A] compared to [B]" on a 1-5 Likert scale; higher = exaggerated message), so "significantly exaggerated perceived magnitude" is accurate. A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024 (DOI 10.1145/3613904.3642102), peer-reviewed, Northwestern MU Collective. The claim scopes itself to exactly the task the quote addresses (length-difference estimation between two bars) and frames the result as contradicting only the BLANKET "always start at zero" rule — which the paper itself explicitly re-evaluates. No overreach: the claim does not generalize to all tasks. Checklist: (1) quote supports it literally; (2) prior contradicting work (Correll et al. 2020 CHI "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?"; Pandey et al. 2015) concerns distortion of PERCEIVED magnitude/effect size, a different measure than difference-estimation accuracy, so it does not refute the scoped claim — the 2024 study was designed to disentangle these; (3) source quality (top-tier peer-reviewed HCI venue) matches claim strength; (4) current (2024); (5) not marketing. Important caveat the claim correctly does NOT violate: the SAME paper found truncation DOES worsen performance for percentage-change/relative-growth judgments, especially non-monotonic truncation — so the result is genuinely task-dependent and must not be generalized to "truncation is always fine." As written, the claim stands.
*Counter-source:* Correll, Bertini, Franconeri 2020, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?" CHI (arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035) — shows truncation distorts perceived effect size (83.5% truncation effect), but on subjective magnitude perception, NOT difference-estimation accuracy; does not contradict the scoped claim.

#### V142 `ae864834` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Explicit visual cues that a y-axis is truncated — broken-axis glyphs and gradient-fill bars — did NOT reduce the exaggeration of perceived effect size. This undercuts the common guideline that marking the axis break (e.g. a 'torn paper' or broken-axis symbol) makes truncation acceptable/honest."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.02035 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results fail to support our first hypothesis: there was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05). [...] Merely indicating that truncation has occurred, even in a prominent and unambiguous way, may not be sufficient to 'de-bias' viewers of truncated charts."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, NOT REFUTED. Primary source: Correll, Bertini & Franconeri, "Truncating the Y-Axis: Threat or Menace?", CHI 2020 (Honorable Mention; arXiv 1907.02035, 2019). The supporting quote is verbatim-accurate (confirmed via ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org rendering): "there was no significant difference between perceived severity among visualization designs (F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05)" and "Merely indicating that truncation has occurred, even in a prominent and unambiguous way, may not be sufficient to 'de-bias' viewers of truncated charts." The rejected hypothesis was that "visual designs with non-zero axes that indicate y-axis breaks or continuations would be perceived as having smaller effect sizes." The paper tested exactly the two cues named in the claim — broken-axis glyphs and gradient-fill bars — and found neither significantly reduced perceived exaggeration, directly supporting the claim that this undercuts the guideline that marking the axis break makes truncation honest. CORROBORATION: first author Michael Correll's own Medium summary: "none of the design interventions tested actually worked"; "just indicating that something screwy is going on with the y-axis was not sufficient to remove the impact." NO CONTRADICTING EVIDENCE: the 2024 follow-up "To Cut or Not To Cut?" (Long et al., CHI 2024) complements, not refutes — it studies objective task performance and treats Correll et al.'s subjective effect-size finding as a distinct, still-valid result. Source quality (peer-reviewed CHI, top-tier vis researchers from Tableau/NYU/Northwestern) matches the claim's strength; not outdated; not marketing. NUANCE FLAG: F(2,60)=3.1, p=0.05 is right at the conventional alpha threshold (borderline), but the authors themselves interpret it as failing to support de-biasing, so the claim reports the authors' own stated conclusion rather than overreaching.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The 2024 CHI follow-up "To Cut or Not To Cut?" (Long, Franconeri et al., dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102) extends but does not contradict; it confirms subjective effect-size persistence while quantifying objective-task performance separately.

Higher score = more memorable (hit-rate-based memorability metric), so the direction in the claim is correct.

The gloss "low data-ink ratio = more chartjunk" and "good data-ink ratio = minimalist" is the paper's own Tufte framing (the paper cites Tufte and Bateman 2010 "Useful Junk?" directly; I confirmed the Bateman reference in the paper's bibliography). The characterization "refuting the authors' own hypothesis that minimalist designs are more memorable" is consistent with the quote ("refutes our fourth and fifth hypotheses," which concerned data-ink ratio and visual density).

Scope check (why it is NOT an overclaim): the claim is narrowly about MEMORABILITY (recognition that you've seen the image), and it says so explicitly. It does not assert chartjunk improves comprehension or effectiveness — which would be unsupported. #### V146 `af56fc20` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors' evidence-based recommendation: when a task is unaffected by truncation (e.g., comparing gaps) a designer may truncate to align visuals to the task, but for ratio/growth tasks they should use monotonic truncation rather than no truncation or non-monotonic truncation."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3613904.3642102 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "if the tasks examine quantities that are affected by truncation (e.g., Compare Ratios and Estimate Ratio), then we suggest performing monotonic truncat[ion]"

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary source verbatim (Sheng Long & Matthew Kay, "To Cut or Not To Cut? A Systematic Exploration of Y-Axis Truncation," CHI 2024, 10.1145/3613904.3642102; full text from mucollective.northwestern.edu/files/2024-to_cut_or_not.pdf). The claim is half-right and half-wrong; the wrong half is material. CORRECT half: the paper does say that for tasks "unaffected by truncation (e.g., Compare Gaps and Estimate Gap), the designer can truncate the axis to... make the visuals more task-aligned." WRONG half (the claim's distinguishing assertion): the claim says ratio/growth tasks "should use monotonic truncation RATHER THAN no truncation." The paper says the opposite — it recommends EITHER, treating them as equivalent: "if the tasks examine quantities that are affected by truncation (e.g., Compare Ratios and Estimate Ratio), then we suggest performing monotonic truncations OR no truncations." And earlier: "no truncation, in general, performs similarly to monotonic truncation, and both perform much better than non-monotonic truncation. Thus, we suggest that the designer perform no truncation OR monotonic truncation for these tasks." The claim's own supporting quote was truncated at "monotonic truncat[ion]," omitting the immediately following "...or no truncations," which is precisely the clause that contradicts "rather than no truncation." So the claim misreads the source by elevating monotonic above no-truncation when the paper recommends them as equally good and explicitly co-equal. Source quality is high (primary, peer-reviewed CHI 2024, current), but the claim does not faithfully represent it.
*Counter-source:* Long & Kay, CHI 2024 (10.1145/3613904.3642102), Discussion: "we suggest performing monotonic truncations or no truncations" and "we suggest that the designer perform no truncation or monotonic truncation for these tasks" — both options recommended equally, contradicting the claim's "monotonic rather than no truncation."

#### V147 `af6e1ea9` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In relative-comparison tasks (estimating one bar as a percentage of another), every tested embellishment EXCEPT bars extended below zero significantly increased reading error versus the plain baseline bar chart — including minor cosmetic changes like merely rounding the tops of bars. This directly counters any blanket claim that 'chartjunk'/embellishment is harmless to comprehension accuracy."

**Source:** https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a signiﬁcantly higher error rate."

*Evidence:* Claim accurately represents Skau, Harrison & Kosara (2015), "An Evaluation of the Impact of Visual Embellishments in Bar Charts," Computer Graphics Forum 34(3):221-230 (EuroVis 2015) — a peer-reviewed primary source confirmed via Wiley (doi 10.1111/cgf.12634), Eurographics digital library, and the authors' own pages. The study tested a plain baseline plus embellishments (rounded tops, triangle, capped, overlap, extended-below-baseline, quadratic). The supporting quote ("All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. Even small changes, for example the rounded bar, produced a significantly higher error rate") fully supports the claim and is the authors' own description. Independent secondary sources corroborate the substance: "bars that extend below the x-axis performed roughly equivalently to the standard bar chart," and that for relative tasks the extended bar was the sole exception (capped was an additional exception only for ABSOLUTE tasks — and the claim correctly restricts itself to "relative-comparison tasks"). The interpretive conclusion (counters blanket "embellishment is harmless to comprehension") is fair and narrowly limited to comprehension/reading accuracy. The usual counterweight, Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?", does not refute it: it measures memorability/recall of cartoonish Holmes-style charts, a different construct, and did not test Skau's micro-embellishments. No credible source disputes the finding; it was reinforced by the same team's 2017 pictorial-bar-charts follow-up. Limitation: the eagereyes.org primary page returned HTTP 403, so the exact verbatim quote could not be independently retrieved, but its substance is corroborated by multiple independent sources.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source. Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" (CHI) is sometimes cited as a counterweight to chartjunk-harm claims, but it measures memorability/recall of cartoonish charts, not precise relative-magnitude reading of micro-embellishments, so it does not refute this Skau-specific accuracy finding.

#### V148 `afab3091` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The authors explicitly state that Tufte's negative, data-ink-ratio-driven view of chart embellishment rests on a general lack of empirical evidence, yet has nonetheless driven the visualization field's minimalism culture — and note the data-ink ratio taken to its logical end would absurdly reduce a bar to a single-pixel line."

**Source:** https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Despite a general lack of empirical evidence supporting this negative view on chart embellishments, it has driven a culture of design simpliﬁcation and sterilization in the data visualization community."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary source full text (Skau, Harrison & Kosara, "An Evaluation of the Impact of Visual Embellishments in Bar Charts," Computer Graphics Forum / Proc. EuroVis 34(3):221-230, 2015; DOI 10.1111/cgf.12634), extracted via PyMuPDF to /tmp/skau_text.txt. The intro states verbatim: "Despite a general lack of empirical evidence supporting this negative view on chart embellishments, it has driven a culture of design simplification and sterilization in the data visualization community." Two sentences later: "Tufte's writings encourage this approach of reducing the data/ink ratio. This may be a good rule of thumb, but it fails to address the real salient features of a chart. By this measure, bars in a bar chart would be better if they were reduced to a single-pixel wide line." Every element of the claim is directly supported: (1) "general lack of empirical evidence" = exact; (2) attribution to Tufte's data-ink-ratio-driven negative view = paper names Tufte as "one of the major drivers" and ties it to "reducing the data/ink ratio"; (3) "driven the minimalism culture" = paper's "driven a culture of design simplification and sterilization"; (4) "single-pixel line" reductio = verbatim "single-pixel wide line," framed as the rule's failure (a logical-extreme/absurdity argument). The supplied supporting quote matches the paper exactly. Source is a peer-reviewed primary venue, matching the claim's strength (a report of what the authors stated). Not outdated as a report of the 2015 authors' framing; no contradicting source exists since the claim accurately reports the authors' own words.
*Counter-source:* None found. The claim reports what the authors literally wrote; the primary PDF confirms it verbatim, including the "single-pixel wide line" reductio (Skau et al. 2015, intro, p.221-222).

#### V149 `afc23976` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "High visual density was associated with significantly higher memorability than low visual density (very dense M=1.83 vs low M=1.28, p<0.001), contradicting the hypothesis that low-density displays are more memorable — again as image recognition, not comprehension."

**Source:** http://olivalab.mit.edu/Papers/Borkin_etal_MemorableVisualization_TVCG2013.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "we observed a statistically signiﬁcant effect of visual density on memorability scores with a high visual density rating of “3” (M = 1.83), i.e., very dense, being greater than a low visual density rating of “1” (M = 1.28,t(115) = 6.08, p < 0.001)"

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-accurate to the primary source. WebSearch returned the identical sentence from Borkin et al. 2013 "What Makes a Visualization Memorable?" (IEEE TVCG, PubMed 24051797): high visual density rating "3" (M=1.83, very dense) > low rating "1" (M=1.28), t(115)=6.08, p<0.001. Numbers, direction ("greater than"), and significance all match. Methodology confirmed via second search: the 2013 study measured memorability as RECOGNITION (hit rate / false-alarm rate in a repeat-detection game), not comprehension — so the claim's caveat "again as image recognition, not comprehension" is accurate and load-bearing. The apparent counter-evidence (storybench summary saying low/clutter density is LESS memorable, measured by "describe the visualization in detail") actually describes a DIFFERENT study — Borkin et al. 2016 "Beyond Memorability: Visualization Recognition and Recall," which measured comprehension/recall. The claim explicitly excludes that axis, so it is not contradicted. Visual density was a crowdsourced rating (observational), and the claim correctly says "associated with" rather than asserting causation. Source is peer-reviewed TVCG/InfoVis — quality matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* Secondary summaries (storybench.org; one arXiv-derived snippet) state low/minimal visual density aids memorability — but these describe comprehension/recall ("describe in detail") from the 2016 "Beyond Memorability" follow-up, a different measure the claim explicitly excludes via its "recognition not comprehension" caveat. Not a valid refutation of the recognition finding.

#### V150 `aff087dc` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Visual embellishments (chartjunk) improved memorability — participants better remembered the information depicted in embellished visualizations, directly contradicting the pure data-ink/minimalism prescription that non-data ink should always be removed."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization."

*Evidence:* CLAIM HOLDS. Source correctly identified: PMID 26357185 = Borgo, Abdul-Rahman, Mohamed, Grant, Reppa, Floridi & Chen (2012), "An Empirical Study on Using Visual Embellishments in Visualization," IEEE TVCG 18(12) — a primary, peer-reviewed controlled study using a dual-task paradigm. The supporting quote ("visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted in visualization") is verbatim from the abstract, so the claim is directly supported by the quote, not an overreach. Independent corroboration: Bateman et al. 2010 (CHI), "Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts," found embellished (Holmes-style) charts were recalled significantly better after a 2-3 week delay with no loss in interpretation accuracy — a separate study reaching the same memorability conclusion. Both directly challenge the strong/universal form of the data-ink "remove all non-data ink" prescription, which is exactly how this literature is framed (the Bateman title is a deliberate rebuttal to "chartjunk"). QUALIFICATIONS (do not refute, but scope the claim): (a) Borgo's own results are mixed — embellishments hurt visual-search SPEED and had a complex/inconsistent effect on concept comprehension; the memory benefit is real but is one of several effects, so embellishments are not "universally better." The claim is correctly scoped to memorability only and does not overstate this. (b) Stephen Few's critique ("The Chartjunk Debate," 2011) argues the Bateman comparison charts were artificially impoverished and that what was remembered better was the topic/gist rather than precise data values — this qualifies the practical significance but does not dispute that embellishment aided memory. Net: the memorability finding is well-replicated across at least two independent controlled studies; source quality (peer-reviewed IEEE TVCG primary study) matches the claim strength; not marketing; 2012 but foundational and still standard in the field.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (Visual Business Intelligence Newsletter, 2011) — critiques but does not refute the memorability finding; Borgo et al. 2012 itself documents speed/comprehension costs that scope the claim to memorability only.

#### `a3c60293` (Run A — canonical (107 agents))
```json
{
  "summary": "Tufte's principles split into three empirical tiers. (1) His perceptual graphical-integrity claims are the best-supported: controlled studies confirm that truncated/non-zero axes and area-as-quantity encodings reliably inflate perceived magnitude (Pandey 2015, Correll 2020, Yang 2021), and that break-mark \"honesty\" cues do NOT remove the distortion. (2) His data-ink/chartjunk minimalism is NOT proven as a comprehension rule and is partly contradicted: across Bateman 2010, Borgo 2012, Li & Moacdieh 2014 and Inbar 2007, embellishment (\"chartjunk\") leaves interpretation accuracy intact, can improve long-term recall, only costs visual-search speed, and people prefer non-minimalist charts — so \"remove all non-data ink to aid understanding\" is convention/taste, not a proven law. (3) The famous normative rules are now relabeled task-dependent: \"bars must always start at zero\" is no longer treated as universal — truncation inflates *perceived* effect size but does NOT degrade *accuracy* on additive-difference tasks (Long & Kay 2024), so the rule depends on task and intent. Small multiples beat a single dense chart only conditionally (large visual span / dispersed comparison; Javed 2010, Gleicher 2018), and direct/redundant labeling measurably improves comprehension (Kopp 2018). Several signature Tufte ideas — sparkline effectiveness, a data-density legibility ceiling, smallest-effective-difference/layering, micro-macro reading, and tables-beat-graphics-for-small-data — produced no controlled evidence in this pass and remain aesthetic/convention.",

The 'embellishment helps' result is specific to Nigel Holmes-style decoration against sometimes-suboptimal plain comparators — do not generalize to 'all decoration is good.'\n\nINTERPRETATION CAUTIONS: Inbar 2007 measures aesthetic PREFERENCE, not comprehension. Bateman's 'value message' recall blends persuasion with comprehension. Area-encoding harm rests largely on one recent controlled study (Pandey 2015) plus classic perception ranking. The truncation literature separates two different dependent variables (perceived effect size vs task accuracy); conflating them produces the false impression that the studies disagree — they do not.\n\nTIME-SENSITIVITY: The normative 'start at zero' question is actively evolving (Long & Kay 2024 is recent; a CHI 2026 follow-up 'Taking Truncation to Task' reportedly corroborates task-dependence and that direct data labels mitigate truncation error). Expect this specific rule to keep shifting toward 'task-dependent.'",
  "openQuestions": [
    "Sparklines: is there ANY controlled evidence that word-sized inline graphics outperform a small table or a full-size chart for a real task? None surfaced — the design rules are currently unvalidated Tufte taste.",
    "Direct labeling vs separate legend: Kopp 2018 validates redundant NUMERIC labels, but does placing series labels AT the line/element (direct labeling) specifically beat a separate keyed legend in controlled studies? Not established here.",
    "Data density: is there a measured legibility ceiling, and does Tufte's 'data density index' predict comprehension at all, or is denser-is-better purely aesthetic? No empirical evidence found either way.",
    "Tables vs graphics for small datasets: does Tufte's claim that tables often win hold up in any controlled comparison? No supporting study surfaced.",
    "Bars-vs-lines zero baseline: the clean dichotomy (bars must start at zero, lines/dot plots need not) failed adversarial verification — what does the current evidence actually say about zero baselines for LINE charts specifically?"
  ]
}
```

```json
    {
      "url": "http://users.umiacs.umd.edu/~elm/projects/multilinevis/multilinevis.pdf",
      "title": "Javed, McDonnel & Elmqvist (2010), \"Graphical Perception of Multiple Time Series\" — IEEE TVCG 16(6):927–934",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The cornerstone controlled experiment for rule (3). Tests superimposed (shared-space) line graphs vs juxtaposed/separated techniques (small multiples, horizon graphs) across comparison, slope, and discrimination tasks. Key empirical result: superimposed wins for LOCAL tasks (values close together / small visual span); separated small-multiple-style designs win for tasks spanning a LARGE visual range or many series. This is the nuanced 'it depends on task' evidence that contradicts any blanket 'always use small multiples' or 'always overlay' rule."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0747563218302012",
      "title": "\"When cognitive fit outweighs cognitive load: Redundant data labels in charts increase accuracy and speed of information extraction\" — Computers in Human Behavior (2018)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Direct empirical support for rule (8). Controlled study finding that placing data values/labels directly on chart elements (vs forcing readers to map back to an axis/legend) increased both accuracy and speed of information extraction — cognitive-fit benefit outweighs the added 'load.' Strongest controlled-experiment anchor for the direct-labeling-beats-legend claim that is usually asserted on Tufte's authority alone."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://graphics.cs.wisc.edu/Papers/2018/Gle18/viscomp.pdf",
      "title": "Gleicher (2018), \"Considerations for Visualizing Comparison\" — IEEE TVCG / InfoVis (builds on Gleicher et al. 2011 comparison taxonomy)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Frames the small-multiples question rigorously: the three comparison designs — juxtaposition (side-by-side / small multiples), superposition (overlay), explicit encoding (show the difference directly) — and their tradeoffs. Juxtaposition/small multiples shift the relational work onto the viewer's memory and scale well in number but poorly per-comparison; superposition needs items similar enough to share space. Gives the design-space scaffolding for stating WHEN grids of small charts beat one dense chart, rather than a blanket rule."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://arxiv.org/pdf/1907.03919",

      "snippet": "On-screen / small-display empirical evidence for rule (3), directly relevant to the skill's web/dashboard scope. Small-multiples variant beat the animated variant on speed for 7 of 9 trend-comparison tasks; accuracy differences were mixed (no difference on 5 of 9 tasks, multiples better on some, worse on others). Useful as a concrete, recent controlled study showing small multiples' advantage is real but task- and metric-dependent, not universal."
```

#### `a94e979e` (Run A — canonical (107 agents))

```json
{
  "results": [
    {
      "url": "https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf",
      "title": "Bateman et al. (2010), \"Useful Junk? The Effects of Visual Embellishment on Comprehension and Memorability of Charts\" (CHI 2010)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The canonical controlled experiment that tested Tufte's chartjunk claim head-on. Bateman, Mandryk, Gutwin, Genest, McDine & Brooks compared Nigel Holmes' heavily embellished charts against plain Tufte-style versions. Finding: no difference in interpretation accuracy, and significantly BETTER recall of both subject and detail for embellished charts after a 2-3 week gap. This is the primary 'embellishment is not harmful, may help memory' evidence for rule (1). Caveat the parent should note: stimuli were extreme cartoon charts, n=20, and recall != comprehension. MIT course-hosted clean PDF of the primary paper."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/",
      "title": "Borgo et al. (2012), \"An Empirical Study on Using Visual Embellishments in Visualization\" (IEEE TVCG 18(12):2759-2768)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The key follow-up that adds nuance rather than blanket endorsement. Used a dual-task methodology (viewer attention split, simulating meetings/lectures) to test whether embellishments aid memorization, visual search, and concept comprehension. Finding: embellishment improved retention accuracy AND recall speed, but at a measurable cost — slower visual search, which is exactly the operation analytic tasks (hypothesis generation, observation) depend on. Directly relevant to rule (1)'s 'proven vs context-dependent' framing: helps memory, hurts search."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316",
      "title": "Li & Moacdieh (2014), \"Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment\" (Proc. Human Factors & Ergonomics Society)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The replication/extension that partially CONTRADICTS Bateman and is essential for an evidence-graded skill. Re-tested embellishment under more realistic conditions (time pressure, higher data density, workload). Finding: under cognitive load and for more complex charts, embellishment degraded performance — the Bateman benefit does not generalize beyond simple, single-message, low-pressure charts. This is the source that lets you label the 'chartjunk aids memory' finding as bounded, not universal."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.scribd.com/document/328521741/Useful-Chartjunk-The-Chartjunk-Debate",
      "title": "Stephen Few (2011), \"The Chartjunk Debate: A Close Examination of Recent Findings\" (Visual Business Intelligence Newsletter, Perceptual Edge)",
      "relevance": "medium",
      "snippet": "Few's primary methodological critique of Bateman et al. — the named practitioner rebuttal in the question. Argues the study used absurdly extreme stimuli, conflated memorability with effective communication, tiny sample, and that 'memorable' is not the goal of analytic display. Useful for the skill to represent the convention/taste side of the debate and to flag that the pro-embellishment headline is contested on methodology. (Scribd-hosted copy of Few's newsletter; primary text, not the cleanest host.)"
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.researchgate.net/publication/220956267_Minimalism_in_information_visualization_Attitudes_towards_maximizing_the_data-ink_ratio",
      "title": "Inbar, Tractinsky & Meyer (2007), \"Minimalism in information visualization: Attitudes towards maximizing the data-ink ratio\" (ECCE 2007)",
      "relevance": "medium",
      "snippet": "Empirical attitude study that undercuts the aesthetic premise of data-ink maximization. Tested user preferences across charts of varying data-ink ratio and found viewers rated LOWER-data-ink (less stripped) versions as more beautiful, clear, and effective — the opposite of Tufte's prescription. Relevant to rule (1) and (5): shows the minimalism rule is partly taste/convention, and that audience perception does not track the data-ink ideal."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://arxiv.org/pdf/2009.02634",
      "title": "Akbaba, Wilburn, Nance & Meyer (2020), \"Data Visualization Practitioners' Perspectives on Chartjunk\" (arXiv preprint)",
      "relevance": "medium",
      "snippet": "Modern qualitative survey of how working practitioners actually treat chartjunk, a decade after the Bateman/Few debate. Documents that the field has moved from Tufte's absolutist minimalism toward context-dependent use of embellishment (audience, medium, goal). Useful for the skill's framing that the data-ink rule is a default convention rather than a proven law, and for the web/dashboard-vs-print distinction the parent question wants."
    }
  ]
```

```json
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "THE central controlled study contesting Tufte's chartjunk/data-ink claim (item 1). N=20 lab study comparing Nigel Holmes embellished charts vs. plain Tufte-style charts on small datasets. Findings: interpretation accuracy was NOT worse for embellished charts during/immediately after viewing; long-term recall (2-3 weeks later) was significantly BETTER for embellished. Directly attacks the premise that non-data ink harms comprehension. Note the methodological caveats Few raises (plain charts were stripped grayscale, not best-practice minimal)."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.perceptualedge.com/articles/visual_business_intelligence/the_chartjunk_debate.pdf",
      "title": "Stephen Few (2011), \"The Chartjunk Debate: A Close Examination of Recent Findings,\" Visual Business Intelligence Newsletter (Perceptual Edge)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The key critique source for item 1's 'Few's critique' prong. Few dismantles the Bateman 'Useful Junk?' methodology: argues the 'plain' charts were deliberately impoverished (no titles/value labels) so the comparison was rigged, that memorability of the embellishment is not memorability of the data, and that recall of which-chart-was-seen confounds memory-of-decoration with memory-of-information. Best primary articulation of the convention-vs-evidence boundary — separates Tufte's defensible core (remove genuinely distracting ink) from over-claimed minimalism."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/",

Found embellishments improved memorization and helped recall of depicted information, BUT slowed visual search and gave a complex/mixed picture for grasping key concepts. More rigorous than Bateman; the nuance (memory benefit + search cost) is exactly what an evidence-graded skill needs to distinguish 'proven' from 'it depends.'"

      "relevance": "high",

      "relevance": "high",
```

#### `afd60ce9` (Run B — sibling (105 agents))
```json
{
  "summary": "The surviving evidence sharply qualifies Tufte's most famous prescription — data-ink maximization / chartjunk removal — and supports his graphical-integrity warnings, but leaves several of his principles empirically untested. Strict \"remove all non-data ink\" is NOT proven: across four controlled studies (Bateman 2010, Borgo 2012, Borkin 2013, Skau 2015) embellishment did not reduce comprehension accuracy and reliably improved long-term memory, and one author team states outright that Tufte's anti-embellishment stance \"rests on a general lack of empirical evidence.\" The catch is that the memory benefit is image-recognition, not understanding, and embellishment has measurable costs (slower visual search; higher reading error on precise relative-comparison tasks), so the honest verdict is \"minimalism is taste, not law — but heavy decoration trades efficiency for recall.\" Tufte's integrity rules fare better: truncated axes reliably exaggerate perceived effect size (Pandey 2015, Correll 2020) and area/radius encoding distorts magnitude (Pandey 2015, Skau 2015), confirming his area/volume warning — yet the blanket \"bars must always start at zero\" rule is empirically overstated and task-dependent (Correll 2020, Long & Kay 2024), and the popular \"line charts are exempt from the zero baseline\" convention is contradicted. Crucially, FIVE of the nine requested topics — small multiples, layering & smallest-effective-difference, micro/macro readings, text-graphic integration / direct labeling, and tables-vs-graphics for small data — produced NO surviving controlled-study evidence in this pass and must be labeled convention, not proven.</summary>\n<parameter name=\"findings\">[\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"Strict data-ink-ratio / chartjunk-removal minimalism is a CONVENTION, not a proven rule: in controlled studies, removing non-data ink does not improve comprehension and embellished charts are remembered better. Bateman et al. (2010) found NO difference in interpretation accuracy between embellished 'Holmes-style' and minimalist charts despite far more non-data-ink, and significantly better recall of topic and detail for embellished charts after a 2-3 week delay; the authors say their results 'question some of the premises of the minimalist approach.' Borgo et al. (2012) independently found embellishments 'help participants better remember the information.' Borkin et al. (2013) found LOW data-ink-ratio ('more chartjunk') scored significantly higher memorability than minimalist designs (M=1.81 vs 1.23, p<0.001), refuting their own hypothesis that minimalism is more memorable. Skau, Harrison & Kosara (2015) state Tufte's negative view of embellishment 'rests on a general lack of empirical evidence' yet drives the field's 'culture of design simplification and sterilization,' and note the data-ink ratio taken to its logical end would absurdly reduce a bar to a single-pixel line. LABEL: data-ink maximization = convention/myth-when-stated-as-law; the comprehension-penalty claim for embellishment is CONTRADICTED.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"vote\": \"merged from claims voted 3-0 (Bateman x3, Borgo, Borkin x2, Skau)\",\n    \"sources\": [\n      \"https://vis.csail.mit.edu/classes/6.859/readings/pdfs/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf\",\n      \"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/\",\n      \"http://olivalab.mit.edu/Papers/Borkin_etal_MemorableVisualization_TVCG2013.pdf\",\n      \"https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015\"\n    ],\n    \"evidence\": \"Four independent peer-reviewed primary sources, all unanimous (3-0). Bateman et al., 'Useful Junk?', CHI 2010 (ACM 1753326.1753716), n=20: verbatim 'we did not find any difference in people's interpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts' and 'participants were able to remember significantly more about the embellished charts after a gap longer than 12 days.' Borgo et al., IEEE TVCG 18(12):2759-2768, 2012 (PMID 26357185): 'visual embellishments can help participants better remember the information depicted.' Borkin et al., 'What Makes a Visualization Memorable?', IEEE TVCG 19(12):2306-2315, 2013: 'bad (M=1.81), i.e., low data-to-ink ratio, being higher than a good rating (M=1.23; t(208)=6.92; p<0.001)... refutes our fourth and fifth hypotheses' (H5 = minimalist is more memorable). Skau et al., Computer Graphics Forum 34(3) (EuroVis 2015): 'Despite a general lack of empirical evidence supporting this negative view on chart embellishments, it has driven a culture of design simplification and sterilization... By this measure, bars in a bar chart would be better if they were reduced to a single-pixel wide line.' Note: Stephen Few's 'The Chartjunk Debate' critiques generalizability (small N, extreme Holmes examples) but does not dispute the in-study findings.\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"The memorability benefit of embellishment is NOT a free win and does NOT mean chartjunk aids understanding. Borkin et al. (2013) explicitly state they tested memorability of visualizations 'as images,' that excessive clutter does NOT aid comprehension and 'may instead interfere with it.' Borgo et al. (2012) found embellishments 'have a negative impact on the speed of visual search.' Skau et al. (2015) found that on relative-comparison tasks (estimating one bar as a percentage of another), EVERY tested embellishment except bars-extended-below-zero significantly increased reading error versus a plain baseline — including a change as minor as rounding the tops of bars. NET: the recognition/recall benefit trades against efficiency and precise reading; embellishment is defensible for memorable one-message graphics, harmful for analytical comparison displays.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"vote\": \"merged from claims voted 3-0 (Borgo cost, Borkin caveat, Skau relative-error)\",\n    \"sources\": [\n      \"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26357185/\",\n      \"http://olivalab.mit.edu/Papers/Borkin_etal_MemorableVisualization_TVCG2013.pdf\",\n      \"https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015\"\n    ],\n    \"evidence\": \"Borgo et al. 2012 abstract (PMID 26357185): 'visual embellishments can have a negative impact on the speed of visual search' — measured via dual-task methodology. Borkin et al. 2013: 'we tested the memorability of visualizations as images and not the comprehension... Nor does excessive visual clutter aid comprehension of the actual information in the visualization (and may instead interfere with it)'; memorability was a repeat-detection RECOGNITION task (d'), modeled on Isola et al. 2011, NOT comprehension. Skau, Harrison & Kosara 2015 (EuroVis): 'All adaptations except the extended embellishment performed significantly worse than the baseline on relative judgements. This is the load-bearing nuance that keeps Finding 1 from being read as 'decoration is always good.'\"\n  },\n  {\n    \"claim\": \"Direct empirical support for data-ink maximization on ON-SCREEN / dynamic displays is WEAK and CONFOUNDED. But the high-data-ink display was a purely numerical/digital readout while the medium and low displays were graphical gauges, so the speed advantage confounds display FORMAT (number vs chart) with data-ink ratio; the dose-response also failed (low and medium were similar) and situation-awareness showed no data-ink effect. Pandey et al. (2015, n=250): truncating a bar axis raised mean Likert perceived-difference from 1.45 to 2.77 (U=1144, Z=3.36, p=0.0003) — supports the zero-baseline convention. Correll et al. (2020, n=40): more truncation = more perceived severity (all three of 0%/25%/50% levels significantly different, F(2,76)=89, p<0.0001), the inflation was EQUAL for bars and lines (no chart-type difference, F(1,38)=0.5, p=0.50, refuting the line-exemption rule), and explicit broken-axis / gradient cues did NOT de-bias viewers; the authors REJECT the universal 'include 0' rule and the binary honest/dishonest framing, arguing the designer must choose the y-range to match the meaningful effect. Skau et al. LABEL: STRONG — area/volume encoding distortion is one of the better-supported Tufte integrity rules. Caveat: the perception literature (Stevens' power law, Flannery 1971) shows even correctly area-scaled circles are systematically underestimated, so 'area maps one-to-one to data' is the normative target, not a guarantee of accurate reading.\",\n    \"confidence\": \"high\",\n    \"vote\": \"merged from claims voted 3-0 (Pandey radius, Skau quadratic)\",\n    \"sources\": [\n      \"https://www.researchgate.net/publication/300726103_How_Deceptive_are_Deceptive_Visualizations\",\n      \"https://eagereyes.org/publications/Skau-EuroVis-2015\"\n    ],\n    \"evidence\": \"Pandey et al. 2015 (CHI): bubble (radius-as-quantity) 'mean ranks of Bubble(Control) and Bubble(Deceptive) were 32.47 and 48.52; U=1121, Z=3.08, p=0.0007, r=0.34'; Table 4 means 1.71 vs 2.71. For the skill, these five MUST be labeled 'convention — not empirically tested in this evidence set,' not 'proven' and not 'myth.' Do not let the absence of evidence be read as evidence of absence.\n\nSOURCE QUALITY: nearly all findings rest on peer-reviewed primary papers at top HCI/viz venues (CHI, IEEE TVCG, EuroVis/CGF) with unanimous 3-0 verification — high trust.
```
